كتاب الله تعالى ١٤ أقسام النظم والمعنى إلا إذا لحقه خصوص معلوم أو مجهول، كآية الربا في البيع فحينئذ يوجب الحكم أي العام على تجوز ان يظهر الخصوص فيه بتعليله أو بتفسيره. أي العام أي حين لحوق الخصوص : من الكتاب بخبر الواحد والقياس خصوص: أي مخصص، وهو * إما كلام مستقل أو غير الكلام، كالعقل والعادة والحس، وزيادة بعض الأفراد ونقصان بعضها، فذلك العام المخصوص منه البعض لا يبقى قطعياً عند الجمهور، سواء كان المخصوص معلوم المراد كالمستأمن، حيث خص من قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ (التوبة: ٥)، فإنه عام فخُص منه المستأمن بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ﴾ (التوبة: ٦)، وهذا المخصص كلام، والمخصوص منه وهو المستأمن معلوم، أو مجهول المراد كالربا، حيث خص من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ الله (البقرة : ٢٧٥)، فإنه عام لدخول لام الجنس فيه أو الاستغراق، يشمل البيع المشتمل على الربا والخالي عنه، فخص منه الربا وهو مجهول؛ لأنه في اللغة: الفضل المطلق، والبيع إنما شرع للفضل، فلو يكون الفضل المطلق حراماً ينسد باب البيع، فعلم أن المراد به غيره فصار مجهولا ، فبينه النبي بقوله : "الحنطة بالحنطة" الحديث. كآية الربا إلخ أي كالتخصيص الثابت في البيع بآية الربا ، وهي وَحَرَّمَ الرّبا (البقرة: ٢٧٥)، فإن المخصوص وهو الربا في هذه الآية مجهول المراد قبل بيانه عليه السلام لا أن التخصيص في آية الربا مجهول، والمصنف لم يتعرّض لمثال المعلوم إشارةً إلى أن هذا المثال يصح للمعلوم أيضاً بعد بيانه عليه، كما هو مثال للمجهول قبل بیانه صلى الله عليه وسلم يوجب الحكم: أي يثبت العام المخصوص منه الحكم في الباقي على تقدير كون الخصوص معلوم المراد وفي الكل على تقدير جهالة الخصوص بتعليله: أي الخصوص المعلوم، هذا على تقدير كون الخصوص معلوما. بتفسيره أي الخصوص المجهول من قبل الشارع على تقدير كونه مجهولا والحاصل أن العام قبل التخصيص قطعي فيما يتناوله، وبعد التخصيص سواء كان المخصوص معلوما أو مجهولا يصير ظنياً؛ وذلك لأن المخصوص إذا كان معلوما كالمستأمن فالظاهر أن يكون المخصص معللا؛ لأنه كلام مستقل، والأصل في النصوص التعليل، فإذا صار معللا فيسري احتمال التخصيص في الباقي أيضاً لوجود علة الخصوص. ألا ترى أنه خص من قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ) (التوبة:٥)، المستأمن بقوله الآخر، وعلم أن علته العجز عن الحرب، وخص من الباقي النسوان والصبيان والعميان والشيوخ والراهبون أيضا بتلك العلة، فإذا سرى احتمال التخصيص إلى الباقي بعد تعليل الخصوص المعلوم لم يبق قطعياً في الباقي، وهذا معنى قوله: "على تجوز" أي على احتمال أن يظهر الخصوص فيه بتعليله، وإذا كان مجهولا كالربا فيلحقه التفسير من الشارع، وبعد لحوق التفسير يصير معلوما ومحتملا للتعليل كالخصوص المعلوم، ألا ترى أنه لما فسر – عليه السلام – الربا بالأشياء الست وصار معلوما، : وهو: وذلك لأن الخصوص عندنا لا يكون بكلام غير مستقل كالشرط والغاية والاستثناء والصفة خلافاً للشافعي.
Book of Allah Almighty. Types of phrasing and meaning. Unless a known or unknown particularity attaches to it, like the verse of riba in sale, then it necessitates the ruling, i.e., the general, with the possibility that particularity appears in it by its cause or its interpretation. That is, at the time of attachment of particularity. From the Book by a solitary report and analogy: particularity is a specifier, and it is either independent speech or non-speech, like reason, custom, sensory perception, and addition of some individuals and omission of some. So that general from which some is specified does not remain decisive according to the majority, whether the specified is known in meaning like the musta'min, where it was specified from His saying: 'Kill the polytheists' [9:5], for it is general, and the musta'min was specified by His saying: 'And if anyone of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection' [9:6], and this specifier is speech, and the specified, the musta'min, is known; or unknown in meaning like riba, where it was specified from His saying: 'And Allah has permitted sale' [2:275], for it is general due to the inclusion of the definite article for genus or for totality, including sale that involves riba and that which is free from it, so riba was specified from it, and it is unknown because in language it is absolute excess, and sale is legislated for excess, so if absolute excess were forbidden, the door of sale would be closed. Thus it is known that the intended meaning is other than it, so it became unknown. Then the Prophet explained it by his saying: 'Wheat for wheat...' etc. Like the verse of riba etc., i.e., like the specification established in sale by the verse of riba which is 'And Allah has forbidden riba' [2:275], for the specified, i.e., riba in this verse, is unknown in meaning before his explanation, peace be upon him, not that the specification in the verse of riba is unknown. And the author did not mention an example of the known, indicating that this example is valid for the known after his explanation as well, just as it is an example of the unknown before his explanation. It necessitates the ruling: i.e., the general from which some is specified establishes the ruling for the remainder on the assumption that the specific is known in meaning, and for all on the assumption of ignorance of the specific. By its cause: i.e., the known specific, this on the assumption that the specific is known. By its interpretation: i.e., the specific unknown by the Lawgiver on the assumption that it is unknown. The gist is that the general before specification is decisive regarding what it encompasses, and after specification, whether the specified is known or unknown, it becomes speculative. That is because if the specified is known, like the musta'min, then it is apparent that the specifier is cause-based; because it is independent speech, and the principle in texts is cause-based reasoning. So when it becomes cause-based, the possibility of specification spreads to the remainder as well due to the existence of the cause of specification. Do you not see that from His statement: 'Kill the polytheists' [9:5], the musta'min was specified by the other statement, and it is known that its cause is inability to fight, and from the remainder, women, children, the blind, the elderly, and monks were also specified by that cause? So when the possibility of specification spreads to the remainder after the cause-based reasoning for the known specific, it does not remain decisive for the remainder. This is the meaning of his saying: 'with the possibility', i.e., with the possibility that specification appears in it by its cause. And if it is unknown, like riba, then interpretation from the Lawgiver attaches to it, and after interpretation it becomes known and capable of cause-based reasoning like the known specific. Do you not see that when he, peace be upon him, interpreted riba as the six items, it became known, and it was known that the cause is volume and genus, so items like plaster and rice were appended to the six items, so the possibility spread to the remainder by the cause, so it did not remain decisive. This is the meaning of his saying: 'or by its interpretation'. But it does not render the proof void. That is because the specific resembles the abrogator in its phrasing in that it is independent speech, just as the abrogator is independent speech, and it resembles the exception in its ruling in that it clarifies that the specified was not included under the ruling, just as the exception clarifies that the excepted is outside the statement. The exception resembles it in being non-independent. So the specific, whether known or unknown, acquires the quality of independence and non-independence. When this is established, we say: If the specific is unknown, i.e., encompassing what is unknown to the listener, then from the aspect of its independence it is like an unknown abrogator, so it falls by itself, and its ignorance does not extend to the general, just as an unknown abrogator does not void the abrogated. From the aspect of its non-independence, it is like an unknown exception, so it necessitates ignorance in the general like an unknown exception. So doubt arises regarding the validity of the general, when it was established with certainty, and certainty is not removed by doubt, so the general is not removed, but it acquires a doubt, becoming speculative, necessitating action without knowledge. And if the specific is known, then from its independence its cause-based reasoning is valid, so it necessitates ignorance in what remains under the general, as it is not known how much is excluded by analogy. And from its non-independence and resemblance to exception, its cause-based reasoning is not valid, just as exception cannot be cause-based, because it is not independent text but rather like a description attached to the statement, so what is beyond the specific is known, and the general remains as it was. So doubt arises regarding the proof-value of the general, when its proof-value was established with certainty, so it is not removed by doubt. This is the dissection of the speech by which the intended meaning is revealed. As for the true analysis, it is: The general when specified is either by something non-independent or independent. In the first, if the excluded is known, it is a proof by agreement; if unknown, like when he says: 'His slaves are free except some', it is not a proof until the intended meaning is clarified. In the second, i.e., specification according to the Hanafis, if the specifier is reason, it is a decisive proof for the remainder; if it is other than speech, like custom, addition of some individuals over others, and sensory perception, then apparently it does not remain decisive due to variation in customs, lack of sensory awareness of details, and concealment of addition and omission. Indeed, if the specified amount is known with certainty, the remainder remains decisive. If it is speech, then according to al-Karkhi the remainder is not a proof at all; according to some, if the specified is known, the general is decisive for the remainder; if unknown, the specifier falls by itself and the general remains decisive. The chosen madhhab is what is in the text: that specification, whether the specified is known or unknown, remains speculative and does not fall, so that it is permissible to specify it by a solitary report and analogy. That is because according to us the specific cannot be by non-independent speech like condition, final clause, exception, and adjective, contrary to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of specification (takhsees) on the general term (amm). It begins by stating that if a general term is followed by a specific meaning, whether that meaning is known (like the exemption of musta'min from the general command to kill polytheists) or unknown (like riba which was specified from the general permissibility of sale but was unknown before the Prophet's explanation), then the general still yields a ruling, but it becomes speculative (zanni) rather than decisive (qati). The reasoning is that once specification occurs, there is a possibility of further specification due to the underlying cause (illa). For example, from the verse 'Kill the polytheists', the musta'min was excluded due to inability to fight, and then other categories like women and children were also excluded by the same cause. This introduces doubt in the remaining generality. Similarly, after riba was specified and explained by the Prophet as six items, the cause (volume and genus) extended the prohibition to other items like plaster and rice. The passage then explores different scholarly positions, including al-Karkhi's view that the general becomes invalid, and the chosen Hanafi position that the general remains speculative but valid as a proof. It also notes a Hanafi peculiarity: specification cannot be by non-independent clauses like condition or exception, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The text concludes with a detailed analysis of how independence and non-independence of the specifier affect the ruling.

Key Terms
عام (amm): A word that includes all its individuals without restriction.
خصوص (khusoos): The specification of a general term to exclude some of its individuals.
قطعي (qati): A ruling or evidence that is definitive and certain.
ظني (zanni): A ruling or evidence that is speculative, requiring action but not knowledge.
تعليل (ta'lil): Assigning a cause (illa) to a ruling, allowing extension by analogy.
مخصِّص (mukhasis): The specifying evidence or text that excludes some individuals from the general.
مستأمن (musta'min): A polytheist who seeks protection from Muslims; exempted from the general command to kill polytheists.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; specified from the general permissibility of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented, what is the status of a general term after it has been specified by a known specific (like the musta'min example)?
A) It becomes invalid and cannot be used as evidence.
B) It remains decisive (qati) for the remainder.
C) It becomes speculative (zanni) but remains a valid proof.
D) It becomes speculative only if the specific is unknown, otherwise decisive.

Key Takeaway
After specification, a general becomes speculative (zanni) but remains a valid proof (hujja), and Hanafis require specification to be by independent speech.

Definition of Mushtarak (Homonym)
Defining mushtarak and distinguishing it from 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of mushtarak, identify an example, and distinguish it from 'amm in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
One word with multiple distinct meanings, no hierarchy — unlike 'amm which covers many instances of one meaning.

In usul al-fiqh, precise terminology is essential for correct interpretation. The term 'mushtarak' (homonym) refers to a word that carries multiple unrelated meanings by linguistic convention. Understanding this concept prevents confusion with 'amm (general), which has a single meaning covering many individuals. This lesson anchors the definition and clarifies the boundary between the two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Locate the term being defined: 'al-mushtarak'. The passage begins with the word in Arabic.
The term is stated first, then defined.
2
Parse the definition
The definition is: 'that in which meanings or names share, not in the manner of ordering.' Break down: 'meanings or names share' means the word has been assigned to more than one meaning. 'Not in the manner of ordering' means these meanings are not arranged hierarchically (like general then specific).
The phrase 'ay lafd' clarifies that it refers to a single word.
3
Understand the negation
The negation 'not in the manner of ordering' is key. It excludes cases where meanings are organized, such as in 'amm where the single meaning applies to all individuals of a category. In mushtarak, the meanings are separate and unordered.
Without this negation, the definition could mistakenly include ordered polysemy.
4
Contrast with 'amm
Compare mushtarak to 'amm (general). 'amm has one meaning that covers many individuals (e.g., 'muslimun' includes all Muslims). Mushtarak has multiple distinct meanings (e.g., 'ayn means eye, spring, spy). This distinction is vital for interpreting sacred texts.
Remember: 'amm = one meaning, many individuals; mushtarak = many meanings, one word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشترك وهو ما اشترك فيه معان أو أسام، لا على سبيل الانتظام. أي لفظ
Al-mushtarak: it is that in which meanings or names share, not in the manner of ordering. i.e., a word.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage defines mushtarak, a term in usul al-fiqh. Mushtarak is a word that has been assigned to multiple meanings (or names) by linguistic convention, without any priority or order among those meanings. For example, the Arabic word 'ayn can mean eye, a spring, or a spy. This distinguishes mushtarak from 'amm (general): 'amm has a single meaning that applies to multiple individuals, while mushtarak has multiple distinct meanings. The phrase 'not in the manner of ordering' means the meanings are not arranged hierarchically like a general and specific. The commentary 'ay lafd' simply clarifies that it refers to a word (lafdh).

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that shares multiple meanings or names, not in an ordered arrangement.
انتظام (intidham): Ordering or arrangement, here meaning that the meanings are not organized in a hierarchy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A word with multiple meanings that are ordered from general to specific.
C) A word shared between multiple meanings without any order among them.
D) A word that is ambiguous due to lack of context.

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak is a word with multiple unrelated meanings; recognizing it is crucial for proper interpretation of sacred texts.

Definition of Mushtarak and Effect of Takhsis on 'Amm
Defines mushtarak and discusses effect of takhsis on 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of mushtarak and explain why, according to Hanafi usul, takhsis renders a general statement speculative (zanni) rather than dropping it.

Core Idea
Specification introduces doubt into the general, but certainty is not removed by doubt – the general becomes speculative but remains authoritative.

This passage opens by defining mushtarak, a word with multiple meanings, then dives into a profound debate about what happens when a general text is specified. The Hanafi school argues that specification does not destroy the general's authority; it merely reduces it from decisive to speculative. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly weighing textual evidence in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define mushtarak
Locate the definition in the text: 'al-mushtarak huwa ma ishtaraka fihi ma'ani aw asami, la 'ala sabil al-intizam.' Understand that mushtarak shares multiple meanings alternatively, not comprehensively.
This is a terminological definition contrasting with 'amm.
2
Distinguish mushtarak from 'amm
Mushtarak is ambiguous (mujmal) and requires interpretation, while 'amm covers all individuals comprehensively and is clear. Takhsis applies to 'amm, not mushtarak.
The passage later discusses takhsis on 'amm.
3
Understand the dual nature of takhsis
Takhsis resembles nasikh (independent speech) and istithna' (non-independent). This duality creates both certainty and doubt.
This is key to why the general becomes zanni.
4
Analyze known vs unknown specific
If the specific (makhsus) is unknown, it acts like an unknown exception, casting doubt on the general. If known, analogy on the specific introduces unknown amounts of exclusion, still causing doubt.
In both cases, doubt remains.
5
Compare views and conclude
Al-Karkhi held the general drops entirely; others said it stays decisive if specific is known. The chosen Hanafi view is that the general becomes zanni but does not drop, allowing further specification by solitary reports and analogy.
This is the operative position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى أقسام النظم والمعنى [المشترك] والمشترك وهو ما اشترك فيه معان أو أسام، لا على سبيل الانتظام. أي لفظ = وعلم أن العلة القدر والجنس ألحق بالأشياء الستة، مثل الجص ،والأرز، فسرى الاحتمال إلى الباقي بالعلة فلم يبق قطعيا، وهذا معنى قوله: "أو بتفسيره" ولكنه لا يسقط الاحتجاج به، وذلك لأن الخصوص يشبه الناسخ بصيغته من حيث إنه كلام مستقل كما أن الناسخ يكون كلاما مستقلا، ويشبه الاستثناء بحكمه من حيث حيث أنه يبين أن المخصوص لم يدخل تحت الحكم، كما أن الاستثناء يبين أن المستثنى خارج عن صدر الكلام، والاستثناء المشبه به عدم أمر غير مستقل فحصل للخصوص- سواء كان معلوما أو مجهولا - وصف الاستقلال ووصف عدم الاستقلال. وإذا تقرر هذا فنقول: إن الخصوص إن كان مجهولا أي متناولا لما هو مجهول عن السامع فهو من جهة استقلاله بمنزلة الناسخ المجهول فيسقط بنفسه، ولا يتعدى جهالته إلى العام كما أن الناسخ المجهول لا يسقط المنسوخ من جهة استقلاله، فهو بمنزلة الاستثناء المجهول فيوجب الجهالة في العام كاستثناء المجهول، فوقع الشك في سقوط العام وقد كان ثابتاً بيقين، واليقين لا يزول بالشك، فلا يزول العام، ولكن يحصل فيه شبهة فيصير ظنياً يوجب العمل دون العلم، وإذا كان الخصوص معلوماً فمن استقلاله يصح تعليله، فيوجب جهالة فيما بقي تحت العام؛ إذ لايدرى كم خرج من القياس، ومن جهة عدم استقلاله وشبهه بالاستثناء لا يصح تعليله كما أن الاستثناء لايصح تعليله؛ لأنه ليس نصا مستقلا بل هو بمنزلة وصف قائم يصدر الكلام، فيكون ما وراء المخصوص معلوما فبقي العام بحاله، فوقع الشك في عدم حجية العام، وقد كان حجيته ثابتاً بيقين، فلا يزول بالشك. هذا هو تشريح الكلام بحيث ينكشف به المرام، وأما تحقيق المقام فهو إن قصر العام لا يخلو إما أن يكون بغير مستقل أو بمستقل، على الأول إن كان المخرج معلوما فهو حجة بالاتفاق، وإن كان مجهولا كما إذا قال: عبيده أحرار إلا بعضا، لا يكون حجة ما لم يتبين المراد، وعلى الثاني وهو التخصيص عند الحنفية إن كان المخصص عقلا فهو حجة قطعية في الباقي، وإن كان غيره سوى الكلام كالعادة وزيادة بعض الأفراد على البعض والحس فالظاهر أنه لا يبقى قطعيا؛ لاختلاف العادات وعدم اطلاع الحس على تفاصيل الأشياء وخفاء الزيادة والنقصان. نعم إذا يعلم القدر المخصوص قطعا فيبقى الباقي قطعيا، وإن كان الكلام فعند الكرخي لا يبقى الباقي حجة أصلا، وعند البعض إن كان المخصوص معلوما فالعام قطعي في الباقي، وإن كان مجهولا يسقط المخصص بنفسه ويبقى العام قطعيا والمذهب المختار ما هو في المتن من أنه بعض التخصيص سواء كان المخصوص معلوما أو مجهولا يبقى ظنّياً ولا يسقط، حتى يجوز تخصيصه بخبر الواحد والقياس.
Book of Allah Almighty. Divisions of wording and meaning. [The Common Word] The common word is that which shares meanings or names, not in a comprehensive manner. That is, it is known that the cause (capacity and genus) is annexed to the six things, like gypsum and rice, so probability spreads to the rest by the cause, so it remains not definitive. This is the meaning of his statement: 'or by its interpretation,' but it does not remove the validity of argumentation with it. That is because the specific resembles the abrogator in its form insofar as it is independent speech, just as the abrogator is independent speech, and it resembles the exception in its ruling insofar as it clarifies that the specified did not enter under the ruling, just as the exception clarifies that the excepted is outside the opening of the speech. The exception resembles a non-independent matter, so the specific - whether known or unknown - acquires the attribute of independence and the attribute of non-independence. When this is established, we say: If the specific is unknown, i.e., addressing what is unknown to the listener, then from the aspect of its independence it is like an unknown abrogator, so it drops by itself, and its ignorance does not extend to the general; just as the unknown abrogator does not drop the abrogated from the aspect of its independence. So it is like an unknown exception, causing ignorance in the general like an unknown exception. Thus doubt occurs in the dropping of the general, while it was established with certainty, and certainty does not depart by doubt. So the general does not drop, but a doubt arises in it, making it speculative, necessitating action without knowledge. If the specific is known, then from its independence its analogical extension is valid, causing ignorance in what remains under the general, since it is not known how much was excluded by analogy. And from the aspect of its non-independence and resemblance to the exception, its analogical extension is not valid, just as analogical extension is not valid for the exception, because it is not an independent text but like an attribute subsisting in the speech. Thus what is beyond the specific is known, and the general remains as it was. So doubt occurs in the non-authoritativeness of the general, while its authoritativeness was established with certainty, so it does not depart by doubt. This is the dissection of speech such that the desired is disclosed. As for the verification of the matter: if the general is qualified, it is either by something non-independent or by something independent. In the first case, if the excluded is known, it is authoritative by consensus; if it is unknown, like if he says 'my slaves are free except some,' it is not authoritative until the intended is clarified. In the second case, which is specification according to the Hanafis: if the specifier is reason, it is a decisive proof for the remainder; if it is other than speech, like custom, addition of some individuals over others, and sense, then apparently it does not remain decisive, due to differences in customs, lack of sense perception of details of things, and hiddenness of increase and decrease. Yes, if the specified amount is known with certainty, the remainder remains decisive. If it is speech, then according to al-Karkhi the remainder is not a proof at all; according to some if the specified is known, the general is decisive in the remainder, and if it is unknown, the specifier drops by itself and the general remains decisive. The chosen madhhab is what is in the text: that specification, whether the specified is known or unknown, leaves (the general) speculative and does not drop, so that it is permissible to specify it by a solitary report and analogy.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a definition and extended commentary on the concept of the common word (mushtarak) and its implications for general statements. The text first defines mushtarak as a word that shares multiple meanings or referents, but not in a comprehensive (shumul) manner, meaning it applies to one of several meanings alternatively, not all simultaneously. The commentator then dives into a sophisticated discussion about the effect of specification (takhsis) on a general ('amm) statement, using the example of annexing new items to the six ribawi goods by analogy. The core argument is about whether a general statement remains decisive (qat'i) after being specified. The Hanafi position, as preferred in the text, is that specification renders the general speculative (zanni), whether the specific (makhsus) is known or unknown. This is because specification resembles both an abrogator (nasikh) and an exception (istithna'), leading to a combination of independence and dependence, which introduces doubt but does not negate the general entirely. The text compares the views of al-Karkhi (who holds the general drops entirely) and others, ultimately affirming that the general remains authoritative but speculative, allowing further specification by solitary reports and analogy. This lesson is crucial for understanding the epistemological weight of general Quranic and Hadith texts in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word that shares multiple meanings or referents, not comprehensively but alternatively, i.e., it can mean any of them.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): The specification of a general term, excluding some of its individuals or meanings.
الظني (al-zanni): Speculative; a ruling that is probable but not certain, yet binding for practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what happens to a general statement after it has been specified (makhsus)?
A) It becomes invalid and no longer authoritative.
B) It remains decisive (qat'i) if the specific is known.
C) It becomes speculative (zanni) and remains a valid proof for action.
D) It becomes doubtful and requires further interpretation to be used.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, takhsis renders the general speculative (zanni) but does not drop its authority, allowing further specification by solitary reports and analogy.

Al-Mushtarak and Al-Mu'awwal: Definitions and Rulings
Defines mushtarak and mu'awwal with rulings and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mushtarak and mu'awwal, apply the ruling of tawaqquf to a mushtarak term, and explain how ta'wil yields mu'awwal with its consequence of wujub al-'amal.

Core Idea
A mushtarak term requires suspension; only after preponderance by dominant opinion does it become mu'awwal, obligating action but not certainty.

In Hanafi usul, words are categorized not only by their generality or specificity but also by their potential for multiple meanings. This lesson unpacks two critical categories: al-mushtarak (the multivocal) and al-mu'awwal (the interpreted). You will learn how a word's shared meanings force a ruling of suspension until reflection leads to preponderance, and how that preponderance transforms the word into a mu'awwal that demands action despite remaining conjectural.

Guided Path
1
Define mushtarak and its two types
Identify the definition: a word shared between multiple meanings or names on an alternative (badal) basis. Distinguish the two types: multivocality of meanings (e.g., nahal for thirst/drinking) and multivocality of named entities (e.g., 'ayn for sun, knee, etc.). Note that the plural form in the definition merely indicates 'more than one', not a minimum of three.
The Hanafi distinction from 'amm: mushtarak is alternative, 'amm is comprehensive.
2
Understand the ruling of mushtarak (tawaqquf)
The core ruling is suspension (tawaqquf): one must not assign a specific meaning without reflection. The condition is ta'ammul (reflection) to seek preponderance. Until then, the word is not immediately actionable.
Example: The word 'qur'' is suspended between purity and menses until context preponderates.
3
Distinguish mushtarak from 'amm
Both involve multiple referents, but 'amm covers all individuals simultaneously (e.g., 'every Muslim'), while mushtarak covers one at a time (e.g., 'qur'' means either purity or menses, not both). This is the 'badal vs. shumul' distinction.
The text explicitly says: 'معناه أن لا يكون هذا الاشتراك بطريق الشمول بل على سبيل البدل فخرج به العام'.
4
Explain mu'awwal as preponderant mushtarak
Mu'awwal is the mushtarak after one meaning becomes preponderant by dominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y). This preponderance can come from solitary report, qiyas, or reflection on wording/context. The ruling is attributed to the wording itself, not the interpretation, because the stronger proof (wording) prevails.
The example of 'qur'' shows how contextual analysis leads to preponderance of menses.
5
Apply the ruling of mu'awwal (wujub al-'amal)
Once mu'awwal, the ruling is obligation to act upon that interpretation, with the probability of error. It does not yield certainty, so denying it does not amount to disbelief.
The text: 'حكمه وجوب العمل به' — but it is zanni (conjectural).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى
أقسام النظم والمعنى
وحكمه التوقف فيه بشرط التأمل ليترجح بعض وجوهه.
[المؤول]
المحتملة
والمؤول وهو ما ترجّح من المشترك بعض وجوهه بغالب الرأي.
والقسم الرابع
= معناه أن لا يكون هذا الاشتراك بطريق الشمول بل على سبيل البدل فخرج به العام. وقوله: "معان أو أسام" تقسيم للمحدود لا للحد؛ لأن المشترك على قسمين: أحدهما ما يكون فيه اشتراك المعاني كلفظ النهل للري والعطش، وثانيهما ما يكون فيه اشتراك الأسامي أي المسمّيات يعني الأعيان الخارجية كلفظ العين، فإنه يشترك فيه الأعيان الخارجية كالشمس والركبة والجارية والذهب والينبوع والمراد بالجمع في قوله "معان أو أسام" ما فوق الواحد، وإنما أتى بصيغة الجمع ليناسب قوله في تعريف العام "جمعاً من المسميات"، فاندفع ما قيل: إن الجمع يوهم أن عدد الثلاث شرط في الاشتراك كما هو شرط في العموم، وليس كذلك بل الاشتراك يثبت بين المعنيين أو الاسمين كالقرء.
ثم اعلم أن المراد من قوله "المشترك": المشترك الاصطلاحي، ومن قوله "ما" اشترك الاشتراك اللغوي، فلا دور. وحكمه التوقف إلخ يعني حكم المشترك أن يتوقف فيه عن اعتقاد معنى معين من المعاني، سوى أن المراد به حق لغرض أن يتأمل فيه ليترجح بعض المعاني بالتأمل؛ لأن اللفظ يحتمل كل واحد من المعاني على السوية، والمراد منها واحد فلا بد من التأمل كما تأمل علماؤنا في لفظ "القرء" المشترك بين الطهر والحيض، فظهر لهم أن المراد به الحيض بعدة وجوه أحدها أنه ورد بلفظ الجمع، وأقله ثلاثة، وإذا يراد الطهر لا يحصل الثلاثة، والثاني ورد لفظ "ثلاثة" ، و إذا يراد الطهر يزيد على الثلاثة أو ينقص منها والثالث أن القرء يدل على معنى الجمع والانتقال، وهذا المعنى يوجد في الحيض؛ لأنه دم والدم يجتمع في الرحم في أيام الطهر ثم ينتقل، بخلاف الطهر والمؤوّل: مأخوذ من آل يؤول إذا رجح، وأولته حققته وصرفته، فإنك إذا عينت أحد معانيه فقد صر عن سائر الوجوه المحتملة ما ترجّح إلخ وقيّد بهذا؛ لأن المراد من المؤول هنا هو المؤول الذي حصل من المشترك بعد الترجيح بعض وجوهه، لا المطلق؛ لأن الخفي والمشكل والمجمل إذا زال خفاؤها بدليل ظني يصير مؤوّلا أيضاً، ولكنه من أقسام البيان لا من أقسام الصيغة. بغالب الرأي: أي الظن الغالب سواء حصل بخبر الواحد أو القياس أو بغيره كالتأمل في نفس الصيغة أو في السياق، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿أُحِلَّ لَكُمْ لَيْلَةَ الصَّيَامِ الرَّفَ ) (البقرة: ۱۸۷)، علم أنه من الحل، وفي قوله أَحَلَّنَا دَارَ الْمُقَامَةِ (فاطر:٣٥)، يعرف أنه من الحلول، أو في السباق كما في القرء يعلم أنه الحيض نظراً إلى ما قبله من الثلاثة، =
والحاصل أن المؤوّل قسم من المشترك حصل بترجح أحد معانيه بتأويل المجتهد، وكان قبل أن يترجح أحد معانيه على الآخر مشتركا والتأويل اعتبار احتمال يعضده دليل يصير به أغلب على الظن من سائر ما دلّ عليه اللفظ، وإنما عد المؤول من أقسام النظم صيغة ولغة وإنه حصل بفعل التأويل؛ لأن الحكم بعد التأويل يضاف إلى الصيغة؛ لأن إضافة الحكم الدليل الأقوى أولى، ولذا أضافوا الحكم في المنصوص عليه إلى النص لا إلى العلة؛ لأنه أقوى منها كالمجمل إذا لحقه البيان بخبر الواحد يكون ذلك ثابتا قطعا والحال أن خبر الواحد لايفيد اليقين، فالحكم بعد البيان أضيف إلى المفسر لكونه أقوى إلى خبر الواحد.
وحكمه إلخ : أي حكم المؤول وجوب العمل به، فيجب العمل بما تقرر من تأويل المجتهد، احتمال أنه غلط والصواب في الجانب الآخر، وذلك لأن التأويل إن ثبت بالرأي فالرأي يحتمل الصواب والخطاء، فيكون الثابت به محتملا، وإن كان بخبر الواحد فهو أيضاً ظني، وبالجملة إنه يوجب العمل دون العلم فلا يكفر جاحده
The Book of Allah Almighty. Sections of [Textual] Structure and Meaning. Its ruling is suspension therein, on condition of reflection, so that some of its aspects become preponderant. [Al-Mu'awwal] The interpreted is that which has some of its aspects preponderant from the multivocal [term] by dominant opinion. The fourth section: Its meaning is that this multivocality is not in the manner of comprehensiveness but in the manner of alternativity, thus the general [term] is excluded. And his statement: 'meanings or names' is a division of the defined, not of the definition; because the multivocal is of two types: one is multivocality of meanings like the word 'nahal' for drinking and thirst, and the second is multivocality of names i.e. named entities meaning external objects like the word 'ayn', for external objects such as the sun, knee, slave-girl, gold, and spring share in it. And by 'plural' in his statement 'meanings or names' is meant more than one. He used the plural form to match his statement in the definition of 'general': 'a plurality of named entities'. Thus what was said is refuted: that the plural gives the impression that the number three is a condition in multivocality as it is a condition in generality, but it is not so; rather multivocality is established between two meanings or two names like 'qur''. Then know that what is meant by his statement 'al-mushtarak' is the terminological multivocal, and by his statement 'what is shared' is the linguistic sharing, so there is no circularity. Its ruling is suspension etc.: meaning the ruling of the multivocal is that one suspends regarding believing in a specific meaning from among the meanings, except that what is intended by it is true for a purpose, so that one reflects upon it so that some meanings become preponderant by reflection; because the word equally bears each of the meanings, and the intended one is only one, so reflection is necessary, just as our scholars reflected upon the word 'qur'' which is multivocal between purity and menses, and it became clear to them that what is intended is menses by several aspects: one that it occurred in the plural form, and its minimum is three, and if purity were intended, three would not be achieved; second that the word 'three' occurred, and if purity were intended, it would exceed three or be less than it; third that 'qur'' indicates the meaning of gathering and transition, and this meaning exists in menses because it is blood and blood gathers in the womb during the days of purity then transitions, unlike purity. And 'al-mu'awwal' is derived from 'ala ya'ulu' if it becomes preponderant, and 'awwaltuhu' I verified it and turned it; for if you specify one of its meanings, you have turned away from the other possible aspects. What became preponderant etc.: he restricted it with this because what is meant by mu'awwal here is the mu'awwal that results from the multivocal after preponderance of some of its aspects, not the absolute; because the hidden, the problematic, and the ambiguous if their obscurity is removed by a probable evidence also become mu'awwal, but it is among the sections of clarification, not from the sections of the wording. 'By dominant opinion': i.e. prevailing conjecture, whether obtained by a solitary report, analogy, or other such as reflection in the wording itself or in the context, as in His statement: 'Permitted for you on the night of fasting is...[ref. to 2:187]' it is known that it is from 'hall' (permissibility), and in His statement: 'He has settled us in the abode of permanence [35:35]' it is known that it is from 'hulul' (settling), or in the preceding context as in 'qur'' it is known that it is menses considering what preceded of the three. The summary is that the mu'awwal is a type of multivocal that results from preponderance of one of its meanings by the interpretation of the mujtahid, and before any of its meanings became preponderant over another it was multivocal. And interpretation is the consideration of a possibility supported by evidence that makes it more dominant in conjecture than the rest of what the word indicates. The mu'awwal is counted among the sections of textual structure in wording and language, even though it results from the act of interpretation; because the ruling after interpretation is attributed to the wording; because attributing the ruling to the stronger proof is preferable, and thus they attribute the ruling in the explicit text to the text itself not to the effective cause because it is stronger than it. Like the ambiguous when clarification reaches it through a solitary report, it becomes definitively established even though the solitary report does not yield certainty; so the ruling after clarification is attributed to the clarified because it is stronger than the solitary report. Its ruling etc.: i.e. the ruling of the mu'awwal is the obligation of acting upon it; so it is obligatory to act upon what the mujtahid's interpretation determined, with the probability that it is mistaken and correct is in the other side. That is because if the interpretation is established by opinion, opinion is subject to correctness and error, so what is established by it is probable; and if it is by a solitary report, it is also conjectural. In sum, it necessitates action but not knowledge, so the one who denies it is not deemed a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical discussion of two key usul al-fiqh terms: al-mushtarak (the multivocal/homonym) and al-mu'awwal (the interpreted). The author first establishes that the fourth division of textual structure is al-mushtarak, emphasizing that its multivocality is alternative (badal) rather than comprehensive (shumul), thus distinguishing it from al-'amm (general). He points out that mushtarak can be of two types: multivocality of meanings (like 'nahal' for both drinking and thirst) or multivocality of named entities (like 'ayn' referring to sun, knee, slave-girl, gold, spring). The use of plural in the definition is only to indicate 'more than one', not to require three as condition. The ruling of mushtarak is tawaqquf (suspension): one must refrain from assigning a specific meaning until reflection leads to preponderance. The classic example is the word 'qur'' which is shared between purity (tuhr) and menses (hayd); Hanafi scholars, through reflection on the context (the number three), determined it means menses. Then comes al-mu'awwal: it is what results from mushtarak when one meaning becomes preponderant through dominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y). The author clarifies that mu'awwal here is specifically the product of preponderance from mushtarak, not the general mu'awwal that can result from clarifying khafi, mushkil, or mujmal through probable evidence. The 'dominant opinion' can come from solitary reports, qiyas, or reflection on the wording or context. Importantly, the mu'awwal is considered a division of textual structure even though it depends on the act of interpretation, because the post-interpretation ruling is attributed to the stronger proof (the wording) rather than the weaker (the interpretation). Its ruling is wujub al-'amal (obligation to act) with the probability of error; it does not yield certainty, so rejecting it does not entail disbelief. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi usul approach of careful linguistic and rational analysis to derive rulings.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word shared between multiple meanings or referents, such that each is equally possible; the multivocality is alternative (badal) not comprehensive (shumul).
المؤول (al-mu'awwal): A mushtarak word after one of its meanings has been rendered preponderant by dominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y) through interpretation (ta'wil).
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): The ruling of a mushtarak word: suspension from assigning a specific meaning until reflection leads to preponderance.
التأويل (al-ta'wil): The consideration of a possibility supported by evidence that makes one meaning more dominant in conjecture than others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) of a mushtarak word before any preponderance?
A) Immediate obligation to act on all meanings
B) Suspension (tawaqquf) until reflection leads to preponderance
C) It is considered mu'awwal from the start
D) It is treated as 'amm (general)

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak word must be suspended until one meaning becomes preponderant by dominant opinion, at which point it becomes mu'awwal and obligates action without certainty.

Definition of al-Zahir and the Condition of Siyāq
Defines al-Zahir and explains intra-Hanafi dispute on siyaq condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-Zahir according to early and later Hanafi scholars, and explain the role of siyaq in distinguishing Zahir from Nass.

Core Idea
Zahir is a text whose meaning is apparent from its wording; later Hanafis require it not be driven for that meaning to distinguish it from Nass.

This passage addresses a key distinction in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: how to classify texts by their clarity. It defines al-Zahir and reveals an important intra-madhhab dispute about whether the text must not be specifically 'driven' (siyaq) for its meaning. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to categorize Quranic verses and hadiths correctly and to follow the later Hanafi preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four divisions of textual clarity
The passage outlines four categories: Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, and Muhkam. These are based on degrees of apparentness and the possibility of interpretation or abrogation.
These categories are distinct in concept but early Hanafis saw them as overlapping in actual texts.
2
Define al-Zahir as per the passage
Zahir is a text whose meaning is apparent from its wording alone, without needing external clues or contextual indications. The 'apparentness' is linguistic (by mere wording to a native speaker).
This excludes khafi (hidden) and mushkil (problematic) texts.
3
Understand the condition of siyaq
Later Hanafi scholars (e.g., Sharh al-Badi') add a condition: Zahir must not be 'masuq' (driven) for its meaning. If it is siyaq, it becomes Nass. Early Hanafis reject this condition.
Siyaq means the text is formulated specifically to convey that meaning.
4
Compare early and later Hanafi positions
Early Hanafis define Zahir purely by apparentness. Later Hanafis require the lack of siyaq to maintain a distinction between Zahir and Nass. The author sides with the later view, calling it 'the truth'.
The practical consequence: a text that is siyaq is classified as Zahir by early Hanafis but as Nass by later Hanafis.
5
Apply the definition to a sample text
Given a Quranic verse or hadith, determine if it is Zahir or Nass by checking if its meaning appears from the wording alone and if it is driven for that meaning.
Later Hanafis would require that it is not siyaq to be Zahir.

Source Text

ثم شرع في التقسيم الثاني فقال: والقسم الثاني إلخ أي التقسيم الثاني في طرق إظهار المعنى للسامع بذلك النظم المذكور في التقسيم الأول من الخاص والعام، يعني كيف يظهر المعنى من النظم سوقاً أو غير سوق، محتملا للتأويل أو لا، والحاصل أن هذا تقسيم باعتبار دلالة النظم على المعنى باعتبار مراتب الظهور. وهي: أي الأقسام الحاصلة من هذا التقسيم الذي وجوه البيان أربعة لأنه ظهر معناه، فلا يخلو من أن يحتمل التأويل أم لا، على الأول إن كان ظهور معناه بمجرد الصيغة فهو الظاهر وإلا فهو النص، وعلى الثاني إن قبل النسخ فهو المفسر وإلا فهو المحكم، وتلك الأقسام متمايزة بحسب المفهوم وباعتبار الحيثية، لكنها متداخلة بحسب الوجود خلافاً للمتأخرين، فإن عندهم أقسام متباينة؛ لأنهم يشترطون في الظاهر عدم كونه مسوقا للمعنى، وفي النص احتمال التخصيص والتأويل أي أحدهما، وفي المفسر احتمال النسخ، وسيجيء الإشارة إليه. ف : اعلم أن هذا التقسيم والرابع يتعلق بالكلام كما أن الأول والثالث يتعلق بالمفرد. ظهر المراد للسامع والمراد بالظهور معناه اللغوي فلا دور بنفس الصيغة أي بمجرد سماعها إذا كان من أهل اللسان بلا قرينة تنضم إليه، واحترز به عن الخفي والمشكل وغيرها؛ إذ ظهور المعنى فيها يتوقف على أمر آخر بعد السماع. ثم اعلم أن كثيرا من المحققين كشارح البديع قالوا: لا بد في تعريف الظاهر من قيد آخر، وهو أن لا يكون مسوقا له؛ لأنه هو الفارق بينه وبين النص، وهذا هو الحق؛ لأن زيادة الوضوح في النص إنما هي لأجل=
Then he began the second division and said: 'And the second division etc.' i.e. the second division concerning the ways of manifesting meaning to the listener through that expression mentioned in the first division of special and general. Meaning, how the meaning appears from the expression, whether driven (siyaq) or not driven, probable for interpretation or not. The gist is that this is a division according to the indication of the expression to the meaning in terms of degrees of clarity. They are the divisions resulting from this division: the aspects of clarification are four, because its meaning is apparent. So it is not free from whether it admits interpretation or not. In the first case, if the emergence of its meaning is by the mere wording, then it is al-Zahir, otherwise it is al-Nass. In the second, if it admits abrogation (naskh), then it is al-Mufassar, otherwise it is al-Muhkam. These divisions are distinct in concept and perspective, but they overlap in existence, contrary to the later scholars, according to whom the divisions are distinct, because they require in al-Zahir that it not be driven (siyaq) for the meaning, and in al-Nass the possibility of specification (takhsis) and interpretation, i.e. one of them, and in al-Mufassar the possibility of abrogation. The reference to this will come. So know that this division and the fourth relate to speech, just as the first and third relate to the word. The intended meaning appears to the listener, and by 'appearance' is meant its linguistic meaning, so there is no circularity. 'By the mere wording' means by just hearing it if one is a native speaker, without any accompanying contextual indication. By this, he distinguishes it from the hidden (khafi) and problematic (mushkil) and others, because the emergence of meaning in those depends on something else after hearing. Then know that many scholars such as the commentator al-Badi' said: 'A condition must be added in the definition of al-Zahir: that it not be driven (siyaq) for its meaning, because that is what differentiates it from al-Nass. This is correct, because the increased clarity in al-Nass is due to...'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second division of textual clarity. It defines al-Zahir as a text whose meaning is apparent from its wording alone, without needing any external clue. The commentator explains the framework: texts can be divided into four categories based on the degree of clarity: Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, and Muhkam. Zahir is the least clear, then Nass, then Mufassar, then Muhkam (which cannot be abrogated). The commentator notes a difference between early and later Hanafi scholars: later scholars require that Zahir not be 'masuq' (driven/siyaq) for its meaning, meaning that the text must not be explicitly formulated to express that meaning. If it is siyaq, then it becomes Nass. The early scholars (like al-Qadi Abu Zayd, Sadr al-Islam Abu al-Yusr, Sayyid al-Imam Abu al-Qasim) reject this condition and say Zahir is defined by apparentness regardless of siyaq. The commentator sides with the later scholars, calling their view 'the truth'. This passage also distinguishes Zahir from other categories like khafi (hidden) and mushkil (problematic), which require additional reasoning after hearing.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-Zahir): A text whose meaning is apparent from its wording alone, without need for external clues or siyaq (according to early Hanafis). Later Hanafis add the condition of not being siyaq.
المسوق (al-Masuq): A text that is 'driven' or formulated specifically to convey that meaning, i.e., having siyaq.
النص (al-Nass): A text whose meaning appears not from the mere wording alone but from being driven (siyaq) for that meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whether Zahir can be abrogated.
B) Whether siyaq is a condition for Zahir's definition.
C) Whether Zahir yields certainty.
D) Whether Zahir is part of the four divisions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Zahir is defined by immediate apparentness; the later Hanafi school adds that it must not be driven for its meaning to preserve a clear distinction from al-Nass.

Two Types of Isti'ara Connection in Legal Language
Classification of metaphorical connections and their legal application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of connection (ittisal) in metaphorical usage and apply the Hanafi classification to a legal conditional statement.

Core Idea
Isti'ara in legal language relies on form-based connection (ittisal suri) and partial emancipation is valid in Hanafi law.

This passage from a classical usul al-fiqh text delves into the linguistic foundations of legal reasoning. It distinguishes two ways a word can be used metaphorically: through a shared attribute (meaning-based) or through contiguity or causality (form-based). The author then applies this distinction to a real juristic problem—conditional emancipation—showing how the type of connection determines the ruling, and highlighting an intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of connection
From the text, extract the definitions of ittisal ma'nawi (meaning-based connection) and ittisal suri (form-based connection). Note the example for each: lion for brave man (ma'nawi) and sky for rain (suri).
Meaning-based involves a shared attribute (e.g., bravery); form-based involves contiguity or causality.
2
Understand the role of causality (sababiyya)
The text explains that form-based connection includes cause-effect relationships. Recognize that in legal language, using 'cause' for 'effect' or vice versa is a form-based metaphor, not meaning-based.
This is key to understanding how conditional phrases like 'If I buy' can stand for ownership.
3
Apply classification to the emancipation case
Take the case: 'If I buy a slave, he is free.' The speaker buys in parts. Classify the relationship between 'buy' and 'own' as form-based (causal). Determine how custom (urf) allows 'buy' to stand for the entire acquisition process.
The text says: 'it is said customarily: he is the buyer of the slave.'
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify Abu Hanifa's position: partial emancipation valid (only the second half freed). Contrast with the Sahibayn: entire slave freed requiring sa'ayah for the first half. Note each side's proof from the text.
Abu Hanifa relies on tajazzi al-i'taq (partial emancipation possible) and custom. The Sahibayn argue emancipation cannot be partial.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the slave is partially free; under the Sahibayn, the slave is fully free but must earn compensation for half. This shows how the type of connection and its interpretation affect fatwa.
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as primary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
في تسمية الشجاع أسداً والبليد حماراً، أو ذاتاً كما في تسمية المطر سماء، لوصف الشجاعة والاتصال سبباً من هذا القبيل.
وهو نوعان: أحدهما اتصال المعنى، والثاني اتصال الصورة. فالاتصال المعنوي أن يكون بين المعنى الحقيقي كالحيوان المفترس للأسد، والمجازي كالرجل الشجاع اشتراك في وصف خاص مشهور في العرف كالشجاعة. والمجاز بهذه العلاقة يسمى استعارة عند علماء البيان. والاتصال الصوري أن تكون صورة المعنى المجازي متصلاً بصورة المعنى الحقيقي بنوع محاورة. تسمية المطر سماء: فإن صورة المطر متصلة بصورة السماء أي السحاب؛ وذلك لأن السماء اسم للسحاب ولكل ما علاك وأظلك، والمطر إنما ينزل من السحاب، فوجد بينهما الاتصال الصوري أي المجاورة، لا المعنوي؛ إذ لا مناسبة بينهما بوجه. والمجاز بهذه العلاقة يسمى مجازاً مرسلا عندهم.
ثم أراد أن المجاز بالاتصال المعنوي والصوري موجود في الألفاظ الشرعية أيضاً كما هو موجود في الألفاظ اللغوية، ولكن لما كان القسم الثاني يبتني عليه المسائل الخلافية من استعارة ألفاظ الطلاق للعتق كما ستعرف، أعرض عن القسم الأول وذكر القسم الثاني.
من هذا القبيل: أي الاتصال باعتبار السببية بأن يكون الأول سبباً للثاني، أو مسبباً عنه، أو علة للثاني، أو معلولا له، من قبيل الاتصال الصوري؛ لأن العلة والحكم، وكذا السبب والمسبب يتصل أحدهما بالآخر صورة فقط؛ إذ لا مناسبة بينهما معنى بوجه ليكون من قبيل الاتصال المعنوي. وقوله: "سبباً" منصوب على التمييز من الاتصال، والمراد بالسبب معناه اللغوي هو ما يتصل به إلى الشيء ويفضي إليه، فيتناول العلة أيضاً؛ لوجود معنى الإفضاء كما يتناول السبب المصطلح.
نوعان: ولما كان علاقة التعليل أقوى من السببية قدمها من الطرفين، وذلك لأن كل واحد منهما لا ينفك عن الآخر. لأن العلة لم تشرع إلا لحكمها والحكم لا يثبت إلا بعلة، فاستوى الاتصال، فعمّت الاستعارة.
ولهذا قلنا فيمن قال: إن اشتريت عبداً فهو حر، فاشترى نصف عبد وباعه ثم اشترى النصف الآخر: يعتق هذا النصف الآخر. ولو قال: إن ملكت، لا يعتق ما لم يجتمع الكل في ملكه.
فإن عنى بأحدهما الآخر تعمل نيته في الموضعين، لكن فيما فيه تخفيف عليه.
فعمّت الاستعارة بأن يذكر أحدهما ويراد به الآخر مجازاً، والمراد بالاستعارة عند أرباب الأصول المجاز، سواء كان بعلاقة التشبيه أو لا.
ولهذا أي لأجل جواز الاستعارة من الجانبين. يعتق هذا النصف إلخ لوجود الشرط بتمامه وهو الشراء الصحيح، وإن وجد تفريقاً، فإنه لا يشترط في الشراء أن يجتمع الكل في الملك، فإنه يقال له عرفاً: إنه مشتري العبد. فالحاصل وجد الشرط بتمامه عند اشتراء النصف الأخير ولا في ملكه حينئذ إلا النصف فيعتق هذا النصف، وهذا على رأي أبي حنيفة لتجزي الإعتاق، وأما عندهما فيعتق الكل. ثم يجب السعاية في نصف أداء الضمان كذا قال صاحب "الكشف".
والمسألة على أربعة أوجه أحدهما هذا والثاني ما بينه بقوله: ولو قال: "إن ملكت عبداً فهو حر"، أي أتى بلفظ "ملكت" مقام اشتريت، والمسألة بحالها لا يعتق في الاستحسان. ما لم يجتمع إلخ لأن الملك مطلق، والمطلق قد يتقيد بدلالة العادة، فيكون المراد بصفة الاجتماع؛ إذ لا يقال عرفاً لمن ملك شيئاً ثم باعه ثم ملك شيئاً آخر: إنه مالك الكل. نعم يقال: إنه مشتري الكل، فالحاصل لم يوجد الشرط بتمامه الملك بصفة الاجتماع، فلا يعتق.
والثالث والرابع أن يقول مشيراً إلى العبد المعين: وهو إن اشتريت هذا العبد فهو حر، وإن ملكت هذا العبد فهو حر، أي لا يقول منكراً كما في الصورتين الأوليين مشيراً إلى المعين، والمسألة بحالها، فيعتق النصف في الفصلين؛ لأن التفريق والاجتماع وصف، والأوصاف لا تعتبر في الحاضر المعين بخلاف الغائب، كما برهن عليه في موضعه.
فلما فرغ من تمهيد المسألة فرع عليه ما هو المقصود من البيان من جواز الاستعارة من الطرفين. فإن عنى بأحدهما بأن يقول: أردت بقولي "إن اشتريت": "إن ملكت" حتى يشترط الاجتماع، ولا يعتق النصف الآخر، أو عكس بأن قال أردت بقولي إن ملكت: إن اشتريت حتى لا يشترط الاجتماع ويعتق النصف الآخر. تعمل نيته إلخ وتصدق ديانةً لصحة الاستعارة من الطرفين. لكن فيما إلخ: وهو فيما إذا نوى الملك بالشراء. وجه التخفيف أنه يحتاج حينئذ إلى وصف الاجتماع.
The third section on the aspects of using textual composition. In naming a brave man 'lion' and a stupid man 'donkey', or 'essence' as in naming rain 'sky', due to the description of bravery and the connection as a cause from this category. It is of two types: one is connection of meaning, and the second is connection of form. The meaning-based connection is that there is between the real meaning, such as the predatory animal for lion, and the metaphorical meaning, such as the brave man, a sharing in a specific attribute known in custom, like bravery. The metaphor with this relationship is called isti'ara by scholars of rhetoric. The form-based connection is that the form of the metaphorical meaning is connected to the form of the real meaning by a type of contiguity. Naming rain 'sky': for the form of rain is connected to the form of sky, meaning clouds; because 'sky' is a name for clouds and for anything that is above you and shades you, and rain only descends from clouds, so there is between them a form-based connection, i.e., contiguity, not a meaning-based one; for there is no suitability between them in any way. The metaphor with this relationship is called majaz mursal by them. Then he intended that metaphor by meaning-based and form-based connection also exists in legal expressions as it exists in linguistic expressions, but since the second section is based on the contentious issues of using the words of divorce for emancipation as you will know, he turned away from the first section and mentioned the second section. From this category: i.e., the connection by causality such that the first is a cause for the second, or an effect of it, or a cause for the second, or an effect of it, from the category of form-based connection; because the cause and the ruling, and likewise the cause and the effect, are connected to each other only in form; for there is no suitability between them in meaning in any way to be from the category of meaning-based connection. His saying: 'a cause' is accusative as a specification of connection, and what is meant by 'cause' is its linguistic meaning: that by which one reaches the thing and leads to it, so it includes the 'illa as well; due to the presence of the meaning of leading, just as it includes the technical cause. Two types: and since the relationship of 'illa is stronger than causality, he presented it from both sides, because each one is inseparable from the other. For the 'illa was not legislated except for its ruling, and the ruling is not established except by an 'illa, so the connection becomes equal, and the isti'ara becomes general. Therefore we said about one who said: 'If I buy a slave, he is free', then he bought half a slave and sold it, then bought the other half: this other half becomes free. If he said: 'If I own', it does not become free unless the whole is combined in his ownership. Then if he intends one by the other, his intention is acted upon in both situations, but in what brings ease upon him. So isti'ara becomes general in that one of them is mentioned and the other is intended metaphorically, and what is meant by isti'ara according to the scholars of usul is majaz, whether with a relationship of similitude or not. Therefore, i.e., due to the permissibility of isti'ara from both sides. This half becomes free etc. due to the presence of the condition in its entirety, which is a valid purchase, even if there is division; for in purchase it is not required that the whole be combined in ownership, for it is said customarily: he is the buyer of the slave. So the result: the condition was found in its entirety at the purchase of the last half, and at that time he only owns half, so this half becomes free. This is according to Abu Hanifa because emancipation can be partial; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the whole becomes free. Then earning (sa'ayah) is necessary for half of the compensation as mentioned by the author of al-Kashf. The issue is on four faces: one is this, and the second is what he explained by his saying: and if he said: 'If I own a slave, he is free', i.e., he uttered 'I own' in place of 'I bought', and the issue remains as it is, it does not become free in istihsan. Unless the whole is combined etc. because ownership is absolute, and the absolute may be restricted by customary indication, so the intended meaning is with the attribute of combination; for it is not said customarily of one who owned something then sold it then owned something else: he is the owner of the whole. Rather it is said: he is the buyer of the whole. So the result: the condition was not found complete, i.e., ownership with the attribute of combination, so it does not become free. The third and fourth: that he says pointing to the specific slave: 'If I buy this slave, he is free', and 'If I own this slave, he is free', i.e., he does not say indefinitely as in the first two cases but pointing to the specific, and the issue remains as it is, then half becomes free in both cases; because division and combination are attributes, and attributes are not considered in a present specific thing, unlike the absent, as proven in its place. When he finished setting up the issue, he branched onto what is the intended purpose of the explanation: the permissibility of isti'ara from both sides. If he intends one by the other, such as saying: I meant by my saying 'If I buy': 'If I own' so that combination is required, and the other half does not become free, or the opposite, by saying: I meant by my saying 'If I own': 'If I buy' so that combination is not required and the other half becomes free. His intention is acted upon etc. in conscience (diyanatan) due to the validity of isti'ara from both sides. But in what etc.: that is, in the case where he intends ownership by purchase. The aspect of easing is that he then needs the attribute of combination.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the third section of a usul work dealing with the aspects of using textual composition (nazm). The author explains the two types of connection (ittisal) that underlie metaphorical transfer (isti'ara) in both linguistic and legal expressions. The first type is meaning-based connection (ittisal ma'nawi), where the real and metaphorical meanings share a common attribute (e.g., shuja'a for lion). The second is form-based connection (ittisal suri), based on contiguity or causality, such as calling rain 'sky' because of spatial proximity. In legal language, form-based connection is more relevant because it gives rise to ikhtilaf, as when using words of divorce for emancipation. The author then applies this to two hypothetical cases: (1) 'If I buy a slave, he is free' – even if the purchase is in parts, upon acquiring the last half the slave is partially freed (according to Abu Hanifa), because 'buying' customarily refers to the entire transaction. (2) 'If I own a slave, he is free' – here ownership is absolute, but custom restricts it to continuous possession; hence freedom only occurs if all parts are owned together. Abu Hanifa holds partial emancipation is possible; his two companions rule the entire slave is freed. The discussion also covers cases where the slave is specified by pointing. The key legal principle is that isti'ara (using one word to mean another) is permissible in oaths and conditions, based on customary usage and the underlying connection. The author shows that the intention of the speaker is given effect (diyanatan) when it aligns with the permissible metaphorical transfer.

Key Terms
اتصال معنوي (Ittisal Ma'nawi): Connection between real and metaphorical meaning based on a shared attribute (e.g., bravery for lion).
اتصال صوري (Ittisal Suri): Connection based on contiguity or causality, not on shared attribute (e.g., calling rain 'sky').
استعارة (Isti'ara): Using one word to mean another metaphorically, either with or without similitude.
سبب (Sabab): Linguistic cause that leads to something, including both 'illa and technical cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of 'If I buy a slave, he is free', where someone buys half, sells it, then buys the other half, what is the Hanafi ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The entire slave becomes free immediately upon purchasing the first half.
B) Only the second half becomes free because the purchase condition is met for that part.
C) No slave becomes free because the purchase was not complete.
D) The first half requires earning (sa'ayah) and the second half is free.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical transfer in legal language relies on form-based connection (causality/contiguity), not just similarity, and the Hanafi school permits partial emancipation based on custom and the type of isti'ara.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Rule of Al-Isti'ara between Cause and Effect
Explaining permissibility of using cause for effect but not vice versa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of isti'ara between cause and effect to determine the validity of using one for the other in legal speech, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, using the cause for the effect is valid; the reverse is not, except when the effect is specific to the cause.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi usul principle governing metaphorical usage (isti'ara) of legal terms. The passage delves into the connection between cause and effect (sabab and musabbab) and why only one direction—cause for effect—is generally permissible. You'll see how this rule plays out in practical examples like saying 'you are free' to a wife intending divorce, and the debate with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of isti'ara
The passage discusses using cause for effect and effect for cause. Note which direction the Hanafis allow and which they prohibit.
The allowed direction: cause (e.g., 'you are free') used to indicate effect (divorce). Prohibited: effect (divorce) used to indicate cause (freedom).
2
Trace the definition of sabab mahd (pure cause)
Understand that a pure cause is one that leads to the ruling without being an effective cause (illa). This is crucial to the metaphor's validity.
'Sabab mahd' is a cause that is not itself an 'illa; the ruling is not attributed to it.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i challenge
Imam al-Shafi'i argued for a meaningful connection between divorce and manumission, allowing both directions. Work through his proof and the Hanafi response.
Shafi'i bases on shared removal (isqat) and pervasion (saraya). Hanafi response distinguishes between removing a right (yamin) and establishing freedom (quwwa).
4
Apply the exception
The verse 'I see myself pressing wine' shows an exception where effect is used for cause because the effect (wine) is specific to the cause (grapes). Determine when this exceptional condition holds.
Exception applies only when the effect is specific to the cause, e.g., wine is the juice of grapes.

الاستعارة على الاتصال المعنوي بينهما، وهو اشتراكهما في معنى شرعي وهو الإسقاط؛ لأن في الإعتاق إسقاط ملك الرقبة وفي الطلاق إسقاط ملك المتعة، ولأن كُلاً منهما يبنى على السراية، فكما أن من طلق امرأته نصفها أو نصف تطليقة يسري طلاقه إلى الكل، كذلك العتق يسري إلى الكل حتى من أعتق نصف عبده يسري إلى الكل، فوجد الاتصال المعنوي. ونحن نقول: كما أنه ليس بينهما الاتصال الصوري؛ لأنه في الشرعيات بالسبية، وقد مرّ أن العكس غير جائز كذلك ليس بينهما الاتصال المعنوي؛ لأن في الطلاق إزالة ملك اليمين وفي العتاق إثبات القوة، فأين بينهما الاشتراك في وصف خاص مشهور كما يوجد بين الأسد والرجل الشجاع؟ وقد يورد على أصل القاعدة بأنه لا اتصال هنا أيضاً؛ لأن زوال ملك المتعة إنما يثبت ضمنًا في زوال ملك الرقبة في الأمة لا في الحرة، فكيف يستعار العتاق للطلاق الذي فيه زوال ملك المتعة صراحةً في المنكوحة؟ أجيب بأن إزالة ملك الرقبة مستلزمة لزوال حقيقة ملك المتعة، وهي حقيقة واحدة نوعية لا تختلف بالذات في ملك النكاح واليمين نعم، تختلف بالاعتبار وهو كونه مقصودا في النكاح وغير مقصود في اليمين وهذا لايقدح في جواز الاستعارة.
Book of Allah the Exalted. The third section on the ways of using linguistic structure. It is not believed in judicial judgment but is believed religiously. Connection of cause and effect. The second [type]: connection of the branch to what is a pure cause that is not a legal cause placed for it, like the connection of the removal of the right of enjoyment (muta) by words of manumission following the removal of ownership of the slave. And half is also not freed upon him, so in it there is a clear benefit for him. So in this case, he is not believed in judicial judgment due to suspicion of lying, not because the metaphor is invalid religiously: i.e., between him and Allah, such that if he consults a jurist, he gives him a fatwa according to his intention, due to the permissibility of the metaphor, even if the judge does not believe him due to suspicion, just as if he asks a scholar: 'I owe someone one hundred dirhams and I have repaid it, am I free of the debt?' He gives a fatwa of discharge, but the judge does not rule on discharge unless he provides evidence of payment. An objection: In intending purchase by ownership there is also easing upon him, because ownership is acquired by purchase, gift, and bequest, and purchase is specific to a particular cause among them, so he should not be believed in judgment there either. And it was raised: If metaphor between cause and effect were allowed from both sides, then marriage would be contracted by 'making lawful' and 'permitting' because marriage is the cause for them? Answer: We do not concede that it is only placed for the permissibility of enjoyment and its permission, rather it is a cause for the ownership of enjoyment and use. Another objection: If it were a cause for ownership of the pleasures of sexual intercourse, it would be contracted by hire and loan due to the meaningful connection between them? Answer: Metaphor is based on transfer from the necessary to the necessary, and hire and loan do not necessitate ownership of the pleasure of sexual intercourse. Then an objection was raised: There is no connection between 'if I own' and 'if I buy' because absolute ownership is more general than ownership acquired by purchase, so it is not an effect of purchase, nor is purchase a cause for it? Answer: Purchase is a cause for specific ownership, and that entails general ownership, so the metaphor is valid. This is according to some commentaries. Connection of the branch: i.e., the effect, meaning the ruling. Pure cause: The cause is that which leads to the ruling, but obligation or existence of the ruling is not attributed to it, and the pure cause is that which is like that and has no aspect of effective cause (illah) in it, and it is called a real cause. Since the condition of a pure cause is that the intervening effective cause between it and the ruling is not attributed to it, just as the ruling itself is not attributed to it, and in the counterpart the effective cause is attributed to the cause, namely the removal of ownership of the slave to the cause, which is his saying 'you are free', indicating that what is meant is that the ruling is not attributed to it, but the effective cause is. 'Not a cause' etc. means by pure cause it is not an effective cause placed for the branch i.e., the ruling, such that that ruling is attributed to it, not that the effective cause also is not attributed to it, for that is not a condition here. Like the connection of removal etc.: For if he says to his female slave 'you are free', or 'I have freed you', or 'I have manumitted you', by those words the right of enjoyment is removed such that it is not permissible for him to have intercourse without marriage. 'Following' etc.: For first by these words ownership of the slave is removed, and by virtue of its removal the right of enjoyment is removed. So the removal of the right of enjoyment is caused by the removal of ownership of the slave. As for these words, they are a pure cause for the removal of the right of enjoyment because they lead to it, and they are not placed in law for that, but rather they are placed for the removal of ownership of the slave. And that is this type of connection of the cause: metaphor of the original for the branch and the cause for the ruling, so it is valid for him to say to his wife 'you are free' intending 'you are divorced' and divorce occurs, because his saying 'you are free' is a cause and original as mentioned, and divorce is a branch and ruling, so it is permissible to mention the cause and intend the effect, because the effect needs the cause in terms of existence. And his saying 'the cause for the ruling' is an explanation of his saying 'the original for the branch', to show that by 'original' and 'cause' and by 'ruling' and 'branch' one thing is meant. This is said without its opposite, so he cannot say to his female slave 'you are divorced' intending 'you are free' and she does not become free, so it is not permissible to mention the effect and intend the cause, because the connection of the branch (the effect) to the original (the cause) with respect to intending the original by it is in the status of non-existence; it is valid because the cause is independent of the branch (the cause). The conclusion is that the connection of the effect to the cause is absent when the cause is intended by it, because the cause is independent of the effect. As for with respect to intending the effect by the cause, the connection exists. Do you not see that his saying 'you are free' and the like are only legislated for the removal of ownership of the slave, and the removal of the right of enjoyment is merely an incidental thing? So it is not permissible to mention the effect and intend the cause unless the effect is specific to the cause, as in Allah's statement telling: 'I see myself pressing wine' (Yusuf 36). For here wine is metaphorically used for grapes, because it is specific to it, considering that wine is the juice of grapes, and grapes do not exist without its juice, so grapes become connected to it and dependent on it. This is according to us. Al-Shafi'i said: The reverse of this metaphor is also valid, i.e., metaphor of the effect for the cause, so it is permissible to use divorce for manumission just as its opposite is permissible. Al-Shafi'i based the validity of this metaphor on the meaningful connection between them, which is their sharing in a legal meaning, namely removal (isqat), because in manumission there is removal of ownership of the slave, and in divorce there is removal of the right of enjoyment. And because each of them is based on pervasion (saraya), just as if a man divorces his wife half a divorce, his divorce pervades to the whole, similarly manumission pervades to the whole, so that if one manumits half his slave, it pervades to the whole. So a meaningful connection exists. But we say: Just as there is no formal connection between them, because in legal matters [connection] is by causation, and it has been stated that the reverse is not permissible, likewise there is no meaningful connection between them, because in divorce there is removal of the right of ownership (yamin), and in manumission there is establishment of freedom (quwwa). So where is there between them sharing in a specific well-known attribute as exists between the lion and the brave man? And it may be objected against the base rule that there is no connection here either, because the removal of the right of enjoyment is only established implicitly in the removal of ownership of the slave in the case of a female slave, not in a free woman, so how can manumission be used metaphorically for divorce which involves removal of the right of enjoyment explicitly in a married woman? Answer: The removal of ownership of the slave necessarily implies the removal of the reality of the right of enjoyment, and it is a single reality of one type that does not differ inherently in the ownership of marriage and ownership of the right hand. Yes, they differ in consideration—it being intended in marriage and not intended in the right hand—and this does not impair the permissibility of the metaphor.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of a specific usul rule: the permissibility of using cause (sabab) to refer to effect (musabbab) via metaphor (isti'ara), and the prohibition of the reverse. The author first distinguishes between judicial (qada) and religious (diyana) spheres: in court, a person's claim to intend a metaphorical meaning may be rejected due to suspicion of lying, but in private religious duty, if he consults a jurist, the jurist may give a fatwa according to his intention. The main topic is the second type of connection: where a branch (effect) is connected to a pure cause (sabab mahd) that is not itself a legal cause ('illa) for that effect. The example given is saying 'you are free' to one's wife intending divorce. Here, the words 'you are free' are a cause for manumission (removal of ownership), but metaphorically used for divorce (removal of marriage rights). This is valid because the effect needs the cause for its existence. However, the reverse—saying 'you are divorced' to a slave intending manumission—is invalid because the cause (manumission) is independent and does not need the effect (divorce). The author cites the Quranic verse 'I see myself pressing wine' (Yusuf 36) as an exceptional case where the effect (wine) is used for the cause (grapes) due to the specificity of wine to grapes. This is followed by the Shafi'i position: Al-Shafi'i allows both directions, arguing a meaningful connection between divorce and manumission based on both involving removal (isqat) and pervasion (saraya). The Hanafi response refutes this, stating that the formal connection is absent because legal matters are based on causation, and the meaningful connection is also absent because divorce removes a right while manumission establishes freedom. The objection that the removal of marriage rights is only implicit in slave manumission is answered by pointing out that the reality of removal is the same type. The passage concludes with the Hanafi position firmly upheld.

Key Terms
اتصال السبب والمسبب (ittisal al-sabab wa al-musabbab): The connection between the cause and the effect in legal language, where one can be used metaphorically for the other under certain conditions.
سبب محض (sabab mahd): A pure cause that leads to a ruling without being an effective cause ('illa); the ruling is not attributed to it.
استعارة (isti'ara): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says to his female slave 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) intending to free her, what is the ruling?
A) She becomes free.
B) She does not become free.
C) It depends on his intention and context.
D) It is valid only if he also intends divorce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, isti'ara from cause to effect is valid, but from effect to cause is not, unless the effect is specific to the cause.
