Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Generality of Majaz: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining the ruling of metaphor and its general scope, with refutation of Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that majaz can be general (ʿumūm al-majāz) and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument, using the hadith of saʿ to derive the ruling on riba in non-food items.

Core Idea
Majaz can be general when the word's form indicates generality; the Qur'an's use of majaz proves it is not a necessity.

This passage tackles a key usul dispute: can metaphorical usage (majaz) convey a general (amm) meaning, just like literal usage (haqiqah)? The Hanafi school affirms it, while some Shafi'i scholars restrict majaz to the minimum necessary, denying generality. The argument hinges on the grammatical form of the word—specifically the definite article (al-) for generality—and the practical consequence for riba rulings. By studying this lesson, you will learn how to analyse a naqli dalil (hadith) and apply the Hanafi methodology to defend the generality of majaz.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on ʿumūm al-majāz
State clearly that the Hanafi school holds that majaz can be general, just like haqiqah. The ruling depends on the word's form: if the word is general (e.g., with definite article), the metaphorical meaning also covers all individuals of a single type of relation.
The text uses the example of 'sa'' (a measure) to show that when used metaphorically for its contents, the definite article 'al-' indicates generality.
2
Understand the Shafi'i counterclaim
Some Shafi'i scholars argue that majaz is a necessity for broadening speech, so it should be limited to the minimum needed to remove ambiguity. Therefore, majaz cannot convey overarching generality; it should be restricted to a specific intended meaning (e.g., food only).
The opposing dalil: the original principle is to use words literally; metaphor leads to ambiguity unless necessity dictates.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two prongs: (1) The Qur'an uses majaz extensively, and Allah is beyond incapacity/necessity, so majaz is not merely a necessity. (2) Generality in majaz is not an extra necessity; it is a literal (haqiqah) meaning of the grammatical form (e.g., al- for istighraq). Thus, when a metaphorical word carries a general article, the generality is a real feature of the word, not an unnecessary expansion.
The text explicitly states that the Shafi'i view is 'bāṭil' (false).
4
Apply the rule to a fiqh example: riba on non-food items
The hadith of Ibn 'Umar prohibits selling a sa' for two sa's. The Hanafi interpretation: 'sa'' metaphorically means its contents; the definite article makes it general, so riba applies to all that the measure contains, including non-food items like gypsum or lime. The Shafi'i interpretation restricts the generality to food only.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.
5
Evaluate the evidence and conclusion
The author concludes that the Shafi'i position is baseless. Note also the objection from al-Talwih that no Shafi'i scholar actually holds this view, pointing to the relation of adjacency (mujāwarah) as the basis for the metaphor. This step reinforces the need to verify attributions in usul disputes.
This critical note does not weaken the Hanafi position but adds nuance to the historical record.

القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
وهو نظير الجملة الناقصة إذا عطفت على الكاملة توقف أول الكلام على آخره؛ لصحته وافتقاره إليه، فأما الأول فتامّ في نفسه لاستغنائه عنه.
[حكم المجاز]
وحكم المجاز وجود ما أريد به خاصاً كان أو عاما، أي من المعنى المجاز بواو العطف.
وهو: أي الاتصال بين السبب والمسبب الذي يثبت من جانب واحد فقط نظيرُ اتصال الجملة الناقصة بالجملة الكاملة إذا عطفت الجملة الناقصة على الجملة الكاملة كما في قوله: هند طالق وزينب ، فقوله: هند" طالق" جملة كاملة لوجود الطرفين، وقوله: "زينب" جملة ناقصة؛ لافتقارها إلى الخبر؛ إذ لو انفردت لاتفيد شيئا، وإنما أفادت توقف إلخ أي الجملة الكاملة على آخره أي على آخر الكلام، يعني به الجملة الناقصة، وهذا التوقف ليس لأجل أنه غير تام بل لصحته أي آخر الكلام وافتقاره إليه فيشترك آخر الكلام وهو قوله: "وزينب" في الخبر، ويصير مفيدا؛ إذ لو لم يتوقف الأول ولم ينتظر إلى ما يقول المتكلم بعده، حتى صار قوله: "هند طالق" كلاماً منفصلا عن قوله: "وزينب لصار آخر الكلام غير مفيد وغير صحيح، فيتوقف أول الكلام على ما بعده؛ لأجل انتظار أن المتكلم ما يقول بعده أيغيره أم يؤكده أم يفيد خبرا آخر بالعطف، حتى لو قال بعد الكلام الأول (وهو هند طالق) وطالق" وطالق" يقع الطلقات الثلث إن كانت مدخولا بها، فتوقف أول الكلام على آخره؛ لما كان لأجل صحة الآخر، لا لأنه غير تام في نفسه، عد في حقه معدوما. لاستغنائه عنه: أي عن آخر الكلام بدليل لو قال لغير المدخول بها بعد الكلام الأول: "وطالق وطالق" لا تقع الثانية والثالثة؛ لأن الجملة الأولى وهى قوله: "وهند" طالق" لما لم تتوقف في نفسها ثبت موجبها قبل التكلم بآخر الكلام، وقد بانت بالأولى، فيلغو ما بعدها، فالحاصل أن التوقف في حق أول الكلام معدوم بوجه عرفت، وفي حق آخره ثابت؛ لتوقفه عليه في حد ذاته، فثبت التوقف من أحد الجانبين كالاتصال من جانب واحد، فصار أحدهما نظير الآخر، ولما كان حكم الحقيقة وثبوت ما وضع له اللفظ خاصاً كان ذلك أو عاماً متفقا عليه عند أرباب الأصول، وكان في حكم المجاز خلاف لبعض أصحاب الشافعي بين حكم المجاز صراحة، وأشار إلى حكم الحقيقة في ضمنه كما فعل ذلك في العام والخاص روما للاختصار. عاماً: بأن يكون اللفظ الذي استعمل في غير ما وضع له بعلاقة خاصاً كالأسد، أو عاماً كالصاع، يعني إن كان اللفظ عاما كان المجاز عاماً، وإن كان خاصاً كان خاصا، وليس المراد بعموم الجاز أن يعم جميع أنواع العلاقات في لفظ واحد بأن يذكر اللفظ ويراد به حاله ومحله وما كان عليه وما يؤول إليه، وقس عليه البواقي، بل أن يعم أفراد نوع واحد من أنواع العلاقات كما يراد بالصاع جميع ما يحل فيه طعاما كان أو غيره.

حكم الحقيقة، ولهذا جعلنا لفظ "الصاع" في حديث ابن عمرهما: "لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع "بالصاعين عاماً فيما يحلّه ويجاوره، وأبى الشافعي ذلك، وقال: لا عموم للمجاز؛ لأنه ضروري، يصار إليه توسعة للكلام، وهذا باطل؛ لأن المجاز موجود في كتاب الله والله تعالى يتعالى عن العجز والضرورات.
حكم الحقيقة: بأنه يراد من اللفظ المستعمل في ما وضع له جميع ما يتناوله عاماً كان اللفظ أو خاصاً. ولهذا: أي الجريان العموم في المجاز مثل الحقيقة. عاماً : يعني لأجل أن العموم يجري في المجاز جعلنا لفظ الصاع الوارد في هذا الحديث عاماً. فيما يحلّه ويجاوره: وذلك لأن المراد من لفظ الصاع هنا ليس معناه الحقيقي بالإجماع؛ إذ لا خلاف لأحد في جواز بيع نفس الصاع الذي يكون من الخشب بالصاعين فعلم أن المراد به: معناه المجازي وهو ما يحل فيه فصار المعنى لا تبيعوا ما يسعه الصاع بما يسعه الصاعان، ولفظ "الصاع" محلى بلام الاستغراق، من المطعوم وغيره، فدلّ بعمومه على أن الربا كما يجري في المطعوم مثل الحنطة يجري في غير المطعوم كالجص والنورة. وأبى الشافعي الله : أي العموم، فالمراد بما يحل في الصاع الطعام. لأنه إلخ لأن الأصل في الكلام أن لا يستعمل في غير ما وضع له؛ لأنه يؤدي الإبهام والاختلال. توسعةً للكلام: فالحاصل أن المجاز ضروري، والضروري يثبت بقدر الضرورة تدفع بإرادة الطعام، فأية ضرورة إلى أن يراد به العموم. وهذا أي قول الشافعي بأن المجاز ضروري.
باطل: لأن المجاز موجود في كتاب الله الذي هو في أعلى رتب الفصاحة والبلاغة، وهو كلام الله تعالى، فلزم أن الله تعالى إنما استعمل المجاز في كتابه عجزاً وضرورة أنه لم يوجد لفظاً آخر حقيقياً فيه. العجز والضرورات هذا على تقدير أن يراد بالضرورة ضرورة المتكلم فلا غبار عليه، وأما إذا يراد الضرورة بالنظر إلى المخاطب وقرّر الكلام :هكذا إن المجاز إنما يعتبره المخاطب ضرورة عدم صحة الحقيقة، وهذه الضرورة تدفع بحمله على معنى، والعموم أمر ،زائد فلا يصار إليه، فحينئذ لايستقيم جواب المتن، فالجواب أن العموم معنى حقيقي؛ لأنه ثابت بدليله، فإن اللفظ لا يدلّ على العموم إلا بكونه محلى باللام مثلا، وهو موضوع بعموم ،مدلوله فهو بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة، وإن كان باعتبار أنه أريد به غير ما وضع له مجازاً.
*
واعلم أنه اعترض صاحب التلويح وقال: لا خلاف فيه لأحد من أصحاب الشافعي، ولم نجده في كتبهم.
وذلك إشارة إلى أن العلاقة المجاورة.
The third section on aspects of using structure. It is analogous to the incomplete sentence when attached to the complete sentence: the first part of the speech depends on its end for its validity and need of it. As for the first, it is complete in itself because it is independent of it. [Ruling of Metaphor] The ruling of metaphor is the existence of what is intended by it, whether specific or general, i.e., from the metaphorical meaning with the conjunction 'waw' (and). It is: the connection between cause and effect which is established from only one side, analogous to the connection of the incomplete sentence to the complete sentence when the incomplete sentence is attached to the complete sentence, as in his saying: 'Hind is divorced and Zaynab.' So his saying 'Hind is divorced' is a complete sentence due to the presence of both parts, and his saying 'Zaynab' is an incomplete sentence because it needs a predicate; if it stood alone, it would convey nothing. It only conveyed meaning by the dependence of the complete sentence on its end (i.e., the end of the speech, meaning the incomplete sentence). This dependence is not because it is incomplete, but for the validity of the end of the speech and its need for it, so the end of the speech (his saying 'and Zaynab') shares in the predicate and becomes meaningful. For if the first did not depend and wait for what the speaker says after it, so that his saying 'Hind is divorced' became a separate speech from his saying 'and Zaynab,' then the end of the speech would be meaningless and invalid. So the first part of the speech depends on what follows it in order to wait for what the speaker says after it: whether he changes it, confirms it, or conveys another predicate by conjunction. Even if after the first speech (which is 'Hind is divorced') he says 'and divorced, and divorced,' the three divorces occur if she was consummated. So the dependence of the first part of the speech on its end was for the validity of the other, not because it is incomplete in itself. It is considered non-existent in its respect due to its independence from it, i.e., from the end of the speech, as evidenced by the fact that if he said to an unconsummated woman after the first speech: 'and divorced, and divorced,' the second and third do not occur, because the first sentence (his saying 'Hind is divorced') since it did not depend in itself, its effect was established before speaking the end of the speech, and she became irrevocably divorced by the first, so what follows is void. The conclusion is that dependence with respect to the first part of the speech is non-existent in a certain way, and with respect to the end it is established because it depends on it inherently. So dependence is established from one side, like connection from one side, so one becomes analogous to the other. Since the ruling of reality (haqiqah) and the affirmation of what the word was coined for, whether specific or general, is agreed upon by the masters of usul, and there is disagreement about the ruling of metaphor from some of al-Shafi'i's followers, he stated the ruling of metaphor explicitly and referred to the ruling of reality implicitly, as he did with general and specific for brevity. 'General': meaning that the word used in other than its original meaning due to a relation may be specific like 'asad' (lion) or general like 'sa' (measure). That is, if the word is general, the metaphor is general, and if specific, it is specific. The intended meaning of the generality of metaphor is not that it encompasses all types of relations in a single word, such that the word is mentioned and its state, place, what it was upon, and what it will become are intended, and analogize the rest; rather it is that it encompasses individuals of a single type of relation, as is intended by 'sa' (measure) all that it contains, whether food or otherwise. (Page 33) The ruling of reality, and therefore we made the word 'sa' in the hadith of Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them): 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams, nor a sa' for two sa's' general in what it contains and what adjoins it. Al-Shafi'i rejected that and said: There is no generality for metaphor because it is a necessity, resorted to for broadening speech. This is false, because metaphor exists in the Book of Allah, and Allah Almighty is exalted above incapacity and necessities. The ruling of reality: that from the word used in its original meaning is intended everything it encompasses, whether the word is general or specific. And therefore: the flow of generality in metaphor is like reality. 'General': meaning because generality runs in metaphor, we made the word 'sa' occurring in this hadith general. 'In what it contains and what adjoins it': because what is meant by the word 'sa' here is not its literal meaning by consensus; for there is no disagreement about the permissibility of selling the actual sa' made of wood for two sa's. Thus it is known that what is meant by it is its metaphorical meaning, which is what it contains. So the meaning becomes: Do not sell what a sa' contains for what two sa's contain. The word 'sa' is with the definite article 'al-' for generality, covering food and other things. So its generality indicates that riba (usury) runs both in food like wheat and in non-food like gypsum and lime. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) refused: i.e., the generality. So what is meant by 'what the sa' contains' is food. Because he said: The original principle in speech is that it not be used in other than its original meaning, because it leads to ambiguity and confusion. 'Expansion of speech': The conclusion is that metaphor is a necessity, and what is necessary is established to the extent of necessity, which is removed by intending food. What necessity is there to intend generality? This statement of al-Shafi'i that metaphor is necessary is false: because metaphor exists in the Book of Allah, which is at the highest level of eloquence and rhetoric, and it is the speech of Allah Almighty. It would necessarily follow that Allah Almighty only used metaphor in His Book out of incapacity and necessity that no other literal word existed for it. 'Incapacity and necessities': This is on the assumption that what is meant by necessity is the necessity of the speaker, so there is no issue. As for if what is meant is necessity with respect to the addressee, and the discourse is established thus: metaphor is only considered by the addressee out of necessity due to the impossibility of the literal meaning, and this necessity is removed by carrying it to a meaning, and generality is an additional matter, so it is not resorted to. Then the answer of the text does not hold. So the answer is that generality is a literal meaning because it is established by its evidence; for the word indicates generality only by being with the definite article 'al-' for example, and it is coined for generality of its denotation. So in this respect it is reality, even if in the respect that what is meant by it is other than what it was coined for, it is metaphor. * And know that the author of al-Talwih objected and said: There is no disagreement about this from any of al-Shafi'i's followers, and we did not find it in their books. And that refers to the relation of adjacency.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh discussion on the ruling of majaz (metaphorical usage) and whether it can be general (amm) like haqiqah (literal usage). The author begins by drawing an analogy between the dependence of an incomplete sentence on a complete one in Arabic syntax and the one-sided connection between cause and effect. He uses the example of 'Hind is divorced and Zaynab' to illustrate how the first part depends on the second for the second's validity, not because the first is incomplete. Then he moves to the core issue: the ruling of majaz. He states that majaz, like haqiqah, can be specific or general depending on the word used. The author clarifies that when a word used metaphorically is general (like 'sa' meaning a measure), the metaphorical meaning can also be general, encompassing all individuals of a particular relation (e.g., all that the measure contains). He contrasts this with the view of some Shafi'i scholars who denied the generality of majaz, arguing that majaz is a necessity resorted to for broadening speech and should be limited to the minimum needed. The author refutes this by citing the Qur'an, which contains majaz, and argues that Allah is beyond necessity. The author further explains that generality in majaz is actually a literal meaning when considering the word's form (like the definite article 'al-' for generality), even though the intended meaning is metaphorical. He concludes by noting an objection from the author of al-Talwih (Taftazani) that the attributed disagreement with Shafi'i's followers is not found in their books, pointing to the relation of adjacency as the basis for the metaphorical transfer.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): A word used in other than its original meaning due to a relation (علاقة)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): A word used in its original meaning for which it was coined
عموم المجاز (ʿumūm al-majāz): The generality of the metaphorical meaning, meaning it encompasses all individuals of a single type of relation
علاقة المجاورة (ʿilāqat al-mujāwarah): The relation of adjacency, used as a basis for metaphorical transfer (e.g., using 'sa'' for its contents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the word 'sa' in the hadith 'Do not sell a sa' for two sa's' indicate generality?
A) Because it is a metaphor for its contents and the definite article indicates generality.
B) Because the word 'sa' is always general in its literal meaning.
C) Because the hadith explicitly says so.
D) Because Imam al-Shafi'i agreed on that.

Key Takeaway
Majaz can convey generality just like haqiqah, because the grammatical form (e.g., definite article) functions literally even in metaphorical usage; this principle expands the scope of rulings, such as including non-food items in riba prohibitions.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Generality of Metaphorical Speech in Usul al-Fiqh
Proving that metaphor carries generality like literal speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the generality of a word is determined by its linguistic form, not its literal or metaphorical use, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
Generality is a real attribute of the word's form, so even metaphor carries it.

When a word is used metaphorically, does it retain its general scope? Al-Shafi'i argued that metaphor is a necessity and must be limited to the minimum. The Hanafi school, however, insists that generality stems from the linguistic form (e.g., the definite article), not from whether the word is literal or metaphorical. This lesson unpacks the proof from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and the broader implication for Quranic usage.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the generality of metaphor
The author affirms that metaphor carries generality like literal speech. The ruling for literal truth (haqiqah) is that a word covers all its referents, and metaphor shares this property.
2
Identify the Shafi'i counterargument
Al-Shafi'i argued that metaphor is a necessity (darurah) resorted to only when needed, thus should be limited to the minimum. The metaphorical 'sa'' should only mean food.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using the Quran
The author refutes by pointing out that metaphor abounds in the Quran, which is the highest eloquence. If metaphor were a deficiency, Allah would be speaking from need, which is impossible.
This is a qiyas al-awlawi (argumentum a fortiori).
4
Examine the proof from the hadith's linguistic form
The word 'sa'' is defined by the definite article (al-istighraq), indicating generality. This generality remains whether the word is used literally or metaphorically.
The indefinite article also can indicate generality, but the definite article is explicit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The Hanafi ruling extends riba to all measured items, including non-food like gypsum. The Shafi'i ruling restricts riba to food only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى كما هو [؟] الوارد القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم حكم الحقيقة، ولهذا جعلنا لفظ "الصاع" في حديث ابن عمرهما: "لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين عاماً فيما يحلّه ويجاوره، وأبى الشافعي ذلك، وقال: لا عموم للمجاز؛ لأنه ضروري، يصار إليه توسعة للكلام، وهذا باطل؛ لأن المجاز موجود في كتاب الله والله تعالى يتعالى عن العجز والضرورات. حكم الحقيقة: بأنه يراد من اللفظ المستعمل في ما وضع له جميع ما يتناوله عاماً كان اللفظ أو خاصاً. ولهذا: أي الجريان العموم في المجاز مثل الحقيقة. عاماً : يعني لأجل أن العموم يجري في المجاز جعلنا لفظ الصاع الوارد في هذا الحديث عاماً. فيما يحلّه ويجاوره: وذلك لأن المراد من لفظ الصاع هنا ليس معناه الحقيقي بالإجماع؛ إذ لا خلاف لأحد في جواز بيع نفس الصاع الذي يكون من الخشب بالصاعين فعلم أن المراد به: معناه المجازي وهو ما يحل فيه فصار المعنى لا العموم، فالمراد بما يحل في الصاع الطعام. لأنه إلخ لأن الأصل في الكلام أن لا يستعمل في غير ما وضع له؛ لأنه يؤدي الإبهام والاختلال. توسعةً للكلام: فالحاصل أن المجاز ضروري، والضروري يثبت بقدر الضرورة تدفع بإرادة الطعام، فأية ضرورة إلى أن يراد به العموم. وهذا أي قول الشافعي بأن المجاز ضروري. باطل: لأن المجاز موجود في كتاب الله الذي هو في أعلى رتب الفصاحة والبلاغة، وهو كلام الله تعالى، فلزم أن الله تعالى إنما استعمل المجاز في كتابه عجزاً وضرورة أنه لم يوجد لفظاً آخر حقيقياً فيه. العجز والضرورات هذا على تقدير أن يراد بالضرورة ضرورة المتكلم فلا غبار عليه، وأما إذا يراد الضرورة بالنظر إلى المخاطب وقرّر الكلام :هكذا إن المجاز إنما يعتبره المخاطب ضرورة عدم صحة الحقيقة، وهذه الضرورة تدفع بحمله على معنى، والعموم أمر زائد فلا يصار إليه، فحينئذ لايستقيم جواب المتن، فالجواب أن العموم معنى حقيقي؛ لأنه وقال: لا خلاف فيه لأحد من أصحاب الشافعي، ولم نجده في كتبهم. وذلك إشارة إلى أن العلاقة المجاورة.
The Book of Allah Almighty as it is... The third section on the modes of using phrasing: The ruling of literal truth, and for this we made the word 'sa'' in the hadith of Ibn 'Umar: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa'' for two sa''s' general in what it contains and its surrounding. Al-Shafi'i refused the generality, meaning what is meant by 'what the sa'' contains' is food. Because etc. because the principle is that speech is not used in other than its original meaning, as it leads to ambiguity and confusion. 'Expansion of speech': the conclusion is that metaphor is necessary, and the necessary is established only to the extent of necessity; it is repelled by intending food. So what necessity is there to intend generality? This is the saying of al-Shafi'i that metaphor is necessary. 'False': because metaphor exists in the Book of Allah, which is at the highest rank of eloquence and rhetoric, and it is the speech of Allah Almighty. So it necessarily follows that Allah used metaphor in His Book due to incapacity and necessity, that no other literal word existed for it. 'Incapacity and necessities': this is on the assumption that 'necessity' means necessity of the speaker, then there is no objection. But if necessity is meant with respect to the addressee, and the speech is interpreted thus: that metaphor is considered by the addressee out of necessity due to the impossibility of the literal meaning, this necessity is repelled by carrying it to one meaning, and generality is an extra matter so it is not resorted to. Then the response of the text does not stand. So the answer is that generality is a real meaning because it is established by its own evidence: the word indicates generality only by being defined by the article etc., and it is placed for the generality of its signification, so in this respect it is literal, although in respect that something other than its original meaning is intended it is metaphorical. * And know that the author of al-Talwih objected and said: There is no disagreement about this among any of al-Shafi'i's followers, and we have not found it in their books. That refers to the relation of adjacency.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the generality of metaphorical speech. The author begins by stating the ruling of literal truth (haqiqah): a word used in its original meaning is taken to cover all its referents, whether general or specific. Then he argues that metaphor (majaz) also carries generality, using the hadith about selling sa' for sa' as an example. The word 'sa'' is used metaphorically to mean 'what the sa'' contains', not the physical container. The Hanafi position is that this metaphorical usage includes all types of content, both food and non-food items, so that riba applies to both. Al-Shafi'i disagreed, claiming metaphor is only a necessity and should be restricted to the minimum needed (i.e., only food). The author refutes this by pointing out that metaphor is abundant in the Quran, which is perfect speech, so it cannot be a sign of incapacity. He further argues that the generality of a word is a real (haqiqi) attribute derived from its form (e.g., being defined by al-), not from the intended meaning. Therefore, even if the word is used metaphorically, its generality remains as a literal feature. The passage ends with a note that al-Taftazani (author of al-Talwih) claimed this disagreement is not found among Shafi'i scholars.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): A word used in its original meaning as placed by language.
المجاز (al-majāz): A word used in other than its original meaning due to a relation (علاقة) and a contextual clue.
العموم (al-ʿumūm): The inclusion of all individuals covered by a word's meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) By consensus of the scholars

Key Takeaway
Generality is a real attribute of the word's linguistic form; hence metaphor carries generality like literal speech.

Applying Haqiqah and Majaz to the Will of an Arab without Wala'
Analyzing the application of haqiqah and majaz in a wasiyya case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the interpretation of the ambiguous word 'mawla' in a will using the Hanafi principle that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz, and determine the ruling on the bequest's distribution.

Core Idea
When context eliminates the figurative meaning, only the literal meaning remains, making the will valid.

This lesson examines a textual analysis from a Hanafi usul work. An Arab with no wala' over him bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his 'mawali.' The word 'mawla' is ambiguous, but by analyzing the testator's status, we can identify that only the literal meaning (manumitter) is intended. The passage demonstrates how the principle against combining haqiqah and majaz (literal and figurative) is applied, and what happens when the bequest is insufficient to cover all intended recipients.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
The word 'mawla' (مولى) is a homonym that can mean either the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the freed slave (mu'taq). Determine which meaning is intended in the context.
Hanafi usul treats this as a case of ishtirak (homonymy).
2
Analyze the testator's status
The testator is an Arab with no wala' over him (لا ولاء عليه). This means he cannot have a freed slave (mu'taq) because Arabs are not typically enslaved and wala' of manumission is not established over them.
The condition of 'no wala' over him' is crucial to exclude the figurative meaning.
3
Apply the haqiqah-majaz principle
Since the figurative meaning (freed slave) is impossible in this context, only the literal meaning (manumitter) remains. Therefore, the word must be interpreted as the manumitter(s).
Hanafi principle: al-haqiqah tuqaddam 'ala al-majaz (literal takes precedence over figurative).
4
Derive the distribution ruling
If the testator has one manumitter, that manumitter receives half of the bequest (the other half returns to heirs because the minimum of 'mawali' is two). If multiple manumitters exist, they share the entire bequest.
The word 'mawali' is plural; in bequests, two is treated as a plurality (اعتبار الوصية بالميراث).
5
Confirm excluding the figurative extension
The bequest's remainder cannot go to the mawali of the mawla (i.e., the manumitter's own freed slaves) because that would require using 'mawla' figuratively, which is prohibited when the literal meaning is intended.
Combining haqiqah and majaz in one usage is invalid (باطل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
لو أن عربياً - لا ولاء عليه - أوصى بثلث ماله لمواليه وله معتق واحد فاستحق النصف، كان الباقي مردودا إلى الورثة، ولا يكون لموالي مولاه؛ لأن الحقيقة أريدت بهذا اللفظ، فبطل المجاز، وإنما عمّهم الأمان فيما إذا استأمنوا على أبنائهم ومواليهم؛ انتصارهم أهل الحرب
لو أن عربيا إلخ تأكيد؛ لأن ولاء العتاقة لا يثبت على العربي ولا ولاء الموالات، أما الأول فلأن من شرطه الرق، والعرب لاتسترق إذ الحكم فيهم إما الإسلام أو السيف لغلظة كفرهم - كما في المرتد – فلا يثبت عليه ولاء العتاقة، وأما الثاني فلأن من شرطه كون المولى الأسفل من غير العرب؛ إذ الحاجة إليه للانتصار، والعرب بالقبائل، ويمكن أن يقال: إنه لما كان يثبت الولاء على العرب بطريق الندرة بأن يتزوج العرب أمة الغير فتلد منه ولداً فيعتقه مولاه، فيكون هو عربياً عليه ولاء، فقيد بقوله هذا، وقيل: احتراز عن أهل الكتاب من العرب، فإن استرقاقهم جائز، فيثبت عليهم الولاء لمواليه: أي لمن أعتقهم هو ؛ إذ من هو عربي ولا ولاء عليه لا يكون له المولى المعتق بالكسر ، وهذا هو المقصود من تقيّده بالعربي وعدم الولاء عليه فحينئذ لا اشتراك في لفظ المولى فلا محالة يتعين المعتق بالفتح، فصح وصيته وأما لو لم يكن هو عربيا وكان الولاء عليه وقال: "أوصيت لموالي بثلث ماله" لبطلت الوصية إن لم يبين في حياته، وذلك لأن المولى يطلق على المعتق بالفتح والكسر بالاشتراك، فلا يمكن القول بعمومه ولا يمكن التعين، فبقي الموصى له مجهولا، وأما على هذه الصورة المفروضة في المتن فلا اشتراك؛ لأن احتمال المعتق بالكسر حينئذ منتف.
معتق واحد قيد به؛ لأنه لو كان له معتقان أو أكثر ، فكان جميع الثلث لهما، ولا يُردَّ إلى الورثة؛ لأن الاثنين في الوصايا بمنزلة الجماعة اعتبارًا للوصية بالميراث وفي الميراث للاثنين حكم الجماعة. كان جواب "لو". مردودا إلخ: لأنه أوصى الجماعة الموالي، وأقلها اثنان فيكون لكل واحد نصف الوصية، وإذا كان المولى واحداً استحق النصف، والباقي ميراث الحقيقة أريدت بهذا والمعنى الحقيقي للفظ المولى هو المعتق وإن كان واحدا، وأما موالي الموالي فمعنى مجازي له، وإذا تعين المعنى الحقيقي فلو أريد المعنى المجازى أيضاً يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، وذا باطل. فبطل المجاز: لهذا، والحاصل أن رجلاً لا ولاءً عليه لأحد لو أوصى لمواليه بثلث ماله، فحينئذ تعين المعتق بالفتح وصحت الوصية، فإن كان له معتقان أو أكثر يثبت لهم جميع الثلث، وإن كان المعتق واحداً يثبت له نصف الثلث، والباقي لورثة الميت ولا يعطى ذلك الباقي لموالي مواليه؛ لأن صدق لفظ المولى عليهم بطريق المجاز، وعلى مولاه بطريق الحقيقة فيلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز.
وإنما عمّهم الأمان: جواب سؤال مقدّر تقريره: إنكم قد جمعتم بين الحقيقة والمجاز فيما إذا استأمنوا أي أهل الحرب على أبنائهم ومواليهم بأن قالوا: "آمنونا على أبنائنا وموالينا" حيث يدخل في الأمن أبناء الأبناء.
لأن اسم الأبناء والموالي ظاهراً يتناول الفروع، لكن بطل العمل به؛ لتقدم الحقيقة أي عرفاً بقي مجرد الاسم شبهة في حقن الدم، وصار كالإشارة إذا دعا بها الكافر إلى نفسه يثبت بها الأمان لصورة المسالمة وإن لم تكن ذلك حقيقةً، وإنما ترك في الاستيمان أي الإشارة هذه الإشارة على الآباء والأمهات اعتباراً لصورة في الأجداد والجدات؛ لأن اعتبار الصورة لثبوت الحكم في محلّ آخر إنما يكون بطريق التبعية، لشيء مذكور في ضمن الأبناء، وموالي الموالي في ضمن الموالي، مع أن أبناء الأبناء مجاز في لفظ الأبناء، وموالي الموالي مجاز في لفظ الموالي، فلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فأجاب بقوله: وإنما عمّهم الأمان الفروع: أيضاً أي أبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي، فإن بني الابن ينسبون إلى الجد بالبنوة مجازاً، كما يقال: بنو هاشم، وقال تعالى: ﴿يَا بَنِي آدَم (الأعراف: ٢٦)، وكذا لفظ الموالي يطلق عرفاً على موالي الموالي، كما يقال لمعتق معتق الرجل: مولى الرجل. العمل به أي بهذا التناول الظاهري لتقدم الحقيقة على المجاز في الإرادة، فلا يثبت لهم الأمان باعتبار تناول الاسم إياهم، وكونهم مرادين بالذات؛ لئلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. بقي مجرد الاسم إلخ: أي لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم يدخلون في الأمان بلا إرادة؛ إذ الأمان يثبت بالشبهة أيضاً، وحاصل الجواب : أنا لا نقول: إنهم يثبت لهم الأمان من حيث إنهم حيث إنهم أيضاً مرادون من اللفظ بالذات؛ ليلزم الجمع، بل لما كان اللفظ في العرف يتناولهم أيضاً مجازاً وتركنا المعنى المجازي لتقدم المعنى الحقيقي، فبقي في الاسم شبهة التناول بعده، فلأجل الاحتياط أثبتنا لهم الأمان؛ إذ في حقن الدماء يكفي الشبهة أيضاً. وصار ثبوت الأمان لهم لشبهة تناول الاسم بعد ترك معناه المجازي كالإشارة أي كثبوته بالإشارة. إلى نفسه بأن يشير إليه باليد: "أنزل إن كنت شجيعاً، فظن أنها أمان لصورة المسالمة: أي لأجل شبهة الأمان. ذلك أي المسالمة، والمراد به الأمان. حقيقة يعني في الإشارة يثبت الأمان لشبهة الأمان وإن لم تكن هذه الإشارة أمانا حقيقةً، فقوله: حقيقة" منصوب على التمييز، ولما كان يرد على هذا الجواب أنه ينبغي أن يعتبر مثل هذه الشبهة لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم فيما إذا استأمن على الآباء والأمهات أيضاً؛ لأن لفظ الآباء والأمهات أيضاً يتناول بظاهر الاسم للأجداد والجدات، فإذا تركتم المعنى المجازي هو الأجداد والجدات في الإرادة خوفاً عن الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فكان شبهة تناول الاسم لهم أيضاً باقية بعده، كما كان في الأبناء والموالي، فيثبت لهم الأمان أيضاً، والحال أنكم لا تثبتون لهم الأمان، فما الفرق؟ فأجاب بقوله : وإنما ترك إلخ يعني إنما ترك اعتبار الصورة أي الشبهة في الأجداد والجدات فيما إذا استأمن على الآباء والأمهات.
The third section on the modes of using word structures. If an Arab – with no wala' over him – bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his mawali and he has one freed slave, then he (the freed slave) takes half [of the bequest], and the remainder is returned to the heirs, and it does not go to the mawali of his mawla; because the literal meaning (haqiqah) was intended by this word, so the figurative meaning (majaz) is invalid. And [the case of] aman includes them [grandchildren and mawali of mawali] when they [the warring people] seek aman for their children and mawali; [this is] due to [the principle of] support [for protecting blood]. The phrase 'if an Arab, etc.' is a confirmation; because the wala' of manumission is not established over an Arab, nor is the wala' of clientage. As for the first, because its condition is slavery, and Arabs are not enslaved, since the rule regarding them is either Islam or the sword due to the severity of their disbelief – like the apostate – so wala' of manumission is not established over him. As for the second, because its condition is that the lower patron be from other than Arabs, since the need for it is for support, and Arabs [have support] through tribes. And it can be said: Since wala' is established over Arabs by way of rarity – such as an Arab marrying the slave woman of another who bears him a child, then his master manumits that child, so he becomes an Arab with wala' over him – so he restricted [the ruling] with this statement. And it is said: [This is a] precaution regarding the People of the Book from among the Arabs, for their enslavement is permissible, so wala' is established over them. [The phrase] 'for his mawali' means for those whom he manumitted; since whoever is an Arab and has no wala' over him does not have a freed slave (with kasr), and this is the purpose of restricting it to an Arab and no wala' over him. Then there is no ambiguity in the word mawla, so inevitably the manumitter (with fath) is determined, and his will is valid. But if he was not an Arab and had wala' over him and said, 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to my mawali,' the will would be invalid unless he clarifies during his lifetime, because mawla is used for both the manumitter (with fath) and the freed slave (with kasr) as a homonym, so it cannot be said to be general nor can it be determined, so the legatee remains unknown. But in this supposed scenario in the text, there is no homonymy, because the possibility of the freed slave (with kasr) is negated. 'One freed slave' is restricted [in the example]; because if he had two or more freed slaves, then the entire one-third belongs to them, and nothing is returned to the heirs, because two in bequests are like a group, analogizing bequest to inheritance, and in inheritance two have the status of a group. The answer to 'if' [is] 'is returned, etc.': because he bequeathed to a group of mawali, and the minimum of a group is two, so each gets half of the bequest. And when there is one freed slave, he takes half, and the remainder is inheritance. The literal meaning intended by this: the true meaning of the word mawla is the manumitter, even if one. As for the mawali of the mawla, it is a figurative meaning for it. And when the true meaning is determined, if the figurative meaning were also intended, it would necessitate combining the literal and figurative, and that is invalid. So the figurative is invalid for this reason. The summary: a man who has no wala' over anyone bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his mawali; then the manumitter (with fath) is determined and the will is valid. If he has two or more freed slaves, the entire one-third is for them; if he has one freed slave, he gets half of the one-third, and the remainder goes to the heirs of the deceased, and that remainder is not given to the mawali of his mawla, because the application of the word mawla to them is figurative, and to his mawla is literal, so combining literal and figurative would be necessary. 'And [the case of] aman includes them' is an answer to an implied question: that you have combined literal and figurative in the case when they – i.e., the warring people – seek aman for their children and mawali by saying, 'Grant us aman for our children and our mawali,' where the children's children are included in the aman. Because the word 'children' and 'mawali' apparently includes descendants, but acting on it is invalid because the literal meaning (i.e., in customary usage) takes precedence; only the mere word remains as a shubha for protecting blood, and it becomes like a sign: if a disbeliever calls [someone] to himself by it, aman is established for the form of peacemaking even if that is not literal. And [the reason] that they did not consider the sign (i.e., this sign) for fathers and mothers, considering the form in grandparents, is because considering the form for establishing a ruling in another place is only by way of subordination to something mentioned, i.e., grandchildren are included within 'children', and mawali of mawali within 'mawali', even though grandchildren are figurative in the word 'children', and mawali of mawali are figurative in the word 'mawali', so combining literal and figurative would be necessary. So he answered by saying: 'And aman includes them' – i.e., the branches also, i.e., grandchildren and mawali of mawali. For the children's children are attributed to the grandfather by paternity figuratively, as it is said: Banu Hashim, and Allah said: 'O Children of Adam' (al-A'raf: 26). Similarly, the word 'mawali' is customarily applied to the mawali of mawali, as it is said for the manumitter of a man's freed slave: mawla of the man. 'Acting on it' means this apparent application, because the literal takes precedence over the figurative in intention, so aman is not established for them by virtue of the word including them and their being intended primarily, to avoid combining literal and figurative. 'Only the word remains, etc.' means: for the sake of caution in preserving blood, they are included in aman without intention, because aman is also established by shubha. The summary of the answer: we do not say that aman is established for them because they are also intended by the word primarily, which would necessitate combining; rather, because the word in custom also applies to them figuratively, and we abandon the figurative meaning due to the precedence of the literal meaning, a shubha of inclusion remains in the word afterward, so for caution we establish aman for them, for in preserving blood, shubha suffices. And the establishment of aman for them due to the shubha of the word's inclusion after abandoning its figurative meaning is like [aman established by] a sign: i.e., like its establishment by a sign, such that he [the disbeliever] points to himself with his hand [saying], 'Descend if you are brave,' and he thinks it is aman for the form of peacemaking, i.e., due to the shubha of aman. 'That' means peacemaking, and what is meant is aman. 'Literal' means that by the sign aman is established due to the shubha of aman even if this sign is not actually aman; so his word 'literal' is in the accusative as a specification. And since this answer is objected to: that such shubha should be considered for caution in preserving blood in the case when aman is sought for fathers and mothers as well, because the word 'fathers' and 'mothers' also apparently includes grandparents; so if you abandon the figurative meaning (i.e., grandparents) in intention for fear of combining literal and figurative, then a shubha of the word's inclusion would also remain for them afterward, as in the case of children and mawali, so aman would be established for them as well, whereas you do not establish aman for them – what is the difference? So he answered by saying: 'And they did not consider, etc.' meaning: they did not consider the form (i.e., the shubha) in grandparents when aman is sought for fathers and mothers.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work, applying the principles of haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (figurative meaning) to two legal scenarios. 

First, the case of a will (wasiyya): An Arab with no wala' over him bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his 'mawali.' The word 'mawla' is ambiguous; it can mean the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the freed slave (mu'taq). The author explains that for this Arab, only the literal meaning (the manumitter) is possible because Arabs are not typically enslaved, so the figurative meaning (freed slave) is excluded. Thus the will is valid, and the bequest goes to the manumitter(s). If there is one manumitter, he gets half of the one-third; if more, they share the whole third. The remainder of the bequest goes to the heirs, not to the mawali of the mawla, because that would involve using the word figuratively, which is invalid when the literal meaning is intended. 

Second, the case of aman (safe conduct): When warring people seek aman for their 'children' and 'mawali,' the aman includes grandchildren and mawali of mawali, even though these are figurative extensions of the words. Why does this not violate the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz? The author explains that in the aman case, the literal meaning is the primary intention, but because the words customarily also extend to these categories figuratively, and because preserving blood is so important, a mere shubha (doubt) from the word's possible inclusion suffices to grant them aman. This is like a sign (ishara): if a disbeliever makes a gesture that appears to grant aman, it is valid as aman due to shubha, even if it is not literally aman. 

However, this reasoning does not extend to the case of seeking aman for 'fathers' and 'mothers' – those words do not customarily extend to grandparents in the same way as 'children' extends to grandchildren. Therefore, the shubha is weaker, and grandparents are not included. 

The passage thus illustrates the nuanced Hanafi application of haqiqah and majaz, and the special role of shubha in matters of blood protection.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal meaning of a word that is primarily intended, as opposed to figurative (majaz).
المجاز (al-majaz): The figurative meaning of a word, which is not intended when the literal meaning is possible.
الولاء (al-wala'): Legal relationship of patronage (clientship) that arises from manumission or contract, establishing inheritance rights.
المولى (al-mawla): A homonym meaning either the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the freed slave (mu'taq).
الأمان (al-aman): Safe conduct or guarantee of protection granted to non-Muslims.
الشبهة (al-shubha): A doubt or uncertainty about the application of a word or ruling, which can be sufficient to establish a legal effect in certain contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the wasiyya case, why does the bequest's remainder (half of the one-third) not go to the mawali of the mawla (the manumitter's own freed slaves)?
A) Because the bequest is invalid and all property goes to the heirs.
B) Because the word 'mawla' is figurative for them and combining literal and figurative is prohibited.
C) Because the manumitter's freed slaves are not included in the bequest by default.
D) Because the testator explicitly excluded them.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz, and the prohibition against combining them, is applied to resolve homonymy in wills, where context can eliminate one meaning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Haqiqah, Majaz, and Shubha in the Granting of Aman (Safe Conduct)
Analyzing haqiqah, majaz, and shubha in the aman case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the application of haqiqah and majaz in the context of aman, and explain the role of shubha (doubt) in extending protection to figurative extensions of words.

Core Idea
In preserving blood, a shubha from a word's possible figurative extension is sufficient to grant aman, even when the literal meaning takes precedence.

This lesson continues the textual analysis from the same Hanafi usul passage. The author addresses an apparent contradiction: why does the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz not prevent including grandchildren and mawali of mawali in the aman grant? The answer lies in the concept of shubha (doubt) and the special status of blood protection. We will walk through the reasoning and the subtle distinction from the will case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Non-Muslims seek aman for their 'children' and 'mawali.' These words literally refer to direct children and primary mawali, but figuratively extend to grandchildren and mawali of mawali.
This is the same homonymy issue, but applied to a different legal context.
2
Recognize the apparent conflict
The literal meaning takes precedence, so the figurative extension should be excluded. However, in the aman case, the grandchildren and mawali of mawali are included.
Why the difference from the wasiyya case?
3
Apply the shubha principle
Although the literal meaning is primarily intended, the mere possibility (shubha) that the words could include these categories is enough to extend aman for protecting blood.
Shubha is sufficient in matters of blood, not in property.
4
Distinguish from the wasiyya case
In the will, property rights require certainty; shubha is insufficient. In aman, blood protection is paramount, so shubha suffices.
This is a key contextual difference.
5
Compare with the case of 'fathers' and 'mothers'
The words 'fathers' and 'mothers' do not customarily extend to grandparents, so the shubha is absent and they are not included.
The strength of the customary extension matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
لو أن عربياً - لا ولاء عليه - أوصى بثلث ماله لمواليه وله معتق واحد فاستحق النصف، كان الباقي مردودا إلى الورثة، ولا يكون لموالي مولاه؛ لأن الحقيقة أريدت بهذا اللفظ، فبطل المجاز، وإنما عمّهم الأمان فيما إذا استأمنوا على أبنائهم ومواليهم؛ انتصارهم أهل الحرب
لو أن عربيا إلخ تأكيد؛ لأن ولاء العتاقة لا يثبت على العربي ولا ولاء الموالات، أما الأول فلأن من شرطه الرق، والعرب لاتسترق إذ الحكم فيهم إما الإسلام أو السيف لغلظة كفرهم - كما في المرتد – فلا يثبت عليه ولاء العتاقة، وأما الثاني فلأن من شرطه كون المولى الأسفل من غير العرب؛ إذ الحاجة إليه للانتصار، والعرب بالقبائل، ويمكن أن يقال: إنه لما كان يثبت الولاء على العرب بطريق الندرة بأن يتزوج العرب أمة الغير فتلد منه ولداً فيعتقه مولاه، فيكون هو عربياً عليه ولاء، فقيد بقوله هذا، وقيل: احتراز عن أهل الكتاب من العرب، فإن استرقاقهم جائز، فيثبت عليهم الولاء لمواليه: أي لمن أعتقهم هو ؛ إذ من هو عربي ولا ولاء عليه لا يكون له المولى المعتق بالكسر ، وهذا هو المقصود من تقيّده بالعربي وعدم الولاء عليه فحينئذ لا اشتراك في لفظ المولى فلا محالة يتعين المعتق بالفتح، فصح وصيته وأما لو لم يكن هو عربيا وكان الولاء عليه وقال: "أوصيت لموالي بثلث ماله" لبطلت الوصية إن لم يبين في حياته، وذلك لأن المولى يطلق على المعتق بالفتح والكسر بالاشتراك، فلا يمكن القول بعمومه ولا يمكن التعين، فبقي الموصى له مجهولا، وأما على هذه الصورة المفروضة في المتن فلا اشتراك؛ لأن احتمال المعتق بالكسر حينئذ منتف.
معتق واحد قيد به؛ لأنه لو كان له معتقان أو أكثر ، فكان جميع الثلث لهما، ولا يُردَّ إلى الورثة؛ لأن الاثنين في الوصايا بمنزلة الجماعة اعتبارًا للوصية بالميراث وفي الميراث للاثنين حكم الجماعة. كان جواب "لو". مردودا إلخ: لأنه أوصى الجماعة الموالي، وأقلها اثنان فيكون لكل واحد نصف الوصية، وإذا كان المولى واحداً استحق النصف، والباقي ميراث الحقيقة أريدت بهذا والمعنى الحقيقي للفظ المولى هو المعتق وإن كان واحدا، وأما موالي الموالي فمعنى مجازي له، وإذا تعين المعنى الحقيقي فلو أريد المعنى المجازى أيضاً يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، وذا باطل. فبطل المجاز: لهذا، والحاصل أن رجلاً لا ولاءً عليه لأحد لو أوصى لمواليه بثلث ماله، فحينئذ تعين المعتق بالفتح وصحت الوصية، فإن كان له معتقان أو أكثر يثبت لهم جميع الثلث، وإن كان المعتق واحداً يثبت له نصف الثلث، والباقي لورثة الميت ولا يعطى ذلك الباقي لموالي مواليه؛ لأن صدق لفظ المولى عليهم بطريق المجاز، وعلى مولاه بطريق الحقيقة فيلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز.
وإنما عمّهم الأمان: جواب سؤال مقدّر تقريره: إنكم قد جمعتم بين الحقيقة والمجاز فيما إذا استأمنوا أي أهل الحرب على أبنائهم ومواليهم بأن قالوا: "آمنونا على أبنائنا وموالينا" حيث يدخل في الأمن أبناء الأبناء.
لأن اسم الأبناء والموالي ظاهراً يتناول الفروع، لكن بطل العمل به؛ لتقدم الحقيقة أي عرفاً بقي مجرد الاسم شبهة في حقن الدم، وصار كالإشارة إذا دعا بها الكافر إلى نفسه يثبت بها الأمان لصورة المسالمة وإن لم تكن ذلك حقيقةً، وإنما ترك في الاستيمان أي الإشارة هذه الإشارة على الآباء والأمهات اعتباراً لصورة في الأجداد والجدات؛ لأن اعتبار الصورة لثبوت الحكم في محلّ آخر إنما يكون بطريق التبعية، لشيء مذكور في ضمن الأبناء، وموالي الموالي في ضمن الموالي، مع أن أبناء الأبناء مجاز في لفظ الأبناء، وموالي الموالي مجاز في لفظ الموالي، فلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فأجاب بقوله: وإنما عمّهم الأمان الفروع: أيضاً أي أبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي، فإن بني الابن ينسبون إلى الجد بالبنوة مجازاً، كما يقال: بنو هاشم، وقال تعالى: ﴿يَا بَنِي آدَم (الأعراف: ٢٦)، وكذا لفظ الموالي يطلق عرفاً على موالي الموالي، كما يقال لمعتق معتق الرجل: مولى الرجل. العمل به أي بهذا التناول الظاهري لتقدم الحقيقة على المجاز في الإرادة، فلا يثبت لهم الأمان باعتبار تناول الاسم إياهم، وكونهم مرادين بالذات؛ لئلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. بقي مجرد الاسم إلخ: أي لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم يدخلون في الأمان بلا إرادة؛ إذ الأمان يثبت بالشبهة أيضاً، وحاصل الجواب : أنا لا نقول: إنهم يثبت لهم الأمان من حيث إنهم حيث إنهم أيضاً مرادون من اللفظ بالذات؛ ليلزم الجمع، بل لما كان اللفظ في العرف يتناولهم أيضاً مجازاً وتركنا المعنى المجازي لتقدم المعنى الحقيقي، فبقي في الاسم شبهة التناول بعده، فلأجل الاحتياط أثبتنا لهم الأمان؛ إذ في حقن الدماء يكفي الشبهة أيضاً. وصار ثبوت الأمان لهم لشبهة تناول الاسم بعد ترك معناه المجازي كالإشارة أي كثبوته بالإشارة. إلى نفسه بأن يشير إليه باليد: "أنزل إن كنت شجيعاً، فظن أنها أمان لصورة المسالمة: أي لأجل شبهة الأمان. ذلك أي المسالمة، والمراد به الأمان. حقيقة يعني في الإشارة يثبت الأمان لشبهة الأمان وإن لم تكن هذه الإشارة أمانا حقيقةً، فقوله: حقيقة" منصوب على التمييز، ولما كان يرد على هذا الجواب أنه ينبغي أن يعتبر مثل هذه الشبهة لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم فيما إذا استأمن على الآباء والأمهات أيضاً؛ لأن لفظ الآباء والأمهات أيضاً يتناول بظاهر الاسم للأجداد والجدات، فإذا تركتم المعنى المجازي هو الأجداد والجدات في الإرادة خوفاً عن الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فكان شبهة تناول الاسم لهم أيضاً باقية بعده، كما كان في الأبناء والموالي، فيثبت لهم الأمان أيضاً، والحال أنكم لا تثبتون لهم الأمان، فما الفرق؟ فأجاب بقوله : وإنما ترك إلخ يعني إنما ترك اعتبار الصورة أي الشبهة في الأجداد والجدات فيما إذا استأمن على الآباء والأمهات.
The third section on the modes of using word structures. If an Arab – with no wala' over him – bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his mawali and he has one freed slave, then he (the freed slave) takes half [of the bequest], and the remainder is returned to the heirs, and it does not go to the mawali of his mawla; because the literal meaning (haqiqah) was intended by this word, so the figurative meaning (majaz) is invalid. And [the case of] aman includes them [grandchildren and mawali of mawali] when they [the warring people] seek aman for their children and mawali; [this is] due to [the principle of] support [for protecting blood]. The phrase 'if an Arab, etc.' is a confirmation; because the wala' of manumission is not established over an Arab, nor is the wala' of clientage. As for the first, because its condition is slavery, and Arabs are not enslaved, since the rule regarding them is either Islam or the sword due to the severity of their disbelief – like the apostate – so wala' of manumission is not established over him. As for the second, because its condition is that the lower patron be from other than Arabs, since the need for it is for support, and Arabs [have support] through tribes. And it can be said: Since wala' is established over Arabs by way of rarity – such as an Arab marrying the slave woman of another who bears him a child, then his master manumits that child, so he becomes an Arab with wala' over him – so he restricted [the ruling] with this statement. And it is said: [This is a] precaution regarding the People of the Book from among the Arabs, for their enslavement is permissible, so wala' is established over them. [The phrase] 'for his mawali' means for those whom he manumitted; since whoever is an Arab and has no wala' over him does not have a freed slave (with kasr), and this is the purpose of restricting it to an Arab and no wala' over him. Then there is no ambiguity in the word mawla, so inevitably the manumitter (with fath) is determined, and his will is valid. But if he was not an Arab and had wala' over him and said, 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to my mawali,' the will would be invalid unless he clarifies during his lifetime, because mawla is used for both the manumitter (with fath) and the freed slave (with kasr) as a homonym, so it cannot be said to be general nor can it be determined, so the legatee remains unknown. But in this supposed scenario in the text, there is no homonymy, because the possibility of the freed slave (with kasr) is negated. 'One freed slave' is restricted [in the example]; because if he had two or more freed slaves, then the entire one-third belongs to them, and nothing is returned to the heirs, because two in bequests are like a group, analogizing bequest to inheritance, and in inheritance two have the status of a group. The answer to 'if' [is] 'is returned, etc.': because he bequeathed to a group of mawali, and the minimum of a group is two, so each gets half of the bequest. And when there is one freed slave, he takes half, and the remainder is inheritance. The literal meaning intended by this: the true meaning of the word mawla is the manumitter, even if one. As for the mawali of the mawla, it is a figurative meaning for it. And when the true meaning is determined, if the figurative meaning were also intended, it would necessitate combining the literal and figurative, and that is invalid. So the figurative is invalid for this reason. The summary: a man who has no wala' over anyone bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his mawali; then the manumitter (with fath) is determined and the will is valid. If he has two or more freed slaves, the entire one-third is for them; if he has one freed slave, he gets half of the one-third, and the remainder goes to the heirs of the deceased, and that remainder is not given to the mawali of his mawla, because the application of the word mawla to them is figurative, and to his mawla is literal, so combining literal and figurative would be necessary. 'And [the case of] aman includes them' is an answer to an implied question: that you have combined literal and figurative in the case when they – i.e., the warring people – seek aman for their children and mawali by saying, 'Grant us aman for our children and our mawali,' where the children's children are included in the aman. Because the word 'children' and 'mawali' apparently includes descendants, but acting on it is invalid because the literal meaning (i.e., in customary usage) takes precedence; only the mere word remains as a shubha for protecting blood, and it becomes like a sign: if a disbeliever calls [someone] to himself by it, aman is established for the form of peacemaking even if that is not literal. And [the reason] that they did not consider the sign (i.e., this sign) for fathers and mothers, considering the form in grandparents, is because considering the form for establishing a ruling in another place is only by way of subordination to something mentioned, i.e., grandchildren are included within 'children', and mawali of mawali within 'mawali', even though grandchildren are figurative in the word 'children', and mawali of mawali are figurative in the word 'mawali', so combining literal and figurative would be necessary. So he answered by saying: 'And aman includes them' – i.e., the branches also, i.e., grandchildren and mawali of mawali. For the children's children are attributed to the grandfather by paternity figuratively, as it is said: Banu Hashim, and Allah said: 'O Children of Adam' (al-A'raf: 26). Similarly, the word 'mawali' is customarily applied to the mawali of mawali, as it is said for the manumitter of a man's freed slave: mawla of the man. 'Acting on it' means this apparent application, because the literal takes precedence over the figurative in intention, so aman is not established for them by virtue of the word including them and their being intended primarily, to avoid combining literal and figurative. 'Only the word remains, etc.' means: for the sake of caution in preserving blood, they are included in aman without intention, because aman is also established by shubha. The summary of the answer: we do not say that aman is established for them because they are also intended by the word primarily, which would necessitate combining; rather, because the word in custom also applies to them figuratively, and we abandon the figurative meaning due to the precedence of the literal meaning, a shubha of inclusion remains in the word afterward, so for caution we establish aman for them, for in preserving blood, shubha suffices. And the establishment of aman for them due to the shubha of the word's inclusion after abandoning its figurative meaning is like [aman established by] a sign: i.e., like its establishment by a sign, such that he [the disbeliever] points to himself with his hand [saying], 'Descend if you are brave,' and he thinks it is aman for the form of peacemaking, i.e., due to the shubha of aman. 'That' means peacemaking, and what is meant is aman. 'Literal' means that by the sign aman is established due to the shubha of aman even if this sign is not actually aman; so his word 'literal' is in the accusative as a specification. And since this answer is objected to: that such shubha should be considered for caution in preserving blood in the case when aman is sought for fathers and mothers as well, because the word 'fathers' and 'mothers' also apparently includes grandparents; so if you abandon the figurative meaning (i.e., grandparents) in intention for fear of combining literal and figurative, then a shubha of the word's inclusion would also remain for them afterward, as in the case of children and mawali, so aman would be established for them as well, whereas you do not establish aman for them – what is the difference? So he answered by saying: 'And they did not consider, etc.' meaning: they did not consider the form (i.e., the shubha) in grandparents when aman is sought for fathers and mothers.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work, applying the principles of haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (figurative meaning) to two legal scenarios. 

First, the case of a will (wasiyya): An Arab with no wala' over him bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his 'mawali.' The word 'mawla' is ambiguous; it can mean the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the freed slave (mu'taq). The author explains that for this Arab, only the literal meaning (the manumitter) is possible because Arabs are not typically enslaved, so the figurative meaning (freed slave) is excluded. Thus the will is valid, and the bequest goes to the manumitter(s). If there is one manumitter, he gets half of the one-third; if more, they share the whole third. The remainder of the bequest goes to the heirs, not to the mawali of the mawla, because that would involve using the word figuratively, which is invalid when the literal meaning is intended. 

Second, the case of aman (safe conduct): When warring people seek aman for their 'children' and 'mawali,' the aman includes grandchildren and mawali of mawali, even though these are figurative extensions of the words. Why does this not violate the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz? The author explains that in the aman case, the literal meaning is the primary intention, but because the words customarily also extend to these categories figuratively, and because preserving blood is so important, a mere shubha (doubt) from the word's possible inclusion suffices to grant them aman. This is like a sign (ishara): if a disbeliever makes a gesture that appears to grant aman, it is valid as aman due to shubha, even if it is not literally aman. 

However, this reasoning does not extend to the case of seeking aman for 'fathers' and 'mothers' – those words do not customarily extend to grandparents in the same way as 'children' extends to grandchildren. Therefore, the shubha is weaker, and grandparents are not included. 

The passage thus illustrates the nuanced Hanafi application of haqiqah and majaz, and the special role of shubha in matters of blood protection.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal meaning of a word that is primarily intended, as opposed to figurative (majaz).
المجاز (al-majaz): The figurative meaning of a word, which is not intended when the literal meaning is possible.
الولاء (al-wala'): Legal relationship of patronage (clientship) that arises from manumission or contract, establishing inheritance rights.
المولى (al-mawla): A homonym meaning either the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the freed slave (mu'taq).
الأمان (al-aman): Safe conduct or guarantee of protection granted to non-Muslims.
الشبهة (al-shubha): A doubt or uncertainty about the application of a word or ruling, which can be sufficient to establish a legal effect in certain contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school include grandchildren in the aman for children but not grandparents in the aman for fathers?
A) Because grandchildren are more closely related than grandparents.
B) Because the word 'children' customarily extends to grandchildren, while 'fathers' does not extend to grandparents in the same way.
C) Because the aman for children is explicitly given by the commander, while for fathers it is not.
D) Because the principle of shubha applies only to descendants, not ascendants.

Key Takeaway
In matters of blood protection, a shubha arising from customary figurative extension can extend the ruling of aman, even when the literal meaning takes precedence.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Not Combining Haqiqah and Majaz in Aman for Descendants
Analysis of including grandchildren in aman using shubha while avoiding combining haqiqah and majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of not combining haqiqah and majaz to identify when shubha suffices to extend a legal ruling, and distinguish between furu' (branches) and usul (roots) in the context of aman.

Core Idea
Aman extends to grandchildren through shubha, not literal meaning, to preserve blood.

In this passage, we explore a nuanced usul al-fiqh problem: when a Muslim grants aman to his 'abna'' (sons), are grandchildren included? The Hanafi answer is yes, but not because the word literally includes them. Instead, the ruling relies on shubha (semblance) and the principle of caution in blood matters. This analysis illustrates how Hanafi usul avoids combining literal and figurative meanings in a single utterance while still achieving a just outcome. You will learn to trace the derivation chain from Quranic evidence to the final ruling, and understand why grandparents are treated differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Core Usul Issue
The passage deals with the inclusion of grandchildren (abna' al-abna') and clients of clients (mawali al-mawali) in aman when the granter uses 'abna'' and 'mawali'. The central usul issue is whether combining literal and figurative meanings in one utterance is permissible. Note the Hanafi position: it is prohibited if the figurative meaning is intended per se.
This is a key principle in Hanafi usul: la yajuz al-jam' bayna al-haqiqah wa al-majaz fi isti'mal wahid.
2
Examine the Quranic Evidence for Figurative Usage
The author cites Surah al-A'raf 7:26 (يَا بَنِي آدَم) and the common phrase 'Banu Hashim' to demonstrate that 'bani' (children) is customarily used figuratively to include all descendants. This establishes a customary (urfi) figurative meaning for 'abna'' as well.
The dalil is Quranic, but its dalalah (indication) is zanni (speculative) because it relies on customary interpretation.
3
Understand How Shubha Operates Without Intending Majaz
The Hanafis avoid combining haqiqah and majaz by not intending the figurative meaning directly. Instead, after taking the literal meaning (sons only), they recognize that the word retains a shubha (semblance) of including grandchildren due to customary usage. For caution in blood matters, this shubha is sufficient to establish aman.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference) and the maxim al-ihtiyat fi hifz al-dimaa'.
4
Distinguish Between Furu' and Usul
The passage contrasts grandchildren with grandparents. When aman is granted to 'aba'' (fathers), grandfathers are not included because they are usul (roots) in creation, while grandchildren are furu' (branches). The subordination by way of following (tab'iyyah) is only appropriate for furu'.
This distinction prevents an apparent inconsistency and shows the subtlety of Hanafi reasoning.
5
Compare with Similar Cases (Oaths and Vows)
The author defends the Hanafi position by analogizing to cases where oaths and vows seemingly combine haqiqah and majaz (e.g., 'not placing foot' covers all modes of entry; 'fast Rajab' when intended as an oath becomes both a vow and an oath). The Hanafi response is that these are not improper combinations because the ruling is based on customary usage or legal effects, not on intending both meanings.
This shows how Hanafis maintain consistency in usul across different chapters of fiqh.

القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم

أي عرفاً
