لشيء مذكور
في ضمن الأبناء، وموالي الموالي في ضمن الموالي، مع أن أبناء الأبناء مجاز في لفظ الأبناء، وموالي الموالي مجاز في لفظ الموالي، فلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فأجاب بقوله: وإنما عمّهم الأمان الفروع: أيضاً أي أبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي، فإن بني الابن ينسبون إلى الجد بالبنوة مجازاً، كما يقال: بنو هاشم، وقال تعالى: ﴿يَا بَنِي آدَم (الأعراف: ٢٦)، وكذا لفظ الموالي يطلق عرفاً على موالي الموالي، كما يقال لمعتق معتق الرجل: مولى الرجل. العمل به أي بهذا التناول الظاهري لتقدم الحقيقة على المجاز في الإرادة، فلا يثبت لهم الأمان باعتبار تناول الاسم إياهم، وكونهم مرادين بالذات؛ لئلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. بقي مجرد الاسم إلخ: أي لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم يدخلون في الأمان بلا إرادة؛ إذ الأمان يثبت بالشبهة أيضاً، وحاصل الجواب : أنا لا نقول: إنهم يثبت لهم الأمان من حيث إنهم حيث إنهم أيضاً مرادون من اللفظ بالذات؛ ليلزم الجمع، بل لما كان اللفظ في العرف يتناولهم أيضاً مجازاً وتركنا المعنى المجازي لتقدم المعنى الحقيقي، فبقي في الاسم شبهة التناول بعده، فلأجل الاحتياط أثبتنا لهم الأمان؛ إذ في حقن الدماء يكفي الشبهة أيضاً. وصار ثبوت الأمان لهم لشبهة تناول الاسم بعد ترك معناه المجازي كالإشارة أي كثبوته بالإشارة.
إلى نفسه بأن يشير إليه باليد: "أنزل إن كنت شجيعاً، فظن أنها أمان لصورة المسالمة: أي لأجل شبهة الأمان. ذلك أي المسالمة، والمراد به الأمان. حقيقة يعني في الإشارة يثبت الأمان لشبهة الأمان وإن لم تكن هذه الإشارة أمانا حقيقةً، فقوله: حقيقة" منصوب على التمييز، ولما كان يرد على هذا الجواب أنه ينبغي أن يعتبر مثل هذه الشبهة لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم فيما إذا استأمن على الآباء والأمهات أيضاً؛ لأن لفظ الآباء والأمهات أيضاً يتناول بظاهر الاسم للأجداد والجدات، فإذا تركتم المعنى المجازي هو الأجداد والجدات في الإرادة خوفاً عن الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فكان شبهة تناول الاسم لهم أيضاً باقية بعده، كما كان في الأبناء والموالي، فيثبت لهم الأمان أيضاً، والحال أنكم لا تثبتون لهم الأمان، فما الفرق؟ فأجاب بقوله : وإنما ترك إلخ يعني إنما ترك اعتبار الصورة أي الشبهة في الأجداد والجدات فيما إذا استأمن على
الآباء والأمهات.

كتاب الله تعالى
٣٦
القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
وذلك إنما يليق بالفروع دون الأصول. فإن قيل: قد قالوا فيمن حلف: "لا يضع قدمه في دار "فلان" إنه يقع على الملك والعارية والإجارة جميعاً، ويحنث إذا دخلها راكباً أو ماشياً وكذلك قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمها فيمن قال: "لله على أن أصوم رجباً"، ونوى به اليمين كان نذراً ويميناً، وفيه جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؟ قلنا:
وذلك: أي اعتبار الشبهة بطريق التبعية بالفروع وهم أبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي، فإنهم فروع في الإطلاق والخلقة، فلهذا يثبت لهم الأمان دون الأصول، وهم هنا الأجداد والجدات فإنهم وإن كانوا فروعاً للآباء والأمهات في إطلاق اللفظ، ولكنهم أصول في الخلقة فكيف يتبعونهم في اللفظ؟ فلذا لم يثبت لهم الأمان، فظهر الفرق، وأما سراية الكتابة إلى الأب إذا اشترى المكاتب أباه فهو لتحقيق الصلة والإحسان، لا لأنه دخول بالتبعية؛ إذ ليس هنا لفظ يدخل فيه تبعاً. وحرمة نكاح الجدات في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَحُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ (النساء:۲۳) ليست بالتبعية، بل بالإجماع أو دلالة النص.
فإن قيل إلخ: تقريره: إذا حلف شخص لايضع قدمه في دار "فلان ولم يكن له نية، فإن دخلها يحنث سواء كانت الدار مملوكة له أو مستعارة أو مستأجرة، وسواء كان دخوله فيها ماشياً أو حافياً أو متنعلا أو راكباً، فهل هذا إلا الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؛ لأن حقيقة دار فلان أن تكون له بطريق الملك، ومجازه أن تكون بطريق العارية والإجارة، وأنتم على كل حال تثبتون الحنث بدخولها، وكذا حقيقة وضع القدم في الدار أن يكون حافياً، ومجازه متنعلا وراكباً، وأنتم على كل حال تثبتون الحنث بوضع القدم فيها. وكذلك: أي كما قالوا جميعاً في المسألة المتقدمة أن الحلف يقع على الملك وغيره. فيمن قال إلخ: تقريره هذا الكلام حقيقة للنذر؛ لعدم توقف ثبوته به على قرينة، كما إذا لم ينو شيئاً، ومجاز لليمين لتوقف ثبوتها به على قرينة النية، والتوقف على القرينة من أمارات المجاز، والنذر واليمين مختلفان؛ لأن موجب الأول الوفاء بالملتزم والقضاء عند الفوات لا الكفارة، وموجب الثاني المحافظة على البر والكفارة عند الفوات لا القضاء. واختلاف الأحكام دليل على اختلاف ذاتها، ذلك قد قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمها : إن من تكلم بهذا الكلام، ونوى به اليمين كان عليه نذر ويمين جميعاً، فلزم الجمع كما قال المصنف. وفيه أي فيما ذكرنا من المسائل. جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز اعلم أنه ذكر فخر الإسلام: رجب بغير تنوين، فالمراد من رجب حينئذ رجب،معين وهو رجب هذه السنة، فهو غير منصرف للعدل والعلمية والمناسب هذا لظهور أثر وجوب القضاء والكفارة عليه في تفويت صوم رجب هذه السنة، وذكر المصنّف منوّناً، فهو منصرف لعدم العلمية.=
Book of Allah Almighty. Section three on the modes of using expressions. Because the term 'sons' and 'masters' apparently includes descendants, but acting upon it is invalid due to the precedence of the literal meaning. There remains mere verbal semblance for the preservation of blood, and it becomes like a gesture: if a disbeliever calls himself by it, aman is established due to the semblance of peaceful conduct, even if it is not literally that. And the consideration of the form for ancestors and grandmothers is omitted in the request for aman for fathers and mothers; because considering the form for the establishment of the ruling in another place only occurs by way of subordination. For something mentioned within 'sons', and 'masters of masters' within 'masters', even though 'sons of sons' is figurative in the word 'sons', and 'masters of masters' is figurative in the word 'masters', thus combining literal and figurative would be necessary. So he answered: 'Rather, aman includes the descendants as well' meaning sons of sons and masters of masters. For the sons of a son are attributed to the grandfather as sons figuratively, as it is said: 'Banu Hashim', and Allah says: 'O children of Adam'. Likewise, the word 'masters' is used customarily for masters of masters, as it is said for the liberator of the liberator of a man: 'master of the man'. Acting upon it, i.e., upon this apparent inclusion, due to the precedence of the literal over the figurative in intention, so aman is not established for them by virtue of the term including them and them being intended per se, to avoid combining literal and figurative. 'There remains mere verbal semblance' etc.: meaning for the sake of caution in preserving blood, they enter into aman without intention; for aman is also established by semblance. The gist of the answer: We do not say that aman is established for them because they are also intended from the word per se, so that combination would be necessary; rather, since the word in custom also includes them figuratively, and we have abandoned the figurative meaning due to the precedence of the literal meaning, there remains in the word a semblance of inclusion after that. So for caution, we establish aman for them; for in preserving blood, semblance suffices. And the establishment of aman for them due to the semblance of the word's inclusion after abandoning its figurative meaning is like the gesture, i.e., like its establishment by gesture. To himself by pointing with his hand: 'Come down if you are brave', and he thought it was aman due to the semblance of peaceful conduct, i.e., due to the semblance of aman. That is peaceful conduct, and what is meant is aman. 'Literal' meaning in the gesture, aman is established due to the semblance of aman even if this gesture is not literally aman. So his word 'literal' is accusative as a specification. And since an objection arises against this answer: such semblance should also be considered for caution in preserving blood when aman is requested for fathers and mothers; for the word 'fathers' and 'mothers' also includes apparently grandfathers and grandmothers. So if you abandon the figurative meaning, which is grandfathers and grandmothers, for fear of combining literal and figurative, then the semblance of the word's inclusion for them also remains after that, as in the case of sons and masters, so aman should also be established for them, while you do not establish aman for them. What is the difference? So he answered: 'Rather, the consideration of the form is omitted' etc., meaning that the consideration of the form, i.e., the semblance, is omitted for grandfathers and grandmothers when aman is requested for fathers and mothers. And that is only appropriate for descendants, not ascendants. For if it is said: They have said concerning one who swears: 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' that it applies to ownership, borrowing, and leasing all together, and he breaks his oath if he enters it riding or walking. Likewise, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said concerning one who says: 'I owe it to Allah that I fast Rajab' and he intended by it (the making of) an oath, it becomes a vow and an oath, and in that there is combining literal and figurative? We say: And that, i.e., consideration of semblance by way of subordination for descendants, and they are sons of sons and masters of masters, for they are descendants in expression and creation, so aman is established for them, but not for ascendants, here grandfathers and grandmothers; for although they are branches of fathers and mothers in expression, they are roots in creation, so how can they follow them in expression? Thus aman is not established for them, so the difference is clear. As for the transfer of kitaba to the father if the mukatib buys his father, it is for realization of connection and kindness, not because it is entry by way of subordination; for there is no word here that enters him as a subordinate. And the prohibition of marriage to grandmothers in Allah's statement: 'Prohibited to you are your mothers' is not by subordination, but by consensus or textual indication. For if it is said etc.: Its explanation: If a person swears: 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' and he has no intention, then if he enters it, he breaks his oath whether the house is owned by him or borrowed or leased, and whether his entry is walking, barefoot, shod, or riding. Is this not combining literal and figurative? Because the literal meaning of 'so-and-so's house' is that it belongs to him by ownership, and its figurative meaning is by borrowing and leasing, and you in any case establish the breaking of the oath by entering it. Likewise, the literal meaning of placing the foot in the house is barefoot, and its figurative meaning is shod and riding, and you in any case establish the breaking of the oath by placing the foot in it. And likewise: i.e., as they all said in the preceding issue that the oath applies to ownership and other. Concerning one who says etc.: Its explanation: This speech is literal for a vow because its establishment does not depend on a context, as when he intends nothing, and figurative for an oath because its establishment depends on a context of intention, and dependence on context is a sign of the figurative. Vow and oath are different because the consequence of the first is fulfillment of the commitment and making up when missed, not expiation; and the consequence of the second is adherence to the virtue and expiation when missed, not making up. Difference in rulings indicates difference in essence. That is, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: Whoever utters this speech and intends by it an oath, upon him are both vow and oath together, so combination is necessary as the author said. And in it, i.e., in what we have mentioned of issues, there is combining literal and figurative. Know that Fakhr al-Islam mentioned 'Rajab' without nunation, so what is meant by 'Rajab' then is a specific Rajab, which is Rajab of this year, so it is diptote due to being adjectival and a proper noun, and this is appropriate for the apparent effect of the obligation of making up and expiation upon missing the fast of this Rajab. And the author mentioned it with nunation, so it is triptote due to lack of proper noun status.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of a fiqh ruling regarding the extension of aman (safe conduct) to certain relatives when the granter uses terms like 'abna'' (sons) and 'mawali' (masters/clients). The discussion centers on the usul principle of not combining haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (figurative meaning) in a single utterance. The author explains that while the literal meaning of 'abna'' does not include grandchildren (abna' al-abna'), and 'mawali' does not include 'mawali al-mawali' (clients of clients), caution in preserving blood (al-ihtiyat fi hifz al-dimaa') allows aman to be extended to them due to shubha (semblance) — the terms are customarily used figuratively to include them. However, this is not because they are intended literally, but because the semblance suffices for aman. The commentary contrasts this with the case of 'aba'' (fathers) and 'ummahat' (mothers): when aman is requested for fathers and mothers, grandfathers and grandmothers are not included because they are 'usul' (roots) in creation, whereas grandchildren are 'furu'' (branches). The argument is supported by a comparison with other fiqh cases: the oath of 'not placing a foot in a house' is treated as covering all modes of ownership (owned, borrowed, leased) and all ways of entering (walking, riding) — this is considered permissible because the oath is enforced generally, and it is not seen as improperly combining haqiqah and majaz. Similarly, the statement 'I owe it to Allah that I fast Rajab' when intended as an oath results in both a nadhr (vow) and a yamin (oath) — a combination that is allowed by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The usul point: combining haqiqah and majaz is normally prohibited, but in these cases, the combination is avoided by not intending the figurative meaning per se; rather, the ruling is based on shubha or custom. The passage also touches on the grammatical treatment of 'Rajab' in the text. Overall, this is a sophisticated exploration of how Hanafi usul handles apparent conflicts between literal and figurative meanings, using istihsan (juristic preference) and the principle of caution in blood matters.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word, as understood in its original or primary sense.
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative meaning of a word, used in a non-literal sense due to a relationship (such as custom or analogy).
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Semblance, ambiguity, or a factor that creates a resemblance to a valid legal category.
الاستيمان (al-istiman): Requesting or granting aman (safe conduct) to a non-Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are grandchildren (abna' al-abna') included in aman when a Muslim grants aman to 'abna'' (sons)?
A) Because the word 'abna'' literally includes all male descendants.
B) Because the figurative meaning is intended, and the literal meaning is abandoned.
C) Because the figurative meaning is not intended, but the word has a shubhah (semblance) of including them, and caution in blood preserves aman.
D) Because grandchildren are considered 'usul' (roots) like fathers.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul avoids combining haqiqah and majaz in one intended meaning, but uses shubha (semblance) to extend rulings for blood preservation, distinguishing between furu' and usul.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Principle of General Metaphorical Meaning (Umum al-Majaz) in Oaths and Vows
Defending Hanafi rulings from the charge of combining literal and metaphorical meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ‘umūm al-majāz to resolve apparent conflicts between literal and metaphorical meanings in oath/vow formulations.

Core Idea
A metaphor, once conventionalized, governs all its instances—no mixing literal and figurative required.

This passage from Muntakhab al-Husam addresses a common criticism of Hanafi rulings on oaths: that they combine literal and metaphorical meanings. The Hanafi response introduces the principle of ‘umūm al-majāz—the general application of the metaphorical sense. We will examine two key examples and the underlying analogy that justifies the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Understand the claim that Hanafi rulings on oaths and vows necessarily combine literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings in a single utterance.
The objector cites two examples: 'placing the foot in a house' and 'fasting Rajab as a vow and oath'.
2
Analyze the first example: the oath on placing the foot
Trace the Hanafi ruling: 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' applies to all modes of entry and all types of occupancy. See how 'placing the foot' is a metaphor for 'entering' and 'house of so-and-so' is a metaphor for 'residence'.
The metaphor is general; the oath is broken by any entry, regardless of means or ownership status.
3
Analyze the second example: the vow involving Rajab
When someone says 'I owe to Allah that I will fast Rajab' intending an oath, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule it is both a vow and an oath. Examine why this is not combining real and figurative but two realities.
The word 'Rajab' with tanwin is indefinite; the utterance meets conditions for both nathr and yamin.
4
Examine the analogy with 'yawm' in conditional emancipation
The expression 'My slave is free on the day Zaid arrives' frees the slave whether Zaid comes by day or night. See how initially it seems to combine literal 'daytime' and metaphorical 'nighttime', but the Hanafi solution is to consider 'yawm' as 'absolute time' when coupled with a non-extended action.
This analogy (qiyas) supports the principle: the metaphor is general, not a combination.
5
Derive the principle: Umum al-Majaz
Conclude that a conventionalized metaphor (like 'placing the foot' for entering) applies to all its instances without needing to refer back to the literal meaning. The general metaphorical meaning is the operative one in oaths and vows.
This principle avoids the logical problem of combining haqiqah and majaz in one utterance.

القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
وذلك إنما يليق بالفروع دون الأصول. فإن قيل: قد قالوا فيمن حلف: "لا يضع قدمه في دار "فلان" إنه يقع على الملك والعارية والإجارة جميعاً، ويحنث إذا دخلها راكباً أو ماشياً وكذلك قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمها فيمن قال: "لله على أن أصوم رجباً"، ونوى به اليمين كان نذراً ويميناً، وفيه جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؟ قلنا:
وذلك: أي اعتبار الشبهة بطريق التبعية بالفروع وهم أبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي، فإنهم فروع في الإطلاق والخلقة، فلهذا يثبت لهم الأمان دون الأصول، وهم هنا الأجداد والجدات فإنهم وإن كانوا فروعاً للآباء والأمهات في إطلاق اللفظ، ولكنهم أصول في الخلقة فكيف يتبعونهم في اللفظ؟ فلذا لم يثبت لهم الأمان، فظهر الفرق، وأما سراية الكتابة إلى الأب إذا اشترى المكاتب أباه فهو لتحقيق الصلة والإحسان، لا لأنه دخول بالتبعية؛ إذ ليس هنا لفظ يدخل فيه تبعاً. وحرمة نكاح الجدات في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَحُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ (النساء:۲۳) ليست بالتبعية، بل بالإجماع أو دلالة النص.
فإن قيل إلخ: تقريره: إذا حلف شخص لايضع قدمه في دار "فلان ولم يكن له نية، فإن دخلها يحنث سواء كانت الدار مملوكة له أو مستعارة أو مستأجرة، وسواء كان دخوله فيها ماشياً أو حافياً أو متنعلا أو راكباً، فهل هذا إلا الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؛ لأن حقيقة دار فلان أن تكون له بطريق الملك، ومجازه أن تكون بطريق العارية والإجارة، وأنتم على كل حال تثبتون الحنث بدخولها، وكذا حقيقة وضع القدم في الدار أن يكون حافياً، ومجازه متنعلا وراكباً، وأنتم على كل حال تثبتون الحنث بوضع القدم فيها. وكذلك: أي كما قالوا جميعاً في المسألة المتقدمة أن الحلف يقع على الملك وغيره. فيمن قال إلخ: تقريره هذا الكلام حقيقة للنذر؛ لعدم توقف ثبوته به على قرينة، كما إذا لم ينو شيئاً، ومجاز لليمين لتوقف ثبوتها به على قرينة النية، والتوقف على القرينة من أمارات المجاز، والنذر واليمين مختلفان؛ لأن موجب الأول الوفاء بالملتزم والقضاء عند الفوات لا الكفارة، وموجب الثاني المحافظة على البر والكفارة عند الفوات لا القضاء. واختلاف الأحكام دليل على اختلاف ذاتها، ذلك قد قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمها : إن من تكلم بهذا الكلام، ونوى به اليمين كان عليه نذر ويمين جميعاً، فلزم الجمع كما قال المصنف. وفيه أي فيما ذكرنا من المسائل. جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز اعلم أنه ذكر فخر الإسلام: رجب بغير تنوين، فالمراد من رجب حينئذ رجب ،معين وهو رجب هذه السنة، فهو غير منصرف للعدل والعلمية والمناسب هذا لظهور أثر وجوب القضاء والكفارة عليه في تفويت صوم رجب هذه السنة، وذكر المصنّف منوّناً، فهو منصرف لعدم العلمية.=

القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
وضع القدم صار مجازاً عن الدخول، وإضافة الدار يراد بها السكنى، فاعتبر عموم حكم "اليوم" متى قرن بفعل ]
المجاز. في الموضعين
وهو نظير ما لو قال: "عبده حرّ يوم يقدم فلان"، فقدم ليلا أو نهاراً عتق؛ لأن اليوم
أحد
و لم ينو شيئا

"اليوم" حينئذ
قالوا: وجب عليه حينئذ صوم رجب غير معين من عمره فلا يظهر الأثر المذكور إلا عند الموت؛ إذ الفوات لا يتحقق إلا عنده فيلزم عليه الوصية بالفدية، والكفارة عند الموت. ثم أجاب المصنّف عن الأسئلة المذكورة بقوله: وضع القدم إلخ يعني يراد من قوله لايضع قدمه لا يدخل، فهذا المعنى مجازي يشمل الدخول حافياً أو متنعلا، فإذا دخل يحنث؛ لعموم المجاز لا بالجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. هذا إذا لم يكن له نية، وإلا فعلى ما نوى حافياً أو متنعلا أو ماشياً أو راكباً. وكذا إضافة الدار يراد بها السكنى على سبيل المجاز، وهذا معنى مجازي يشمل للملك
والعارية والإجارة، فيحنث بعموم المجاز ، لا بالجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز وهو أي اعتبار عموم المجاز ههنا. فقدم: فلان سواء كان قدومه ليلا أو نهارا، عتق عبده. ففي هذه المسألة في بادي الرأي الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؛ إذ "اليوم" حقيقة في النهار ومجاز في الليل، وكلاهما مرادان من لفظ "اليوم" هنا حيث يعتق عبده بقدوم فلان ليلا كان ذلك القدوم أو نهاراً، فوجد الجمع، ولكنه عند التحقيق اعتبار عموم المجاز. لا يمتد: أي: لايصح تقديره بمدة عرفاً، كالخروج والدخول والقدوم. مطلق الوقت على سبيل المجاز عند الأكثر، و قيل: "اليوم"
مشترك بين النهار وبين مطلق الوقت فأريد هنا معنى الوقت فعلى هذا القول لا احتياج إلى عموم المجاز. ثم الوقت الذي هو معنى المجاز. الليل والنهار يعني هو يشملهما، فيحنث باعتبار عموم المجاز لا باعتبار الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فكما اعتبر عموم المجاز في هذا المقام كذا اعتبر في قوله: "لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان"، فهو نظيره. ثم اعلم أن المصنف لم يبين صراحةً حتى يراد باليوم النهار ، ولكن يفهم من قوله على سبيل المقابلة. وحاصل الضابطة هو أن "اليوم" إذا قرن بفعل غير ممتد يراد به مطلق الوقت؛ لأنه يكفي لذلك الفعل جزء من الوقت، وإذا قرن بفعل ممتد يراد به النهار؛ لأنه زمان ممتد يصلح أن يكون معياراً للفعل اللمتد. ثم اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أنه أي فعل يعتبر في هذا الباب المضاف إليه أو المظروف الذي هو عامل؟ فذهب جماعة إلى أنه لا اعتبار للمضاف إليه ؛ لأنه غير عامل في المضاف الذي هو اليوم هنا، كما يفهم من "الهداية". وقال جماعة: إذا كان المضاف إليه والعامل ممتدّين مثل : أمرك بيدك يوم يركب "زيد" ويراد به النهار أو غير ممتدين مثل: "عبدي حرّ =
عبدي: ففي هذ المثال "عبدي حرّ" مظروف وعامل، ويقدم "فلان" المضاف إليه، وكلاهما غير ممتدّين، وقس
عليه البواقي.
The Book of Allah the Exalted. The third section: on the modes of using linguistic structures. That is only appropriate for branches (furu') not for principles (usul). For if it is said: They have said regarding one who swears: 'I will not place my foot in the house of so-and-so' that it applies to ownership, borrowing, and renting all together, and he breaks the oath if he enters it riding or walking. And similarly Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who says: 'I owe to Allah that I will fast Rajab' and intends it as an oath, it becomes a vow and an oath. And in this is a combination of literal and metaphorical meaning? We say: And that: i.e., considering resemblance by way of derivation (taba'iyya) for branches, and they are the children of children and the clients of clients, for they are branches in usage and creation, so for this reason security is established for them without the roots (usul). And here the roots are grandfathers and grandmothers, for although they are branches of fathers and mothers in the usage of the word, they are roots in creation, so how can they follow them in the word? Thus security is not established for them; the difference appears. As for the transfer of kitaba to the father when the mukatab buys his father, it is for the realization of connection and kindness, not because it is entry by taba'iyya, since there is no word here that enters by taba'iyya. And the prohibition of marriage of grandmothers in His saying: 'Forbidden to you are your mothers' (4:23) is not by taba'iyya, but by consensus or by indication of the text. For if it is said etc.: Its explanation: If a person swears 'I will not place my foot in the house of so-and-so' and he has no intention, then if he enters it, he breaks the oath whether the house is owned by him, borrowed, or rented, and whether his entry is walking, barefoot, wearing shoes, or riding. Is this not a combination of literal and metaphorical? Because the literal meaning of 'house of so-and-so' is that it belongs to him by ownership, and its metaphorical meaning is by borrowing and renting. And you in any case establish breaking of the oath by entering it. Similarly, the literal meaning of 'placing the foot' in the house is that he is barefoot, and its metaphorical meaning is wearing shoes or riding. And you in any case establish the breaking of the oath by placing the foot in it. And similarly: i.e., as they all said in the preceding issue that the oath applies to ownership and other than it. Regarding one who said etc.: Its explanation: This speech is literal for a vow (nathr) because its establishment does not depend on a contextual indicator (qarina), as if he intends nothing; and metaphorical for an oath (yamin) because its establishment depends on a contextual indicator of intention, and dependence on contextual indicator is a sign of metaphor. And the vow and oath are different because the requirement of the former is fulfillment of the obligation and making up (qada') when missed, not expiation (kaffara), and the requirement of the latter is adherence to righteousness and expiation when missed, not making up. Difference in legal effects indicates difference in their essences. It has been said by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) that whoever speaks this speech and intends with it an oath, upon him is both a vow and an oath together, so combination is necessary as the author said. And in it, i.e., in what we have mentioned of issues, is a combination of literal and metaphorical meaning. Know that Fakhr al-Islam mentioned 'Rajab' without tanwin, so the meaning of Rajab then is a specific Rajab, i.e., the Rajab of this year, so it is indeclinable for being definite and proper, and this is appropriate for the appearance of the effect of obligation of making up and expiation upon him in missing the fast of this year's Rajab. And the author mentioned it with tanwin, so it is declinable for lack of definiteness. = The Book of Allah the Exalted. The third section: on the modes of using linguistic structures. 'Placing the foot' has become a metaphor for entering, and the addition of 'house' intends residence. So the general meaning of 'day' whenever coupled with an action is considered. Metaphor in both places. And it is analogous to if one said: 'His slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives,' then if he arrives at night or day, the slave is freed, because 'day' when coupled with a non-extended action is taken as absolute time, then time includes night and day. Then 'day' then. They said: Then upon him is the obligation of fasting Rajab unspecified in his life, so the mentioned effect does not appear except at death, because missing is not realized except at it, so upon him is a will for ransom and expiation at death. Then the author answered the mentioned questions with his statement: 'Placing the foot' etc., meaning: from his statement 'I will not place my foot' is intended 'I will not enter', so this meaning is metaphorical and includes entry barefoot or wearing shoes. So if he enters, he breaks the oath due to the generality of the metaphor, not by a combination of literal and metaphorical. This is if he has no intention; otherwise according to what he intended, barefoot or wearing shoes or walking or riding. Similarly, the addition of 'house' intends residence by way of metaphor, and this metaphorical meaning includes ownership, borrowing, and renting. So he breaks the oath by the generality of the metaphor, not by combination of literal and metaphorical. And it (i.e., the consideration of the generality of the metaphor) is here. So so-and-so arrives whether his arrival is at night or day, his slave is freed. So in this issue, at first glance there is combination of literal and metaphorical, because 'day' is literal for daytime and metaphorical for nighttime, and both are intended from the word 'day' here where his slave is freed if so-and-so arrives at night or day. So combination is found, but upon investigation it is considering the generality of the metaphor. 'Non-extended': i.e., it cannot be estimated with a duration customarily, like exiting, entering, and arriving. 'Absolute time' by way of metaphor according to the majority, and it is said: 'day' is shared between daytime and absolute time, so here the meaning of time is intended. So on this view there is no need for the generality of the metaphor. Then time, which is the metaphorical meaning, includes night and day, meaning it encompasses them both. So he breaks the oath by considering the generality of the metaphor, not by considering the combination of literal and metaphorical. So just as the generality of the metaphor is considered in this context, it is considered in his statement: 'I will not place my foot in the house of so-and-so', so it is its analogue. Then know that the author did not explicitly state that 'day' intends daytime, but it is understood from his statement by way of contrast. The summary of the rule is that 'day' when coupled with a non-extended action intends absolute time, because a part of time suffices for that action; and when coupled with an extended action it intends daytime, because it is an extended time suitable as a measure for the extended action. Then know that they disagreed about which action is considered in this chapter: the added-to (mudaf ilayh) or the container (mazruf) which is the agent? A group went that the added-to is not considered because it is not an agent in the added (mudaf) which is 'day' here, as understood from al-Hidaya. Another group said: If the added-to and the agent are both extended, like 'Your affair is in your hand on the day Zaid rides', and 'daytime' is intended, or both non-extended like 'My slave is free = abdī: So in this example 'My slave is free' is the container and agent, and 'so-and-so arrives' is the added-to, and both are non-extended. And measure the rest accordingly.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage from Muntakhab al-Husam is an advanced Usul al-Fiqh discussion on how to interpret legal language in oaths and vows without falling into the error of combining literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings in a single utterance. The author addresses objections against Hanafi rulings and defends the principle of 'considering the general application of the metaphor' (i' tibār 'umūm al-majāz).

The passage begins by clarifying a distinction: the principle of taba'iyya (derivative following) applies to branches (furu') like grandchildren and clients, not to roots (usul) like grandparents. This is contrasted with the case of a mukatib buying his father—this is an act of kindness, not derivative inclusion, since no word implies following.

The central objection: When someone swears 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house', the Hanafi ruling considers the oath violated whether the house is owned, borrowed, or rented, and whether entry is on foot, barefoot, or riding. Critics claim this combines literal and metaphorical meanings (haqiqah for ownership/foot, majaz for renting/riding). The author responds by explaining that both expressions have become metaphorical: 'placing the foot' is a metaphor for 'entering', and 'house of so-and-so' is a metaphor for 'residence' (sukna). Thus the entire utterance is metaphoric, and the oath applies to the general metaphoric sense—not a combination. If the speaker had a specific intention, that governs; otherwise, the broad metaphor applies.

Similarly, the vow 'I owe to Allah that I will fast Rajab' when intended as an oath becomes both nathr and yamin according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Critics see this as combining the literal meaning of nathr and metaphorical meaning of yamin. But the author explains that the word 'Rajab' itself may be specific or general depending on whether it is with tanwin (indefinite) or without (definite). The combination is not of true and figurative but of two realities (nathr and yamin) when the utterance meets the criteria for both.

The passage then analyzes the word 'yawm' (day). In oaths like 'My slave is free on the day Zaid arrives', the slave is freed whether Zaid arrives by day or night. At first glance this combines literal (daytime) and metaphorical (nighttime). But the Hanafis resolve this by considering that when 'yawm' is coupled with a non-extended action (like arrival), it is taken as 'absolute time' (mutlaq al-waqt), which includes both day and night. This is the generality of metaphor. The author notes a disagreement about whether the agent (the action) or the added-to (the arrival of Zaid) determines the meaning, citing al-Hidayah.

The key takeaway: The Hanafi school consistently applies 'umum al-majaz to avoid combining haqiqah and majaz in one expression, whether in oaths, vows, or conditional statements. The ruling is based on the customary usage of language and the intent of the speaker, not on a forced merging of literal and metaphoric senses.

Key Terms
عموم المجاز (‘umūm al-majāz): The principle that a metaphorical expression is applied in its general (customary) sense, covering all cases that fall under that metaphor, rather than mixing literal and metaphorical meanings.
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning of a word as originally coined for its specific referent.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical or figurative meaning of a word used in a sense other than its original due to a connection (علاقة).
نذر (nathr): A vow to perform an act of worship; binding with requirement of fulfillment and qada’ if missed.
يمين (yamīn): An oath invoking Allah or a substitute; binding with requirement of bar (righteousness) and kaffara if broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle of umum al-majaz, if someone swears 'I will not place my foot in Zaid's house' without specifying intention, what is the ruling if he enters Zaid's house riding a horse?
A) He does not break the oath because 'placing the foot' literally means walking.
B) He breaks the oath because the metaphor 'placing the foot' generally means entering, regardless of mode.
C) He breaks the oath only if he intended to include riding.
D) He does not break the oath because the house is not owned by Zaid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school avoids combining literal and metaphorical meanings by applying the general metaphorical sense of conventional expressions in oaths and vows.

The Principle: Interpreting 'اليوم' Based on Action Type
Delivers the core rule for interpreting 'اليوم' in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule for interpreting the word 'اليوم' in oaths when paired with extended vs non-extended actions.

Core Idea
اليوم means absolute time with non-extended actions, but daytime with extended actions.

This passage reveals a fundamental Hanafi usul principle for resolving ambiguities in oath formulations. The word 'اليوم' does not always mean 'daytime'; its meaning shifts based on whether the action it is linked to is extended (can be done over time) or non-extended (instantaneous). Understanding this principle is essential for correctly determining when an oath is fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action paired with 'اليوم'
Determine whether the action is extended (can be measured over time, e.g., riding, writing) or non-extended (occurs in an instant, e.g., arrival, exit).
2
Apply the Hanafi rule
If non-extended, 'اليوم' is interpreted metaphorically as 'absolute time' (includes night). If extended, 'اليوم' is interpreted literally as 'daytime'.
This is based on the principle that a part of time suffices for a non-extended action.
3
Verify using the examples
In 'عبده حرّ يوم يقدم فلان' (non-extended arrival), 'اليوم' includes night, so slave is freed even if arrival at night. In an extended action like riding, 'اليوم' means daytime.
The text also clarifies that this is not combining literal and metaphorical, but applying the generality of the metaphor (عموم المجاز).

والعارية والإجارة، فيحنث بعموم المجاز ، لا بالجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز وهو أي اعتبار عموم المجاز ههنا. فقدم: فلان سواء كان قدومه ليلا أو نهارا، عتق عبده. ففي هذه المسألة في بادي الرأي الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؛ إذ "اليوم" حقيقة في النهار ومجاز في الليل، وكلاهما مرادان من لفظ "اليوم" هنا حيث يعتق عبده بقدوم فلان ليلا كان ذلك القدوم أو نهاراً، فوجد الجمع، ولكنه عند التحقيق اعتبار عموم المجاز. لا يمتد: أي: لايصح تقديره بمدة عرفاً، كالخروج والدخول والقدوم. مطلق الوقت على سبيل المجاز عند الأكثر، و قيل: "اليوم"
مشترك بين النهار وبين مطلق الوقت فأريد هنا معنى الوقت فعلى هذا القول لا احتياج إلى عموم المجاز. ثم الوقت الذي هو معنى المجاز. الليل والنهار يعني هو يشملهما، فيحنث باعتبار عموم المجاز لا باعتبار الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فكما اعتبر عموم المجاز في هذا المقام كذا اعتبر في قوله: "لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان"، فهو نظيره. ثم اعلم أن المصنف لم يبين صراحةً حتى يراد باليوم النهار ، ولكن يفهم من قوله على سبيل المقابلة. وحاصل الضابطة هو أن "اليوم" إذا قرن بفعل غير ممتد يراد به مطلق الوقت؛ لأنه يكفي لذلك الفعل جزء من الوقت، وإذا قرن بفعل ممتد يراد به النهار؛ لأنه زمان ممتد يصلح أن يكون معياراً للفعل اللمتد. ثم اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أنه أي فعل يعتبر في هذا الباب المضاف إليه أو المظروف الذي هو عامل؟ فذهب جماعة إلى أنه لا اعتبار للمضاف إليه ؛ لأنه غير عامل في المضاف الذي هو اليوم هنا، كما يفهم من "الهداية". وقال جماعة: إذا كان المضاف إليه والعامل ممتدّين مثل : أمرك بيدك يوم يركب "زيد" ويراد به النهار أو غير ممتدين مثل: "عبدي حرّ = عبدي: ففي هذ المثال "عبدي حرّ" مظروف وعامل، ويقدم "فلان" المضاف إليه، وكلاهما غير ممتدّين، وقس عليه البواقي.
The third section on the ways of using formulations.
The placing of the foot became a metaphor for entering, and the addition of the house is intended to mean residence. So consider the general meaning of the ruling of "day" when it is paired with an action... It is similar to if he said: "His slave is free on the day Zaid arrives," then he arrived at night or during the day, the slave is freed because "day" when paired with a non-extended action is applied to absolute time, and then time includes night and day. They said: then he must fast Rajab unspecified from his life, so the mentioned effect does not appear except at death; since failure is only realized then, so the bequest of ransom and expiation at death is necessary. Then the author answered the mentioned questions by saying: "Placing the foot etc." meaning from his saying "he does not place his foot" is intended he does not enter. This meaning is metaphorical and includes entering barefoot or with sandals. If he enters, he breaks his oath due to the generality of the metaphor, not due to combining literal and metaphorical. This is if he had no intention; otherwise, it is according to what he intended: barefoot, with sandals, walking, or riding. Similarly, the addition of the house is intended to mean residence metaphorically, and this metaphorical meaning includes ownership, borrowed, and rented. So he breaks his oath by the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical. That is, considering the generality of the metaphor here. So "arrives" – Zaid – whether his arrival is at night or day, his slave is freed. But upon investigation, it is consideration of the generality of the metaphor. "Does not extend" means it cannot be estimated with a customary duration, like exiting, entering, and arrival. Absolute time metaphorically according to the majority. It was said: "day" is shared between daytime and absolute time, so here the meaning of time is intended. According to this saying, there is no need for the generality of metaphor. Then time, which is the meaning of metaphor, night and day includes both. So he breaks his oath by considering the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical. The summary of the principle is that "day" when paired with a non-extended action is intended to mean absolute time, because a part of time suffices for that action. And when paired with an extended action, daytime is intended, because it is extended time suitable as a standard for the extended action. One group held that no consideration is given to the annexed term because it does not operate on the annexed (which is "day" here), as understood from Al-Hidayah. Another group said: If the annexed term and the operator are both extended, like "Your affair is in your hand on the day Zaid rides," and daytime is intended; or both are non-extended, like "My slave is free = My slave: so in this example, 'My slave is free' is the container and operator, and 'Zaid arrives' is the annexed term, and both are non-extended. And analogize the rest.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the third section concerning the usage of linguistic formulations in legal contexts. The author begins by explaining that the word 'اليوم' (day) can have both literal and metaphorical meanings. When paired with a non-extended action like 'قدم' (arrival), it is used metaphorically to mean absolute time (including night), not just daytime. This is demonstrated by the example of an oath: 'If my slave is free on the day Zaid arrives'—if Zaid arrives at night, the slave is freed because 'اليوم' refers to absolute time. The author clarifies that this is not a case of combining literal and metaphorical meanings in one utterance, but rather applying the generality of the metaphor. He then establishes a principle: 'اليوم' paired with a non-extended action means absolute time, and with an extended action means daytime. The passage also discusses a dispute among scholars about whether to consider the annexed term (المضاف إليه, the action) or the operator (العامل, the container word like 'حرّ' or 'أمرك') when determining the meaning of 'اليوم'. Some refer to Al-Hidayah that the annexed term is irrelevant, while others consider both if they match in extension. When they differ, the operator is considered. This discussion is essential for correctly interpreting oaths and vows in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اليوم (al-yawm): In this passage, 'اليوم' is analyzed as having a literal meaning (daytime) and a metaphorical meaning (absolute time including night). Which meaning applies depends on the type of action it is paired with.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical use of a word. Here, 'اليوم' is used metaphorically to mean absolute time when the action is non-extended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The slave is freed because 'اليوم' means absolute time (including night).
B) The slave is not freed because 'اليوم' only means daytime.
C) The slave is freed only if the oath was made without intention.
D) The oath is invalid because night is not included.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'اليوم' in oaths depends on whether the associated action is extended (literal daytime) or non-extended (metaphorical absolute time).

The Dispute: Which Element Governs the Meaning of 'اليوم'?
Explains the dispute over which element determines the meaning of 'اليوم'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for determining which element (the annexed term or the operator) governs the interpretation of 'اليوم' in oaths, and identify the practical consequence of this dispute.

Core Idea
The operator (العامل) governs the meaning of 'اليوم', not the annexed term (المضاف إليه).

This passage addresses a subtle but important dispute among Hanafi scholars: When 'اليوم' is linked to an action, should we consider the action itself (the annexed term) or the main verb of the oath (the operator)? The majority Hanafi position, as derived from Al-Hidayah, holds that only the operator matters. This lesson dissects the arguments and shows the practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
One group holds that only the operator (العامل, e.g., 'حرّ' in 'عبدي حرّ') is considered. Another group considers the annexed term (المضاف إليه, e.g., 'يقدم فلان') as well, but only when both are extended or both non-extended.
2
Understand the reasoning for ignoring the annexed term
The annexed term does not govern (يعمل) the word 'اليوم'; it is merely the time of the action. The operator is the effective word in the sentence.
This is the view of the majority, as understood from Al-Hidayah.
3
Note the condition for the minority view
The minority only considers the annexed term when it matches the operator in extension (both extended or both non-extended). When they differ, all agree the operator is considered.
Thus, the dispute only applies in cases where both match.
4
Apply the Hanafi tarjih
Since the majority view is based on grammatical principle and is supported by the ultimate agreement when they differ, the Hanafi position is to ignore the annexed term entirely.
The text seems to prefer this view by referencing Al-Hidayah.

So "arrives" – Zaid – whether his arrival is at night or day, his slave is freed. In this issue, at first glance, there is combining of literal and metaphorical because "day" is literal for daytime and metaphorical for night, and both are intended from the word "day" here, so combining is found. But upon investigation, it is consideration of the generality of the metaphor. "Does not extend" means it cannot be estimated with a customary duration, like exiting, entering, and arrival. Absolute time metaphorically according to the majority. It was said: "day" is shared between daytime and absolute time, so here the meaning of time is intended. According to this saying, there is no need for the generality of metaphor. Then time, which is the meaning of metaphor, night and day includes both. So he breaks his oath by considering the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical. Just as the generality of the metaphor was considered in this case, so it is considered in his saying: "He does not place his foot in so-and-so's house," so it is its parallel. Then know that the author did not explicitly state that by "day" daytime is intended, but it is understood from his saying by way of contrast. The summary of the principle is that "day" when paired with a non-extended action is intended to mean absolute time, because a part of time suffices for that action. And when paired with an extended action, daytime is intended, because it is extended time suitable as a standard for the extended action. Then know that they differed concerning which action is considered in this chapter: the annexed term or the container which is the operator? One group held that no consideration is given to the annexed term because it does not operate on the annexed (which is "day" here), as understood from Al-Hidayah. Another group said: If the annexed term and the operator are both extended, like "Your affair is in your hand on the day Zaid rides," and daytime is intended; or both are non-extended, like "My slave is free = My slave: so in this example, 'My slave is free' is the container and operator, and 'Zaid arrives' is the annexed term, and both are non-extended. And analogize the rest.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the third section concerning the usage of linguistic formulations in legal contexts. The author begins by explaining that the word 'اليوم' (day) can have both literal and metaphorical meanings. When paired with a non-extended action like 'قدم' (arrival), it is used metaphorically to mean absolute time (including night), not just daytime. This is demonstrated by the example of an oath: 'If my slave is free on the day Zaid arrives'—if Zaid arrives at night, the slave is freed because 'اليوم' refers to absolute time. The author clarifies that this is not a case of combining literal and metaphorical meanings in one utterance, but rather applying the generality of the metaphor. He then establishes a principle: 'اليوم' paired with a non-extended action means absolute time, and with an extended action means daytime. The passage also discusses a dispute among scholars about whether to consider the annexed term (المضاف إليه, the action) or the operator (العامل, the container word like 'حرّ' or 'أمرك') when determining the meaning of 'اليوم'. Some refer to Al-Hidayah that the annexed term is irrelevant, while others consider both if they match in extension. When they differ, the operator is considered. This discussion is essential for correctly interpreting oaths and vows in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
المضاف إليه (al-muḍāf ilayh): The annexed term, here the action that 'اليوم' is added to, e.g., 'يقدم فلان'.
المظروف (al-maẓrūf): The container word, which is the operator (العامل) that includes 'اليوم' as a circumstance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position derived from Al-Hidayah, which element governs the interpretation of 'اليوم' in an oath?
A) The annexed term (المضاف إليه)
B) The operator (العامل/المظروف)
C) Both equally
D) The intention of the speaker

Key Takeaway
In interpreting 'اليوم' in oaths, the Hanafi school relies on the operator, not the annexed term, as the determining factor.

Oath Interpretation When Shariah Abandons Literal Meaning
Explaining the rule when Shariah abandons literal meaning of an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that when Shariah abandons a literal meaning (mahjoor shar'an), the metaphorical meaning governs in definite oaths, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
Shariah's abandonment of a literal meaning overrides linguistic defaults, affecting oath interpretation.

In Usul al-Fiqh, we often analyze how Shariah interacts with language. This passage tackles a fascinating case: when the literal meaning of an oath is abandoned by Islamic law (like abandoning speech with a child), does the oath still apply to the same entity? The Hanafi school divides on this, with Abu Hanifa holding that the entity remains the object, while his two main disciples consider the description relevant. Understanding this requires a careful look at how definiteness and motive shape the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle of Mahjoor Shar'an
Recognize that when Shariah abandons a literal meaning (e.g., abandoning speech with a child is discouraged), the metaphorical meaning takes precedence over the literal. This is derived from the hadith on mercy to children.
The principle is: 'Mahjoor shar'an is like mahjoor bil-'adah' (abandoned in Shariah is like abandoned by custom).
2
Distinguish the Two Hanafi Positions
Abu Hanifa holds that in definite oaths (e.g., 'this child'), the description (childhood) is not considered; only the entity matters. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the metaphorical meaning applies generally, so the description is considered, restricting the oath to children.
The thamarah al-khilaf: If he speaks to the same person after adulthood, Abu Hanifa says he breaks the oath; the two say he does not.
3
Analyze the Linguistic Distinction: Definite vs. Indefinite
The passage explains that when the oath is indefinite (e.g., 'a child'), the description is always considered. When definite, the description is considered only if it is a valid motive for the oath. Here, the child's bad manners are a valid motive, but Shariah overrides this by abandoning the literal meaning.
In the definite case, the description is considered linguistically but abandoned legally, so the oath refers to the entity.
4
Apply to the Example of Speaking to a Child
If someone swears 'I will not speak to this child' (definite), the oath is interpreted metaphorically as 'I will not speak to this entity' using the whole for a part. Speaking to him after childhood breaks the oath because the entity persists.
The oath is valid despite the prohibition of abandoning speech; the Shariah abandonment only affects interpretation.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, breaking the oath requires expiation (kaffarah). Under the two companions, if he speaks after adulthood, he does not break the oath, so no expiation is needed.
The text supports Abu Hanifa's view in the definite case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن هجران الصبي مهجور شرعاً كما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الحنطة أو لا يشرب من هذه الفرات، فعند أبي حنيفة بالله العمل بالحقيقة أولى، وعندهما العمل بعموم المجاز أولى. مهجور شرعا قال النبي : "ليس منا من لم يرحم صغيرنا و لم يوقر كبيرنا". [الترمذي، رقم: ۱۹۱۹] وفي ترك الكلام ترك الرحم، فصار هجران الصبي بالكلام مهجوراً شرعا والمهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عادةً، فيترك الحقيقة وينصرف إلى المجاز كأنه قال: "لا أكلم هذه الذات بطريق إطلاق اسم الكل على البعض، فلو كلمه بعد زوال صفة الصبا يحنث لبقاء الذات. فإن قلت: قد تقرّر في محاورة العرب أن الوصف في المشار إليه لا يعتبر، فيعتبر الذات، فعلى هذا لا حاجة إلى جعله مهجوراً شرعياً. قلتُ: لانسلّم أن الوصف مطلقاً لا يعتبر في المشار إليه، بل إذا لم يصح داعياً إلى اليمين، وهنا يصلح؛ لأن وصف الصبا يصلح داعياً إلى الحلف بترك الكلام مع الصبيان لسفاهتهم وسوء أدبهم، فالوصف حينئذ يعتبر لغة، ولكن ترك اعتباره شرعاً كما عرفت فإن قلت على هذا يلزم أن يكون قوله : "لا أكلم هذا الصبي" بمنزلة قوله : "لا أكلم صبيا" منكرا، وقد قالوا لو قال منكراً يعتبر وصف الصبا حيث يتقيد يمينه بالصبيان، حتى لو كلّم بعد زمان صباه لا يحنث. قلت: إنما اعتبر الوصف فيما قال منكراً شرعاً، ولم يعتبر في الإشارة، مع كونهما معتبرين لغة؛ لأن الوصف في الأول صار مقصوداً بالحلف؛ لكونه هو المعرف للمحلوف عليه، فيعتبر شرعاً في اليمين، وإن كانت اليمين على الحرام كمن :حلف ليشر بنّ اليوم خمراً ينعقد اليمين، وإن كان شربها حراما فيحنث إن لم يشرب بخلاف الثاني، فإن الوصف هنا ضمني فلا يعتبر به وتحقيق ذلك أن اليمين إذا انعقد على شيء بوصف، فإن ذكره منكراً يعتبر مطلقاً، سواء كان الوصف يصلح داعياً إلى اليمين أو لا، وإن ذكره معرفاً فإن كان الوصف يصلح داعياً إلى اليمين يعتبر أيضاً، كما في قوله: "لا اكلم هذا الصبي" وإلا لا.
Because abandoning speech with a child is abandoned in Shariah, as if he swore not to eat from this wheat or not to drink from this Euphrates, then according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) acting upon the literal meaning is preferable, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) acting upon the general metaphorical meaning is preferable. Abandoned in Shariah: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'He is not of us who does not show mercy to our young and does not respect our elders.' [Tirmidhi, no. 1919]. And in refraining from speech there is refraining from mercy, so abandoning speech with a child becomes abandoned in Shariah, and that which is abandoned in Shariah is like that which is abandoned by custom, so the literal meaning is left and one turns to the metaphorical meaning as if he said: 'I will not speak to this entity by way of using the whole for a part,' so if he speaks to him after the attribute of childhood has left, he breaks his oath due to the persistence of the entity. If you say: It has been established in Arab discourse that the description of the one referred to is not considered, only the entity is considered; thus there is no need to make it abandoned in Shariah. I say: We do not concede that the description is absolutely not considered in the one referred to; rather, it is when it is not a valid motive for the oath, and here it is valid because the description of childhood is a valid motive for swearing to refrain from speaking with children due to their foolishness and bad manners. So the description is then considered linguistically, but its consideration is abandoned in Shariah as you have learned. If you say: This would entail that his statement 'I will not speak to this child' is equivalent to his statement 'I will not speak to a child' as indefinite, and they have said that if he said it indefinitely, the description of childhood is considered so that his oath is restricted to children, such that if he speaks after the time of childhood he does not break his oath. I say: The description is considered in what he said indefinitely in Shariah, and is not considered in the indicative form, while both are considered linguistically, because the description in the first has become intended by the oath as it is the identifier of the sworn thing, so it is considered in Shariah in the oath, even if the oath is upon a prohibited thing, like someone who swears to drink wine today, the oath is valid even though drinking it is prohibited, and he breaks his oath if he does not drink, unlike the second, for the description there is incidental and is not considered. The verification of that is that when an oath is contracted upon something with a description, if it is mentioned indefinitely, it is considered absolutely, whether the description is a valid motive for the oath or not; if it is mentioned with definite article, then if the description is a valid motive for the oath, it is also considered, as in his statement 'I will not speak to this child,' otherwise not.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an expansion explaining the ruling when the literal meaning of a word has been abandoned in Islamic law (مهجور شرعاً). The commentator uses the example of swearing to not eat 'this wheat' or drink 'this Euphrates'. Literally, these mean eating the actual grains and drinking directly from the river (by cupping hands). However, in Islamic law, abandoning speech with a child (هجران الصبي) is considered abandoned because the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded kindness to children. Thus, if someone swears not to speak to 'this child', the literal reference to the child as an entity remains, but the description of childhood is not considered because Shariah discourages abandoning speech with children. Therefore, the oath is interpreted metaphorically: it is as if he said 'I will not speak to this person' by using the whole for a part, so if he speaks to him after he becomes an adult, he breaks his oath. The passage then addresses a potential objection: in Arabic discourse, when pointing to someone, the description is usually not considered; only the entity matters. The response is that descriptions are not considered only when they are not a valid motive for the oath, but here the child's bad manners are a valid motive. Nevertheless, Shariah overrides this linguistic consideration. The passage further distinguishes between indefinite and definite mention of the sworn item: if indefinite, the description is always considered; if definite, the description is considered only if it is a valid motive for the oath. Thus, the ruling in the abandoned case is that the description is not considered, so the oath applies to the entity itself.

Key Terms
هجران (hajrān): Abandoning or refraining from speech with someone.
مهجور شرعاً (mahjūr shar‘an): A literal meaning that is abandoned (not acted upon) due to Islamic legal considerations.
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): The original literal meaning of a word as coined.
مجاز (majāz): The metaphorical or non-literal meaning of a word.
مشار إليه (mushār ilayhi): The indicated entity when pointing to someone or something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to this child' (using a definite pointing), and then speaks to him after he becomes an adult, according to Abu Hanifa, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he spoke to the same entity.
B) No, because the description of childhood no longer applies.
C) It depends on whether the child was rude.
D) No, because the oath was invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
When Shariah abandons a literal meaning, the oath is interpreted referring to the entity, not the description, but only in definite oaths; indefinite oaths still consider the description.

Five Indicators for Leaving Literal Meaning
Explains the five contextual factors that override literal meaning in Arabic usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five contextual indicators that override literal meaning in Arabic and apply them to interpret Hanafi legal texts and oaths.

Core Idea
Literal meaning is the default, but five specific factors override it: context, custom, speaker state, textual flow, and word etymology.

This passage presents the Hanafi position on when to abandon the literal meaning (haqiqah) for metaphorical meaning (majaz). After establishing the general preference for literal meaning, the author lists five indispensable contextual indicators that force a metaphorical interpretation. Understanding these five factors is essential for accurately interpreting Quranic verses, prophetic hadith, and legal oaths according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Understand the foundational dispute
Recognize that Imam Abu Hanifa prefers literal meaning when both literal and metaphorical meanings are in common use, while his two companions prefer metaphor when it yields the more common ruling. This sets the stage for the five indicators.
The dispute is based on whether substitution is considered from the speaker's perspective (Abu Hanifa) or from the ruling's perspective (the two companions).
2
Identify the five indicators
Memorize the five indicators mentioned: (1) indication of context of speech, (2) indication of custom, (3) indication of a meaning relating to the speaker (oath of immediacy), (4) indication of composition context (siyaq al-nazm), and (5) indication of the wording itself (derivation/etymology).
Each indicator has a specific example in the passage.
3
Apply each indicator to the examples
Work through the given examples: 'Do not eat from this palm tree' (context), 'Do not place his foot' (custom), the angry husband's oath (speaker state), the Quranic verse on disbelief (composition context), and the meat/fruit oaths (wording derivation). Explain why literal meaning is abandoned in each case.
Pay attention to the detailed reasoning for the oath of immediacy and the derivation-based restrictions.
4
Distinguish the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical differences between Abu Hanifa's position and that of the two companions. For example, in the oath about meat, Abu Hanifa excludes fish due to etymology, while the companions might include it if custom treats fish as meat.
The text indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is often preferred but not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى
القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم
فصارت الحقيقة أولى وعندهما المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة في الحكم، وفي الحكم للمجاز رجحان لاشتماله على حكم الحقيقة، فصار أولى. ثم جملة ما يترك به الحقيقة خمسة قد تترك بدلالة محلّ الكلام، وبدلالة العادة كما ذكرنا، في الشرعيات بالاستقراء حتى أثبت الخلفية للمجاز في التكلم كما مرّ. فصارت الحقيقة المستعملة التي غير مهجورة في العادة أولى من المجاز وإن كان متعارفاً. وعندهما المجاز إلخ كما مرّ تشريحه، فإذا كان الحكم عندهما معتبراً حتى أثبتنا الخلفية للمجاز في الحكم. رجحان إما لاشتماله على حكم الحقيقة كما في صورة عموم المجاز، وإما لغلبة استعماله لكونه متعارفا. :أولى من الحقيقة، وكان الأولى للمصنّف أن يقول : "الغلبة استعماله بدل قوله لاشتماله على حكم الحقيقة"؛ ليشمل كلا النوعين من المجاز عموم المجاز، وغيره. وتقرير المقام بحيث ينكشف به المرام هو أن هذه المسألة (أي إذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة والمجاز متعارفاً فعنده الحقيقة أولى وعندهما (المجاز مبنية على أصل آخر، وهو أن الاعتبار في الخلفية عنده للتكلم فالتكلم بالحقيقة ،أولى، فإذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة في التكلم عادةً فأية حاجة إلى جعل اللفظ مجازاً، وعندهما الاعتبار للحكم حتى يجعل المجاز خلفاً فيه فإذا كان المعنى المجازي للفظ متعارفاً فكان الحكم في المجاز راجحاً على الحكم الذي في الحقيقة؛ لكونه غير متعارف، فلا بد أن يحمل اللفظ على المجاز لاعتبار رجحان الحكم الذي عليه المدار. ثم شرع المصنّف في بيان القرائن التي بها يترك العمل بالحقيقة ويعمل بالمجاز محل الكلام أي بدلالة ما وقع فيه الكلام وما يتعلق به بأن لا يكون صالحاً للمعنى الحقيقي، إما للزوم الكذب فيمن هو معصوم عنه أو لوجه آخر ، فإذا لم يقبل المحل المعنى الحقيقي فيصار إلى المجاز لا محالة. وبدلالة العادة أي العادة في استعمال الألفاظ وفهم المعاني منها، وذلك لأن الكلام موضوع للإفهام، فإذا نقل عن معناه اللغوي واستعمل في العرف لشيء آخر فلا محالة يراد به هذا المعنى، وهذا إذا لم يكن الحقيقة مستعملة، وإلا لكانت الحقيقة أولى من المجاز عند أبي حنيفة رحاله كما عرفت. كما ذكرنا في قولنا: "لا يأكل من هذه النخلة، وفي قولنا: "لا يضع قدمه في دار "فلان"، فإن الحقيقة في الأول تركت بدلالة محل الكلام؛ إذ المحل لا يصلح أن يكون مأكولاً للتعذر فيراد المجاز وهو الثمر أوالثمن، وفى الثاني تركت بدلالة العادة؛ لأن المعنى الحقيقي وهو وضع القدم حافياً متروك، وإنما تعارف المعنى المجازي، وهو الدخول، فيراد هذا، وكذا سائر الألفاظ المنقولة شرعاً كالصلاة والزكاة والحج، أو عرفاً عاما أو خاصا.

وبدلالة معنى يرجع إلى المتكلم كما في يمين الفور، وبدلالة سياق النظم كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَاراً﴾ (الكهف: ٢٩)، وبدلالة اللفظ في نفسه، كما إذا حلف: "لايأكل لحما"، فأكل لحم السمك لم يحنث، وكذا إذا حلف: "لايأكل فاكهة"، فأكل العنب لم يحنث عند أبي حنيفة؛ لقصور في المعنى المطلوب في الأول وزيادة في الثاني. أي لحم السمك بدلالة معنى إلخ وقصده فيحمل على الأخص، وإن كان اللفظ دالا على العموم بحقيقته. يمين الفور: والفور في الأصل مصدر، فارت القدر إذا غلت واشتدت فاستعير للسرعة، ثم سميت به الحالة التي لا ربث فيها ولا لبث، يقال: جاء فلان من فوره أي من ساعته والمراد بيمين الفور: قول الرجل في حالة الغضب لامرأته تريد الخروج من الدار): إن خرجت فأنت طالق، وإنما سميت بهذا الاسم لصدورها من المتكلم في حالة فوران الغضب، فحقيقة هذا الكلام أن تطلق في كل ما خرجت، ولكن بسبب معنى حدث في المتكلم، وهو فوران الغضب، يحمل هذا الكلام على المعنى المجازي وهو هذه الخرجة المعينة لهذه القرينة، حتى لو مكنت ساعة ثم خرجت بعد ذلك لا تطلق، فحمل على الخاص، وهذا النوع من اليمين أخرجه أبو حنيفة لله ، خاصةً، وكان القوم فيما سبق يقولون: اليمين مؤقتة، كقوله: "والله لا أفعل اليوم كذا"، ومؤبدة كقوله: "والله لا أفعل كذا" فأخرج الإمام مؤقتة معنى ومطلقة لفظاً. بدلالة سياق النظم أي بسبب سوق الكلام بقرينة لفظية التحقت به سابقة عليه أو متأخرة إلا أن السابق أكثر استعمالاً من المؤخرة فليكفر فإن حقيقة قوله فليكفر وجوب الكفر، ولكنها تركت بقرينة قوله: وإِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَارا (الكهف: ۲۹)، وحمل على المعنى المجازي وهو التوبيخ من قبيل ذكر الضد إرادة الآخر لمعاقبة بينهما؛ إذ المراد من مثل هذا الأمر النهى ومثله قول الرجل لآخر : طلق امرأتي إن كنت رجلا، وافعل في مالي ما شئت إن كنت رجلا لا يكون توكيلاً، وكذا قوله للكافر: "انزل إن كُنتَ رجلا" لا يكون أماناً بقرينة قوله: "إن كنت رجلا". بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه أي باعتبار مادة حروفه ومأخذ اشتقاقه لا باعتبار إطلاقه، وذلك بأن يكون اللفظ موضوعاً لمعنى لكن يتفاوت أفراده بالزيادة والنقصان، بأن يكون هذا المعنى في بعضه زائداً أو ناقصاً عن باقي الإفراد، فيخرج الناقص والزائد، ويسمّى مشككا. في الثاني: أي العنب، وذلك لأن اللحم مأخوذ من الالتحام. يقال: التحم القتال أي اشتدّ، ثم سمي اللحم بهذا الاسم؛ لقوة فيه باعتبار تولده من الدم، فهذا اللفظ =
The Book of Allah Almighty. The third section on the ways of using expressions. Then the literal meaning becomes preferable, and according to the two (companions), metaphor is a substitute for literal meaning in ruling, and in ruling metaphor has preference because it includes the ruling of the literal meaning, so it becomes preferable. Then the total of what causes abandoning literal meaning is five: it may be abandoned by indication of the context of speech, and by indication of custom as we mentioned, in religious matters by induction until we established the substitutability for metaphor in speech as before. So the used literal meaning that is not abandoned in custom is preferable over metaphor even if it is conventional. And according to the two (companions), metaphor etc. as its explanation passed. So when the ruling according to them is considered until we established substitutability for metaphor in ruling. Preference either because it includes the ruling of the literal meaning as in the case of general metaphor, or because of its frequent usage due to being conventional. (It is) preferable over literal meaning, and it would have been better for the author to say 'its frequent usage' instead of 'because it includes the ruling of the literal meaning' to include both types of metaphor: general metaphor and others. The exposition of the matter such that the intent is uncovered is that this issue (i.e., when literal meaning is used and metaphor is conventional, then according to him [Abu Hanifa] literal meaning is preferable, and according to the two [companions] metaphor is preferred) is based on another principle: that the consideration in substitutability according to him is for the speaker, so speaking with literal meaning is preferable; so if literal meaning is used in speech customarily, what need is there to make the wording metaphorical? And according to them, the consideration is for the ruling, so that metaphor is made a substitute in it; so if the metaphorical meaning of the wording is conventional, then the ruling in metaphor is preponderant over the ruling that is in the literal meaning because it is unconventional; so the wording must be interpreted metaphorically due to the preponderance of the ruling upon which the matter revolves. Then the author began explaining the indications by which one leaves acting upon literal meaning and acts upon metaphor: indication of the context of speech, i.e., by indication of what the speech occurs about and what relates to it, such that it is not suitable for the literal meaning, either due to necessitating falsehood from one who is infallible from it or for another reason; so when the context does not accept the literal meaning, then one resorts to metaphor inevitably. And by indication of custom, i.e., custom in using words and understanding meanings from them, because speech is meant for communication; so when it is transferred from its linguistic meaning and used in custom for something else, then inevitably this meaning is intended. And this is when the literal meaning is not used; otherwise, literal meaning would be preferable over metaphor according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, as you know. As we mentioned in our saying: 'Do not eat from this palm tree,' and in our saying: 'Do not place his foot in the house of so-and-so.' For the literal meaning in the first is abandoned by indication of the context of speech, since the context is not suitable to be eaten due to impossibility, so metaphor is intended, which is the fruit or the price. In the second, it is abandoned by indication of custom, because the literal meaning—placing the foot bare—is abandoned, and the metaphorical meaning—entering—is conventional, so this is intended. And similarly all legally transferred words like prayer, zakat, pilgrimage, or customarily general or specific. And by indication of a meaning relating to the speaker, as in an oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr), and by indication of the context of the composition (siyaq al-nazm) as in His statement: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' (Quran 18:29), and by indication of the wording itself, as when he swears: 'I will not eat meat,' then he eats fish, he does not break his oath; and similarly if he swears: 'I will not eat fruit,' then he eats grapes, he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa; because of a deficiency in the intended meaning in the first and an excess in the second. That is, fish meat by indication of a meaning etc., and he intends it, so it is interpreted as the specific, even if the wording indicates generality by its literal meaning. Oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr): fawr originally is a verbal noun; the pot boils when it bubbles and becomes intense, then it is used metaphorically for speed, then the state in which there is no delay or stay is named by it. It is said: 'He came immediately (min fawrihi),' i.e., from his hour. The meaning of an oath of immediacy is a man's saying to his wife in a state of anger while she intends to leave the house: 'If you go out, you are divorced.' It is named by this name because it issues from the speaker in a state of anger boiling up. The literal meaning of this speech is that she is divorced every time she goes out, but due to a meaning occurring in the speaker—which is anger boiling up—this speech is interpreted as the metaphorical meaning, which is this specific exit due to this indication, such that if she waits an hour and then goes out after that, she is not divorced. So it is interpreted as the specific. This type of oath was specifically introduced by Abu Hanifa for Allah. Previously, people used to say: oaths are temporary, like his saying: 'By Allah, I will not do such-and-such today,' and permanent like his saying: 'By Allah, I will not do such-and-such.' So the Imam introduced a temporally restricted meaning with absolute wording. By indication of the context of the composition (siyaq al-nazm), i.e., due to the flow of speech with a verbal indication attached to it, preceding or following it, except that the preceding is more frequently used than the following. 'Let him disbelieve': the literal meaning of 'let him disbelieve' is the obligation of disbelief, but it is abandoned by the indication of His statement: 'Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' (Quran 18:29), and interpreted as the metaphorical meaning, which is rebuke, from the category of mentioning the opposite intending the other due to a connection between them; for the intent of such a command is prohibition. Similar to this is a man's saying to another: 'Divorce my wife if you are a man,' and 'Do as you wish with my wealth if you are a man'—this does not constitute an agency. Similarly, his saying to a disbeliever: 'Descend if you are a man' does not constitute a guarantee of safety, due to the indication of his saying 'if you are a man.' By indication of the wording itself, i.e., by considering the material of its letters and the source of its derivation, not by considering its broader application. This is when the wording is placed for a meaning but its individuals differ by excess and deficiency, such that this meaning in some of them is excessive or deficient compared to the rest of the individuals, so the deficient and excessive are excluded. This is called equivocal (mushakkak). In the second: i.e., grapes. Because 'meat' is derived from 'iltiham' (interlocking). It is said: 'the battle interlocked' meaning it intensified, then meat was named by this name due to a strength in it because it is generated from blood. So this word...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the usul principle regarding when to abandon the literal meaning (haqiqah) in favor of metaphorical meaning (majaz). The author begins by summarizing the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Hanifa holds that when both the literal and metaphorical meanings are in use, the literal is preferable because the speaker's usage is the basis for substitution. The two companions consider the ruling itself, so if the metaphorical meaning is more commonly associated with that ruling, they prefer it. This dispute affects many fiqh examples, such as interpreting oaths and legal terms.

After establishing this foundation, the author enumerates five specific indicators that cause the literal meaning to be set aside: (1) indication of the context of speech (e.g., 'Do not eat from this palm tree' cannot mean eating the tree, so it must mean its fruit or value); (2) indication of custom (e.g., 'Do not place his foot in the house' conventionally means entering, not literal foot placement); (3) indication of a meaning relating to the speaker, exemplified by the 'oath of immediacy' (yamin al-fawr) where a husband in anger says 'If you go out, you are divorced' — this is interpreted as applying only to that specific immediate exit, not all future exits, due to the speaker's state; (4) indication of the context of composition (siyaq al-nazm), as in the Quranic verse 'Let him disbelieve' which literally commands disbelief but is understood as a threat due to the following verse about punishment; (5) indication of the wording itself, where the word's etymology restricts its application — for example, 'meat' derives from 'iltiham' (strength/interlocking), so fish is excluded, and 'fruit' derives from being a product of a tree, so grapes (which are from a vine) are excluded according to Abu Hanifa.

Throughout, the author explains the reasoning behind each indicator using Hanafi examples, always relating back to the principle that the literal meaning is the default unless one of these five factors necessitates a metaphorical interpretation. The commentary emphasizes the Hanafi madhhab's systematic approach to textual interpretation.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The original literal meaning of a word as per linguistic convention.
المجاز (al-majāz): A word used in a meaning other than its original due to a contextual indication (qarīnah).
يمين الفور (yamīn al-fawr): An oath made in a state of anger or immediacy, interpreted restrictively to the specific occasion due to the speaker's emotional state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats fish, why does he not break his oath?
A) Because fish is not considered meat according to the literal meaning derived from its etymology.
B) Because the oath is nullified due to lack of intention.
C) Because fish is not a meat in any custom.
D) Because the wording 'meat' applies only to red meat.

Key Takeaway
The default is literal meaning; only when one of five specific indicators (context, custom, speaker state, composition context, word derivation) is present should a jurist move to metaphorical interpretation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
