Three Modes of Textual Implication in Hanafi Usul
Commentary on the third section: modes of textual implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modes of textual implication and apply them to interpret oaths and Quranic commands according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Meaning is not always literal; context, speaker's state, and word derivation restrict or shift legal implication.

This passage from Muntakhab al-Husami introduces three fundamental modes of interpreting legal texts: implication from the speaker's intent, from contextual clues, and from the word's etymology. Understanding these modes is essential for deriving correct rulings, especially when a general word appears but the intended meaning is specific. We will work through each mode with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes
List the three modes as described: (1) bi-dalālat ma‘nā yarji‘u ilā al-mutakallim, (2) bi-dalālat siyāq al-nazm, (3) bi-dalālat al-lafẓ fī nafsih.
Each mode represents a different source of restriction: speaker's state, verbal context, and derivational meaning.
2
Analyze the oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr)
Examine how the speaker's anger (fawr) causes a general oath to be interpreted as specific to the immediate instance. The literal meaning 'every time you go out' is restricted to 'this specific outing' due to the speaker's state.
Abu Hanifa treats this as temporary in meaning but absolute in wording, specific to Allah.
3
Examine contextual implication (siyaq al-nazm)
Look at the Quranic verse 'فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ' (18:29). The apparent command 'let him disbelieve' is understood as a rebuke because of the following threat of fire. Identify how the verbal context (the phrase 'أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَاراً') overrides the literal imperative.
This is an example of mentioning the opposite to intend the opposite (dhikr al-ḍidd irādat al-ākhar).
4
Work through the derivation example (dalālat al-lafẓ fī nafsih)
Understand the example of someone swearing not to eat 'meat' (laḥm) and then eating fish. Trace the derivation: laḥm comes from 'iltiḥām' meaning strength/blood, and fish lack blood. Thus, by its root meaning, 'meat' does not include fish. Similarly, 'fruit' (fākiha) is derived from 'tafakkuh' meaning enjoyment, and since grapes/dates are nourishing, they are excluded from 'fruit' in oaths.
Abu Hanifa uses this etymological approach; Shafi‘i and Malik rely on common usage (the Quran calls fish meat in 16:14).

Source Text

القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم وبدلالة معنى يرجع إلى المتكلم كما في يمين الفور، وبدلالة سياق النظم كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَاراً﴾ (الكهف: ٢٩)، وبدلالة اللفظ في نفسه، كما إذا حلف: "لايأكل لحما"، فأكل لحم السمك لم يحنث، وكذا إذا حلف: "لايأكل فاكهة"، فأكل العنب لم يحنث عند أبي حنيفة؛ لقصور في المعنى المطلوب في الأول وزيادة في الثاني. الأول أي لحم السمك بدلالة معنى إلخ وقصده فيحمل على الأخص، وإن كان اللفظ دالا على العموم بحقيقته. يمين الفور: والفور في الأصل مصدر، فارت القدر إذا غلت واشتدت فاستعير للسرعة، ثم سميت به الحالة التي لا ربث فيها ولا لبث، يقال: جاء فلان من فوره أي من ساعته والمراد بيمين الفور: قول الرجل في حالة الغضب لامرأته تريد الخروج من الدار): إن خرجت فأنت طالق، وإنما سميت بهذا الاسم لصدورها من المتكلم في حالة فوران الغضب، فحقيقة هذا الكلام أن تطلق في كل ما خرجت، ولكن بسبب معنى حدث في المتكلم، وهو فوران الغضب، يحمل هذا الكلام على المعنى المجازي وهو هذه الخرجة المعينة لهذه القرينة، حتى لو مكنت ساعة ثم خرجت بعد ذلك لا تطلق، فحمل على الخاص، وهذا النوع من اليمين أخرجه أبو حنيفة لله ، خاصةً، وكان القوم فيما سبق يقولون: اليمين مؤقتة، كقوله: "والله لا أفعل اليوم كذا"، ومؤبدة كقوله: "والله لا أفعل كذا" فأخرج الإمام مؤقتة معنى ومطلقة لفظاً. بدلالة سياق النظم أي بسبب سوق الكلام بقرينة لفظية التحقت به سابقة عليه أو متأخرة إلا أن السابق أكثر استعمالاً من المؤخرة فليكفر فإن حقيقة قوله فليكفر وجوب الكفر، ولكنها تركت بقرينة قوله: وإِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَارا (الكهف: ۲۹)، وحمل على المعنى المجازي وهو التوبيخ من قبيل ذكر الضد إرادة الآخر لمعاقبة بينهما؛ إذ المراد من مثل هذا الأمر النهى ومثله قول الرجل لآخر : طلق امرأتي إن كنت رجلا، وافعل في مالي ما شئت إن كنت رجلا لا يكون توكيلاً، وكذا قوله للكافر: "انزل إن كُنتَ رجلا" لا يكون أماناً بقرينة قوله: "إن كنت رجلا". بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه أي باعتبار مادة حروفه ومأخذ اشتقاقه لا باعتبار إطلاقه، وذلك بأن يكون اللفظ موضوعاً لمعنى لكن يتفاوت أفراده بالزيادة والنقصان، بأن يكون هذا المعنى في بعضه زائداً أو ناقصاً عن باقي الإفراد، فيخرج الناقص والزائد، ويسمّى مشككا. في الثاني: أي العنب، وذلك لأن اللحم مأخوذ من الالتحام. يقال: التحم القتال أي اشتدّ، ثم سمي اللحم بهذا الاسم؛ لقوة فيه باعتبار تولده من الدم، فهذا اللفظ باعتبار مأخذ اشتقاقه لا يتناول لحم السمك لقصور هذا المعنى فيه؛ إذ لا قوة والشدة بغير الدم، والسمك لا دم فيه؛ لأن الدموي لايعيش في الماء، فلذا لا يحنث بأكل لحمه، وبقولنا: "باعتبار مأخذ اشتقاقه" احترز عن التناول باعتبار آخر، فإنه بالاعتبار الآخر قد أطلق عليه اللحم في القرآن، قال تعالى: وَلِتَأْكُلُوا مِنْهُ لَحْماً طَرِيّا (النحل: ١٤)، و به تمسك مالك والشافعي رحم لها حيث قالا يحنث بأكل لحم السمك، وكذا الفاكهة اسم لما يؤكل على : سبيل التفكه وهو التنعم، ولا يكفي وحده للغذاء. قال الله تعالى: انْقَلَبُوا فَكِهِينَ ﴾ (المطففين:٣١) أي متنعمين، والتنعم أمر زائد على ما به قوام البدن ،وبقاؤه والعنب والرطب يتعلق بهما القوام، ويكفي بهما في بعض الأمصار للغذاء، فحصل فيهما أمر ،زائد فبهذه الزيادة لايتناولهما اسم الفاكهة، فلا يحنث بأكل العنب عند أبي حنيفة إذا لم ينو شيئا، وعندهما يحنث، وهو قول الشافعي له، وأما لو نواها فيحنث اتفاقاً. وهنا أبحاث شريفة تركناها مخافة التطويل، ولما فرغ عن الحقيقة والمجاز شرع في الصريح والكناية.
The third section on the modes of using texts: By implication of meaning referring back to the speaker, as in the oath of immediacy; by implication of the context of the text, as in His statement: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a Fire' (al-Kahf:29); and by implication of the word itself, as when one swears: 'I will not eat meat,' and he eats fish meat, he does not break his oath, and likewise if he swears: 'I will not eat fruit,' and he eats grapes, he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa, due to deficiency in the intended meaning in the first and excess in the second. The first being fish meat; by implication of meaning, etc., and his intent is taken into account, so it is interpreted in the specific sense, even if the word indicates generality by its literal meaning. Oath of immediacy: al-fawr originally is a verbal noun; the pot boils when it rages and becomes intense, then it is metaphorically used for speed, then the state in which there is no delay or lingering is named after it. It is said: 'He came from his fawr,' meaning immediately. What is meant by the oath of immediacy is a man's statement to his wife in a state of anger when she wants to leave the house: 'If you go out, you are divorced.' It is named thus because it issues from the speaker in a state of boiling anger. The literal meaning of this statement is that she is divorced every time she goes out, but due to a meaning that occurs in the speaker (the boiling of anger), this statement is interpreted metaphorically as this specific instance of going out due to this contextual indication, so that if she stays for an hour and then goes out afterwards, she is not divorced. Thus it is interpreted in the specific sense. Abu Hanifa extracted this type of oath as specific to Allah. Formerly, people used to say: oaths are temporary, like saying 'By Allah, I will not do such-and-such today,' and permanent like saying 'By Allah, I will not do such-and-such.' The Imam brought out temporary in meaning but absolute in wording. By implication of the context of the text: i.e., due to the flow of speech with a verbal indication attached to it, preceding or following, except that the preceding is more commonly used than the following. For His word 'fal-yakfur' (let him disbelieve): the literal meaning of 'let him disbelieve' is the obligation of disbelief, but it is abandoned due to the indication of His statement: 'Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a Fire' (al-Kahf:29), and interpreted metaphorically as rebuke, from the category of mentioning the opposite to intend the other, because of the interrelationship between them; for what is meant by such a command is prohibition. An example of this is a man saying to another: 'Divorce my wife if you are a man' and 'Do whatever you wish with my wealth if you are a man'; this does not constitute agency. Likewise, saying to a disbeliever: 'Descend if you are a man' does not constitute amnesty due to the indication of 'if you are a man.' By implication of the word itself: i.e., considering the material of its letters and the root of its derivation, not considering its absolute application. This is when a word is placed for a meaning, but its instances vary in excess or deficiency, such that this meaning is excessive or deficient in some of them compared to the rest, so the deficient and excessive are excluded, and it is called mushakkak (ambiguous). In the second: i.e., grapes. This is because 'meat' is derived from 'iltiham' (joining). It is said: 'the battle became intense' (iltahama), then meat is named by this name due to strength in it by virtue of its generation from blood. Thus this word, by consideration of its derivational root, does not include fish meat because this meaning (strength) is deficient in it, since there is no strength and intensity without blood, and fish have no blood because a blood-based creature cannot live in water. Therefore, he does not break his oath by eating it. By our saying 'by consideration of its derivational root,' we guard against inclusion by another consideration, for by another consideration it is called meat in the Quran. He said: 'that you may eat from it fresh meat' (al-Nahl:14). Malik and al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on them, adhered to this and said that he breaks his oath by eating fish meat. Likewise, 'fruit' is a name for what is eaten for enjoyment, which is pleasure, and it alone is not sufficient for nutrition. Allah said: 'They turned away rejoicing' (al-Mutaffifin:31), i.e., enjoying themselves. Enjoyment is an additional matter beyond what maintains the body and its survival. Grapes and fresh dates are related to nourishment, and they suffice for nutrition in some regions. Thus there is an additional matter in them, so by this excess the name 'fruit' does not include them. Therefore, according to Abu Hanifa, he does not break his oath by eating grapes if he did not intend anything. According to them (the two companions), he breaks his oath, and that is the saying of al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him. But if he intended them, he breaks his oath by agreement. There are noble investigations here which we have left out for fear of lengthening. When he finished with literal and metaphorical speech, he began explicit and implicit speech.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the third section of the text, explaining the three modes of using expressions in legal interpretation. The first mode is 'by implication of meaning referring back to the speaker,' exemplified by the 'oath of immediacy' (yamin al-fawr). The commentator clarifies that this oath is uttered in a state of anger, so despite the general wording, it is interpreted as specific to that immediate instance. The second mode is 'by implication of context,' where the Quranic verse 'let him disbelieve' is understood as rebuke rather than a command due to the context of threatening punishment. The third mode is 'by implication of the word itself,' focusing on the etymological derivation. For example, 'meat' implies strength from blood, so fish meat (which lacks blood) is excluded; hence a person swearing not to eat meat does not break the oath by eating fish. Similarly, 'fruit' implies enjoyment beyond nutrition, so grapes and dates (which are nourishing) are not considered fruit in this context. Abu Hanifa's positions are contrasted with the two companions and al-Shafi‘i. The passage also notes that if the swearer intended the specific item, he breaks his oath by consensus.

Key Terms
يمين الفور (yamin al-fawr): An oath uttered in a state of anger, interpreted as applying only to the immediate action despite general wording.
مشكك (mushakkak): A word whose instances vary in the degree of the meaning, so some are deficient and some excessive.
بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه (bi-dalālat al-lafẓ fī nafsih): Implication of the word itself based on its letters and derivation, not its absolute application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of implication by context (siyaq al-nazm) according to the passage?
A) The oath of immediacy where anger restricts the scope.
B) The command 'let him disbelieve' interpreted as rebuke due to the following threat.
C) The exclusion of fish from 'meat' because of etymological derivation.
D) The consensus that if the swearer intended fish, the oath is broken.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul recognizes three modes of textual implication—speaker's state, context, and derivation—that restrict literal meaning to produce specific legal rulings.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Kinayah for Talaq: Deriving Rulings from Implication and Metaphor
Analyzing three kinayah words for talaq and their ruling as revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principles of iqtiḍāʾ and istiʿārah to derive the ruling of revocable talaq from each of the three kinayah expressions, and distinguish the cases of consummated and unconsummated wives.

Core Idea
Kinayah requires intention; necessity implies a prior talaq from the command to observe ʿiddah.

In this lesson, we dive into how Hanafi usul al-fiqh handles indirect expressions for divorce. The passage examines three phrases—'Observe ʿiddah,' 'Purify your womb,' and 'You are one'—and shows how, when the husband intends divorce, they effect a single revocable talaq. The key tools are the principle of necessity (iqtiḍāʾ) and permissible metaphor (istiʿārah) between cause and effect. Understanding this allows you to see how the school derives fiqh rulings from ambiguous expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three kinayah words
List the three expressions: iʿtadī (Observe ʿiddah), istabriʾī raḥimaki (Purify your womb), and anti wāḥidah (You are one). Note that each is a kinayah, not ṣarīḥ, so intention is required.
Kinayah means the wording does not directly say 'talaq' but implies it.
2
Analyze the iqtiḍāʾ argument for iʿtadī
When a husband says 'Observe ʿiddah' to a consummated wife, ʿiddah is only obligatory after talaq. To make the command valid, a prior talaq must be assumed. This necessity (ḍarūrah) establishes revocable talaq (one rajʿī).
The necessity is limited to what suffices—one revocable talaq, not more.
3
Distinguish consummated vs. unconsummated cases
For an unconsummated wife, ʿiddah does not apply, so the phrase cannot carry the same implication. Instead, it is taken as a pure metaphor (istiʿārah) for 'divorce yourself,' still yielding revocable talaq.
This shows the madhhab's flexibility in interpreting expressions based on context.
4
Examine the objection and response about metaphor
An objector claims that metaphor from caused (ʿiddah) to cause (talaq) is generally impermissible. The Hanafi response: since ʿiddah is specific to talaq (excluding other causes like death or emancipation), metaphor is allowed in both directions.
This rebuttal refines the understanding of istiʿārah in usul.
5
Apply the broader principle to hudud
The passage concludes that kinayah requires intention, and this affects criminal law: hudud punishments are not imposed based on kinayah confessions. For example, 'I had intercourse with her' does not incur the hadd of zina without explicit wording.
This reinforces the importance of ṣarīḥ in serious consequences.

القسم الثالث في وجوه استعمال النظم

فإذا نوى الأقراء وزال الإبهام بالنية وجب به الطلاق بعد الدخول اقتضاء، الدخول جعل مستعاراً محضاً عن الطلاق؛ لأنه سببه فاستعير الحكم لسببه، وكذلك قوله: "استبرئي رحمك".
= أي الطلاق وهو الاعتداد المسبب العدة، فإذا غير الحيض من إنعام الله تعالى ويحتمل اعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن العدة الطلاق الرجعي، فإن كانت المرأة مدخولاً بها يثبت به الطلاق اقتضاء: فكأنه قيل: "اعتدي؛ لأني طلَّقتُكِ، أو كوني طالقاً ثم اعتدي، أو طلقي ثم اعتدي"، فلما أمرها بالاعتداد ولم يكن العدة واجباً عليها قبل الطلاق فلا بد من تقدير الطلاق قبل الأمر ضرورة صحة الأمر، والضرورة ترتفع بإثبات أصل الطلاق من غير حاجة إلى إثبات أمر زائد، كالبينونة والزيادة على واحدة، وخلاصة الكلام أن قوله: "اعتدي" معناه الحقيقي الحساب، وهو لا يؤثر في انقطاع النكاح، فلا يمكن العمل بنفسه ما لم يحمل على أحد المعاني المحتملة وهو اعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن نوى هذا المعنى فكأنه أمر باعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن العدة، والمرأة المدخول بها محلّ العدة، ففي حقها يثبت هذا الأمر، وهذا الأمر يتوقف على ثبوت الطلاق قبله ضرورة صحته، والضرورة تقدّر بقدر ما يكفي به، وهو الطلاق بغير وصف البينونة وغيرها، فيثبت الواحدة الرجعية، وإن كانت غير مدخول بها فهي ليست بمحل العدة؛ لأن العدة لا تثبت في حقها فلا يصح في حقها أن يقال: كأنه أمر باعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن العدة، فلا بدّ أن يجعل قوله: "اعتدي" مستعاراً من الطلاق كأنه قال: طلقي نفسك مجازاً، لإطلاق المسبب على السبب، ويثبت من قوله: "طلقي نفسك واحدة" رجعي. سببه أي سبب اعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن العدة لسببه وهو الطلاق، أي فاستعير الاعتداد للطلاق بأن أطلق الاعتداد وأريد به الطلاق. فإن قيل استعارة السبب للمسبب جائز وأما استعارة المسبب للسبب فغير جائز عند الأصوليين كما مر سابقاً، فكيف جوّزتم هنا؟ قلنا: إذا كان المسبب مختصاً بالسبب جازت الاستعارة من الطرفين، وهنا الاعتداد المسبب مختص بسببه الطلاق. فإن قيل: لا نسلم الاختصاص؛ لأن الأمة إذا أعتقت تعتد، وكذا إذا مات زوج المرأة تعتد، فقد وجدت العدة بغير الطلاق؟ قلنا: لا؛ لأن الأمة إنما تعتد تشبيهاً بالطلاق تقديراً، والتي مات زوجها فعدتها في الحقيقة ليست لتعرف براءة الرحم بل لأجل الحداد، ولذا شرعت بالأشهر دون الحيض، فلا يكون في الواقع من العدة.

واعلم أن المرأة إذا كانت مدخولاً بها فيصح في حقها أيضاً أن يراد بقوله: "اعتدي" طلقي استعارة المسبب للسبب كما جاز في غير المدخول بها، فالوجه الأول مخصوص بالمدخول بها، والثاني يشملهما.

استبرئي رحمك هذه كلمة ثانية من الكلمات الثلاثة التي يقع بها الطلاق الرجعي، فقوله: "استبرئ رحمك" يحتمل أن يكون طلب براءة الرحم لأجل الولد، أو لنكاح زوج آخر، فإذا نوى هذا يقع به الطلاق الرجعي.
وقد جاءت به السنة أن النبي قال لسودة "اعتدي" ثم راجعها، وكذلك "أنت واحدة" يحتمل نعتاً للطلقة ويحتمل صفة للمرأة، فإذا زال الإبهام بالنية كان دلالة على الصريح، لا عاملاً بموجبه.
ثم الأصل في الكلام هو الصريح، فأما الكناية ففيها ضرب قصور من حيث إنه يقصر عن البيان بدون النية، وظهر هذا التفاوت فيما يدرأ بالشبهات حتى أن المقرّ على نفسه ببعض الأسباب الموجبة للحد ما لم يذكر اللفظ الصريح لا يستوجب العقوبة.
فإن كانت مدخولاً بها فكأنه قال: استبرئي رحمك لأجل نكاح زوج آخر؛ لأني طلقتك، فيراد الطلاق لضرورة صحة الأمر، وإن كانت غير مدخول بها يكون قوله مستعاراً عن الطلاق، كما مر تقريره في قوله "اعتدي"، فتذكره. وقد جاءت إلخ وهي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال للسودة بنت زمعة: "اعتدي" ثم راجعها. فثبت من هذا أن قوله: "اعتدي" يقع به الطلاق الرجعي حيث راجعها النبي بعد قوله هذا. أنت واحدة: يقع به الطلاق الرجعي، وهذه كلمة ثالثة، وذلك لأن قوله هذا لا يؤثر بنفسه في قطع النكاح، حتى يراد به أحد المعاني المحتملة التي بينه المصنف بقوله: يحتمل إلخ. نعتاً للطلقة فيكون المراد بقوله هذا: "أنت طالق" طلقة واحدة. صفة للمرأة بأن يكون معناه: أنت واحدة في الجمال، أو المال، أو غير ذلك من الأوصاف الحميدة. فإذا أريد به المعنى الأول الصريح: أي صريح الطلاق بناء على أن ذكر الصفة وهي قوله: "واحدة" بالنصب دليل على وجود الموصوف وهو طلقة بمعنى الطلاق؛ لأن تقدير قوله: أنت طالق طلقة واحدة فيقع الطلاق الرجعي كما في الصريح، ولا يكون هذا الكلام في قطع النكاح عاملاً بموجبه. بموجبه: أي بمقتضاه؛ إذ موجبه التوحد، ولا أثر له في قطع النكاح، فيحمل على أحد المعاني كما مر تقريره آنفاً.

ثم الأصل في الكلام هو الصريح لأن وضع الكلام إنما هو للإفهام، والصريح في هذا الأمر أتم حيث يثبت المقصود به بغير النية أيضاً، إنه أي الكناية بتأويل المذكور بدون النية فلا يظهر المراد بغير النية أو ما يقوم مقامها من بالشبهات: وهي الحدود والكفارات، فإنها لا تثبت بالكنايات حتى أن المقر إلخ: فلو قال: إني جامعتُ فلانةً جماعاً حراماً، أو واقعتها، أو وطئتها، لا يجب عليه الحد ما لم يقل: نكتها، أو زنيت بها، وكذا لو قال لأحد: فجرت فلانة أو جامعتها لا يجب عليه حد القذف ما لم يقل: "زنيت بها أو نكتها"، فإنها صريح في القذف.
The third section on the ways of using linguistic structure.

So if he intends [the meaning of] menstruations and ambiguity is removed by intention, then revocable talaq is established by implication after consummation; making [the command] a pure metaphor for talaq because it is its cause, so the ruling is metaphorically applied to its cause. Similarly his statement: 'Purify your womb'.
= That is, talaq, which is the caused 'iddah. So if [she interprets it as] menstruation from the blessings of Allah, and it is possible [to intend] 'iddah of menstruation for completion of the 'iddah, then for the woman who has been consummated with, talaq is established by implication: as if it was said: 'Observe 'iddah because I have divorced you, or be divorced then observe 'iddah, or divorce him then observe 'iddah.' Since he commanded her to observe 'iddah and 'iddah was not obligatory before talaq, it is necessary to assume talaq before the command due to the necessity of the command's validity. Necessity is removed by establishing the basic talaq without needing to establish an extra matter, like final separation and more than one. The summary is that his statement 'Observe 'iddah' has the literal meaning of counting, which has no effect on terminating marriage, so it cannot be acted upon unless it is carried to one of the possible meanings, which is 'iddah of menstruation for completion of the 'iddah. So as if he commanded 'iddah of menstruation for completion of the 'iddah. The woman who has been consummated with is the locus of 'iddah, so this command is established for her. This command depends on the existence of talaq before it for the necessity of its validity. Necessity is estimated by what suffices, which is talaq without the description of final separation, so one revocable talaq is established. If she has not been consummated with, then she is not the locus of 'iddah because 'iddah is not established for her, so it is not valid to say regarding her: 'as if he commanded 'iddah of menstruation for completion of the 'iddah.' Rather, his statement 'Observe 'iddah' must be made a metaphor for talaq, as if he said 'Divorce yourself' metaphorically, by naming the caused for the cause. And from his statement 'Divorce yourself one' a revocable [talaq] is established. Its cause, i.e., the cause of 'iddah of menstruation for completion of the 'iddah is talaq, so the 'iddah is metaphorically applied to talaq. If it is said: Metaphor of cause for caused is permissible, but metaphor of caused for cause is not permissible according to the usuliyyun as previously mentioned, so how do you permit it here? We say: If the caused is specific to its cause, then metaphor is permissible from both sides. Here, the caused 'iddah is specific to its cause talaq. If it is said: We do not admit specificity, because a slave woman when freed observes 'iddah, and likewise if a woman's husband dies she observes 'iddah, so 'iddah exists without talaq? We say: No, because the slave woman observes 'iddah by analogy with talaq in estimation, and the one whose husband dies, her 'iddah is in reality not for knowing the purity of the womb but for mourning; therefore it is legislated with months not menstruation, so it is not actually from 'iddah.

Know that if the woman has been consummated with, it is also valid in her case that by his statement 'Observe 'iddah' he meant 'Divorce' by metaphor of caused for cause, as was permissible for the one not consummated with. So the first interpretation is specific to the consummated, and the second includes both.

'Purify your womb': this is a second word from the three words by which revocable talaq occurs. His statement 'Purify your womb' can mean a request for purity of the womb for the sake of a child, or for another marriage. So if he intends this, revocable talaq occurs.

And the Sunnah has come with it, that the Prophet said to Sawda 'Observe 'iddah' then he took her back. Similarly 'You are one' can be an adjective for the talaq or an attribute of the woman. When ambiguity is removed by intention, it is an indication of the explicit [talaq], not acting according to its [literal] implication.

Then the default in speech is the explicit. As for kinayah, it has a kind of deficiency in that it falls short of clarity without intention. This difference appears in what is prevented by doubts, such that a person who confesses against himself with some of the causes that necessitate punishment, as long as he does not mention the explicit word, does not incur punishment.

If she has been consummated with, then it is as if he said: 'Purify your womb for another marriage because I have divorced you,' so talaq is intended due to the necessity of the command's validity. If she has not been consummated with, his statement is a metaphor for talaq, as established in 'Observe 'iddah', so remember it. And it has come etc., that the Prophet said to Sawda bint Zam'ah: 'Observe 'iddah' then he took her back. So from this it is established that by his statement 'Observe 'iddah' revocable talaq occurs, because the Prophet took her back after this statement. 'You are one': revocable talaq occurs, and this is a third word. This is because this statement does not by itself affect the termination of marriage, until one of the possible meanings that the author explained by saying 'it can be etc.' is intended: an adjective for talaq, so the meaning of this statement is 'You are divorced with one talaq'; an attribute of the woman, meaning that you are one in beauty, wealth, or other praiseworthy qualities. If the first meaning is intended, it is explicit talaq based on the fact that mentioning the attribute 'one' in the accusative is evidence for the existence of the described, which is 'talaq' meaning divorce; because the implicit assumption is 'You are divorced with one talaq', so revocable talaq occurs as in the explicit, and this statement does not in terminating marriage act according to its implication. Its implication: i.e., its requirement, since its requirement is uniqueness, and it has no effect on terminating marriage, so it is carried to one of the meanings as established above.

Then the default in speech is the explicit, because the purpose of speech is communication, and the explicit in this matter is more complete as it establishes the intended without intention also. Indeed, kinayah with interpretation mentioned without intention does not reveal the intended without intention or what stands in its place from doubts: that is, hudud and kaffarat, for they are not established by kinayah. So if someone says: 'I had intercourse with so-and-so in a forbidden manner, or I had sex with her, or I penetrated her,' the hadd punishment is not incumbent on him unless he says: 'I had intercourse (nikah) with her, or I committed zina with her.' Similarly, if he says to someone: 'I committed adultery with so-and-so or had intercourse with her,' the hadd of qadhf is not incumbent on him unless he says: 'I committed zina with her or had intercourse (nikah) with her,' for these are explicit in qadhf.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage, from the third section on the usage of linguistic structures, analyzes three kinayah (allusive) expressions that effect revocable talaq (talāq rajʿī) when intended by the husband: 'Observe 'iddah' (iʿtadī), 'Purify your womb' (istabriʾī raḥimaki), and 'You are one' (anti wāḥidah). The author explains the legal reasoning for each, rooted in the principle of necessity (ḍarūrah) and the permissibility of metaphorical extension (istiʿārah) between cause and effect. For a woman who has been consummated with (madkhūl bihā), the command to observe 'iddah necessarily implies a prior divorce because 'iddah is only obligatory after talaq; thus a revocable talaq is established. For a woman not consummated with, the term 'iddah is inapplicable, so the phrase must be taken as a pure metaphor for 'divorce yourself,' yielding the same result. The author defends this against the objection that metaphor from caused to cause is generally impermissible, arguing that when the caused is specific to the cause (as 'iddah is specific to talaq), metaphor is permissible in both directions. He also addresses counterexamples (slave woman's 'iddah upon freedom, widow's 'iddah) by distinguishing their purposes. The second and third expressions are treated similarly: 'Purify your womb' implies talaq for the consummated woman and is a metaphor for the unconsummated; 'You are one' is understood as 'you are divorced with one talaq' if intended that way. The passage concludes with a broader usul principle: the default in speech is the explicit (ḥaqīqah), while kinayah requires intention; this difference is applied in criminal law where hudud punishments are not imposed based on kinayah alone, exemplified by the distinction between explicit and implicit confessions of zina or qadhf.

Key Terms
اعتدي (iʿtadī): A command to observe the waiting period ('iddah); as a kinayah for divorce, it effects revocable talaq when intended.
كناية (kināyah): An indirect expression that does not explicitly state divorce but implies it, requiring intention to effect talaq.
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit wording that directly effects talaq without need for intention.
اقتضاء (iqtiḍāʾ): Implication; a ruling derived from the necessity of making a command valid.
استعارة (istiʿārah): Metaphorical usage; using a word in a non-literal sense based on a relationship between meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Observe ʿiddah' (iʿtadī) and intends divorce, what type of talaq occurs according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Three irrevocable talaqs
B) One revocable talaq
C) No talaq because it is kinayah
D) One irrevocable talaq

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses iqtiḍāʾ and istiʿārah to derive a ruling of one revocable talaq from kinayah expressions that imply divorce, demonstrating the importance of intention and necessity in legal interpretation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Indication of the Text (Isharat al-Nass) in Hanafi Usul
Defining and exemplifying isharah al-nass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define isharah al-nass, distinguish it from ibarah al-nass, and apply it to derive a Hanafi ruling from the Quranic verse about the Muhajirun.

Core Idea
Isharah al-nass: a binding ruling from the wording, though not the primary intent.

In Hanafi usul, the Quran yields rulings not only from its explicit wording (ibarah) but also from what the wording indicates indirectly (isharah). This lesson explores isharah al-nass through the classic example of the 'poor emigrants' in Surah al-Hashr, showing how the Hanafis derive a ruling on loss of ownership from a verse about booty distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Locate the definition of isharah al-nass in the passage. Note how it is 'established by the wording like the first [ibarah], except that it is not what the speech is intended for.'
The word 'ma' in 'ma siqa' is negative, meaning the ruling is not the primary purpose.
2
Understand the example from Quran 59:8
The verse 'for the poor emigrants...' is primarily about allocating a share of khums. The isharah is that the Muhajirun lost their property, because they are called 'poor' despite having wealth before Hijrah.
Poverty linguistically means loss of ownership (zawal al-milk).
3
Analyze the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i interprets 'poor' metaphorically (majaz). The Hanafis insist on the literal meaning, establishing an indication of property loss.
The dispute is over whether the verse indicates a legal consequence beyond its immediate context.
4
Equal binding force but priority of ibarah
Both ibarah and isharah are qat'i (definitive) in establishing rulings, but ibarah takes precedence when they conflict because it is the intended meaning.
This hierarchy is crucial in Hanafi deduction.
5
Recognize the definitive nature
The ruling derived via isharah is not based on ijtihad but directly from the wording, giving it the same strength as ibarah.
This distinguishes isharah from qiyas.

القسم الرابع في معرفة وجوه الوقوف إشارة النص ودلالة النص] والإشارة ما ثبت بالنظم مثل الأول إلا أنه ما سيق الكلام له، كما في قوله تعالى: أي الحكم الثابت بالأشارة للفقراء المهاجرين الذين الآية، سيق الكلام لبيان إيجاب سهم من الغنيمة لهم، أي في هذا الكلام ففي هذه الآية وفيه إشارة إلى زوال أملاكهم إلى الكفار، وهما سواء في إيجاب الحكم إلا أن الأول أحق عند التعارض، وأما دلالة النص فما ثبت بمعنى النص لغةٌ لا استنباطا بالرأي. المحسوسات أي الثابت بدلالة النص = فدخل فيه القسم الأول فقط، فإن تمسك أحد في إباحة النكاح بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَانْكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم﴾ (النساء:۳)، كان استدلالا بعبارة النص لا بإشارته كذا قيل والإشارة: أي الحكم الثابت بإشارة النص. مثل الأول: أي مثل الحكم الثابت بالأول في أن كلا منها ثابت بالنظم له: أي لذلك الحكم وكلمة "ما" نافية، ومثاله في هو أن ينظر إنسان إلى أحد بقصده ومع ذلك يقع نظره إلى من هو قريبه يميناً ويساراً من غير التفات إليه، فالأول بمنزلة الثابت بعبارة النص والثاني بإشارته. سيق الكلام إلخ لأن قوله تعالى: "للفقراء" بدل من قوله: ﴿وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى وَالْيَتَامَى وَالْمَسَاكِينِ وَابْنِ السَّبِيلِ﴾ (الأنفال: ٤١) بتكرير العامل، أو عطف على الأول بغير واو، كما يقال: "المال" لزيد لبكر، وعلى التقديرين السوق لبيان مصارف الخمس. وحاصل هذه الآية أن الخمس للفقراء المهاجرين الذين أخرجوا من ديارهم وأموالهم. إشارة إلى إلخ لأنه تعالى مع كونهم ذا أموال وأملاك سماهم فقراء، كما قال: ﴿وَلِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِنْ دِيَارِهِمْ وَأَمْوَالِهِمْ﴾ (الحشر : ۸)، وحقيقة الفقر زوال الملك، لا بعد اليد من المال، ولذا يكون ابن السبيل مع بُعد يده عن المال غنيّاً، ويجب عليه الزكاة، فثبت بذلك أن المهاجرين زال ملكهم عن أموالهم بسبب غلبة الكفار واستيلائهم على أموالهم، وفيه خلاف للشافعي الله فهو يرتكب المجاز في هذا الكلام. سواء إلخ : يعني: العبارة والإشارة مستويان في إثبات الحكم؛ إذ الثابت بكل واحد ثابت بنفس النظم، أو في كونهما قطعي الدلالة على المراد، لكن العبارة ترجّح على الإشارة عند التعارض؛ لأنها مقصودة بالسوق بخلاف الثاني.
The fourth section on knowing the types of stopping. Indication of the text and implication of the text. Indication: what is established by the wording like the first except that it is not what the speech is intended for, as in His statement, meaning the ruling established by indication: 'for the poor emigrants who...' the verse, the speech was intended to explain the obligation of a share of the spoils for them, meaning in this speech. So in this verse, and in it is an indication of the loss of their possessions to the disbelievers. And both are equal in establishing the ruling, except that the first has more right in case of conflict. As for the implication of the text: it is what is established by the meaning of the text linguistically, not by deduction through opinion. The sensible objects, meaning what is established by implication of the text. So the first section only enters into it. If someone argues for the permissibility of marriage using His statement: 'So marry what is pleasant for you' (4:3), it would be a deduction by the expression of the text, not by its indication. So it was said. And the indication: meaning the ruling established by the indication of the text. Like the first: meaning like the ruling established by the first in that each of them is established by the wording for it: meaning for that ruling. And the word 'ma' is negative. Its example is that a person looks at someone intentionally, yet his glance falls on his relative to the right or left without turning to him. The first is like what is established by the expression of the text, and the second by its indication. The speech etc. because His statement 'for the poor' is a substitute for His statement 'and for the near relative and the orphans and the needy and the traveler' (8:41) by repeating the governing word, or is a conjunction without 'waw', as it is said: 'the wealth is for Zaid, for Bakr'. On both possibilities, the speech is to explain the expenditures of the khums. The gist of this verse is that the khums is for the poor emigrants who were expelled from their homes and their wealth. An indication etc. because He, despite them having wealth and property, called them poor, as He said: 'And for the poor emigrants who were expelled from their homes and their wealth' (59:8). The reality of poverty is the loss of ownership, not being distant from wealth. For this reason, the wayfarer, despite being distant from his wealth, is considered rich and zakat is obligatory on him. So it is established by that that the emigrants lost ownership of their wealth due to the dominance of the disbelievers and their seizure of their wealth. And there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, for he resorts to metaphor in this speech. 'Equal etc.': meaning: the expression and the indication are equal in establishing the ruling, since what is established by each is established by the wording itself, or they are both definitive in evidence of the intended meaning, but the expression is preferred over the indication in case of conflict because it is intended by the speech, unlike the second.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary defining two types of textual inference from the Quran: isharah al-nass (indication of the text) and dalalah al-nass (implication of the text). The author begins by stating that isharah is what is established by the wording 'like the first' but not intended by the speech. He gives the example of the verse about the poor emigrants (Al-Hashr 59:8). The primary purpose of that verse is to allocate a share of booty, but it also indicates (isharah) that the emigrants lost their property to the disbelievers. This is drawn from the fact that despite having wealth, they are called 'poor', and poverty linguistically means loss of ownership. The author notes that both ibarah (direct wording) and isharah are equal in establishing rulings, but ibarah takes precedence when they conflict. He explains that if someone used the verse 'So marry what is pleasant for you' to prove permissibility of marriage, that would be through ibarah, not isharah. The passage then clarifies that the phrase 'like the first' means like the ruling established by ibarah in that both are from the wording, but isharah is secondary. The example of looking intentionally but also seeing a relative is given as an analogy: the intentional glance is like ibarah, the accidental one is like isharah. The author then engages with a Shafi'i position regarding the verse of the emigrants, where Shafi'i interprets 'poor' metaphorically. The Hanafi position insists on the literal reality of poverty as loss of ownership, thereby establishing the indication. Finally, the passage notes that ibarah and isharah are both definitive (qat'i), but ibarah is preferred because it is the intended meaning.

Key Terms
إشارة النص (isharat al-nass): A ruling derived from the wording of the text but not as the primary intended meaning, yet directly from the expression.
دلالة النص (dalalat al-nass): A ruling derived from the linguistic meaning of the text (not the wording itself) without need for analogy.
عبارة النص (ibarat al-nass): The explicit wording of the text directly giving the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why does the Hanafi school consider that Quran 59:8 indicates the Muhajirun lost their property?
A) The verse explicitly states 'they lost their property'.
B) The literal meaning of poverty is loss of ownership, and the Muhajirun were called 'poor' despite having wealth before Hijrah.
C) There is a hadith that directly states this.
D) It is derived by qiyas from other verses.

Key Takeaway
Isharah al-nass is a definitive Hanafi tool that extracts binding rulings from the secondary implications of Quranic wording, as seen in the property loss indication from 'the poor emigrants' verse.

Distinction between Muqtada and Mahdhuf in Hanafi Usul
Defines and contrasts muqtada and mahdhuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muqtada and mahdhuf in a given Quranic or legal text and apply the Hanafi rule that muqtada does not admit generality.

Core Idea
Muqtada is a meaning required for legal soundness; mahdhuf is an omitted word. Only words admit generality, so muqtada is not general.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction: the difference between what the text requires (muqtada) and what is simply omitted for brevity (mahdhuf). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the principle of generality (umum) in legal interpretation, and it is a point of divergence between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Concepts
Recognize muqtada as the meaning legally required by the text but not explicitly stated, and mahdhuf as a word omitted for brevity with the indication remaining.
Muqtada is a meaning, mahdhuf is a word.
2
Apply the Linguistic Test
If adding the omitted element explicitly changes the meaning or syntax, it is muqtada; if the meaning and syntax remain unchanged, it is mahdhuf. Test with Quranic examples.
Example: 'Ask the village' – adding 'people' changes the object from village to people.
3
Determine the Ruling on Generality
According to early Hanafis, muqtada (a meaning) does not admit generality; mahdhuf (a word) does. The Shafi'i school disagrees and allows generality for muqtada.
Later Hanafis like Shams al-A'imma made an exception for muqtada that is purely for legal soundness.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Development
Later Hanafi scholars allowed generality for muqtada in specific cases, such as 'Divorce yourself' where the estimation is purely to make the speech legally operative.
This narrows the scope of the earlier blanket rule.
5
Analyze the Objections and Responses
The passage presents two counterexamples that appear to challenge the distinctions. Understand how verifiers responded, reinforcing the general principle.
These objections highlight the rigor of Hanafi methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين المقتضى والمحذوف] وقد يُشكل على السامع الفصل بين المقتضى والمحذوف، وهو ثابت لغةً، وآية ذلك أن ما اقتضى غيره ثبت عند صحة الاقتضاء، وإذا كان محذوفاً فقدر مذكوراً أي يقدر مذكورا المتعلق بالقرية أي ينقطع انقطع عن المذكور، كما في قوله تعالى: واسئل القرية، فإن السوال يتحوّل عن القرية إلى المحذوف، وهو الأهل عند التصريح به. فإذا يقدر هنا "الأهل" ثم اختلفوا في قبوله العموم، فذهب أصحابنا إلى عدم عمومه والشافعية إلى عمومه والمتأخرون من أصحابنا کشمس الأئمة وفخر الإسلام وصاحب الميزان لما رأوا أن العموم في بعض أقسامه ثابت (وهو المقدر لتصحيح الكلام كما في قوله لامرأته: طلّقي نفسك، حيث جوزوا نية الثلاث جعلوا المقتضى ما يقدّر لصحة الكلام شرعاً فقط، وجعلوا ما وراءه قسماً واحداً، أو سموه محذوفاً ومضمراً، وجوزوا فيها العموم خاصة، وتابعهم المصنّف في بيان الفرق وإيراد العلامة. لغةً: فالمحذوف ثابت لغةً، والمقتضى شرعاً، ولذا قيل في تعريف المحذوف إنه ما أسقط من الكلام اختصار الدلالة الباقي عليه، فكان ثابتا لغة وآية ذلك أي علامة الفصل. أن ما اقتضى غيره: أي الذي يقتضي غيره وهو المقتضي بالكسر. ثبت عند إلخ: أي يقرّر ويثبت عند وجود المقتضى، يعني الفرق بين المحذوف والمقتضى من وجوه: أحدها ما مرّ، والثاني أن المقتضي بالكسر يقرّ عند التصريح بالمقتضى بالفتح بخلاف المحذوف، فإن عند تصريح المحذوف ينقطع ما يضيف إلى المذكور، ويتعلّق بالمحذوف، وهذا معنى قوله: وإذا كان ما يحتاج إليه المنطوق. انقطع إلخ : أي ينقطع ما يضاف إلى المذكور ويتعلّق بالمحذوف. المحذوف وهو إلخ أقول: تنقض القاعدتان، أما الأخيرة فبقوله تعالى: ﴿فَقُلْنَا اضْرِبْ بِعَصَاكَ الْحَجَرَ (البقرة: ٦٠)، فانفجرت منه اثنتا عشرة عيناً، فإن المحذوف هنا فضرب فانشق الحجر فانفجرت منه هذا لا يتغير الكلام عند التصريح به ولا يتغير الإعراب، وأما الأولى فبقوله : "اعتق" عبدك عنى بألف"، فإنه إن قدر البيع ويقال: بع عبدك عنى وكن وكيلي بالإعتاق، فإنه يتغير الكلام مع أنه مقتضى؛ لأنه كان مأموراً بإعتاق عبده، وبعد التقدير يصير مأمورا بإعتاق عبد الآمر المتكلم، وقد تكلّف في الجواب عنهما بعض المحققين، ولذا قيل: إن الفرق بينهما بالوجه الأول، وهو أن المقتضى شرعي، والمحذوف لغوي.
The difference between muqtada (required meaning) and mahdhuf (elided element): It may be unclear to the listener how to distinguish between muqtada and mahdhuf. Both are established linguistically, but the sign of that is: what requires another is established when the requirement is valid, while if it is mahdhuf, then that which is omitted is estimated as mentioned. For example, in His statement 'Ask the village,' the asking is transferred from the village to the elided element, which is the people, when it is explicitly stated. So here 'the people' is estimated. Then they differed regarding its acceptance of generality: our companions (Hanafis) hold that it has no generality; the Shafi'is hold that it has generality; and the later scholars among our companions, such as Shams al-A'imma, Fakhr al-Islam, and the author of al-Mizan, seeing that generality is established in some of its types (namely what is estimated for the soundness of speech, as in his statement to his wife: 'Divorce yourself,' where they permitted the intention of three), they made muqtada only what is estimated for the soundness of speech legally, and made the rest one category, calling it mahdhuf and mudmar, and they allowed generality specifically in it. The author (musannif) followed them in explaining the difference and presenting the signs. Linguistically: mahdhuf is established linguistically, and muqtada is established legally. Hence it was said in the definition of mahdhuf: it is what is omitted from speech for brevity, with the indication remaining upon it, so it is established linguistically. And the sign of that is what requires another: that which requires another (muqtadi bi al-kasr) is fixed when the required (muqtada bi al-fath) is present. The difference between mahdhuf and muqtada includes: one aspect is what has passed; the second is that the muqtadi (require) is fixed when the muqtada (required) is explicitly mentioned, unlike mahdhuf, for when the mahdhuf is explicitly mentioned, what was added to the mentioned is cut off and attaches to the mahdhuf. That is the meaning of his statement: 'And when what the spoken needs... cuts off.' I say: The two rules are contradicted: the latter by His statement 'Strike the stone with your staff,' for the mahdhuf here is 'he struck' and the stone split and twelve springs gushed forth; the speech does not change when it is explicitly mentioned, nor does the parsing change. The former by his statement 'Free your slave from me for a thousand,' for if sale is estimated and it is said: 'Sell your slave from me and be my agent for emancipation,' then the speech changes even though it is muqtada; because he was commanded to free his slave, and after estimation he becomes commanded to free the slave of the commander/speaker. Some verifiers have strained to answer these, so it is said that the difference between them is by the first aspect: that muqtada is legal, and mahdhuf is linguistic.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a foundational definition in Hanafi usul al-fiqh explaining the distinction between two key terms: muqtada (what is required/implied by the text) and mahdhuf (what is omitted/elided). The commentator begins by noting that differentiating them can be tricky, then provides a linguistic test: if you can explicitly mention the omitted element without changing the syntax or meaning, it is mahdhuf; if mentioning it changes the meaning, it is muqtada. The classic example is 'Ask the village' where 'village' cannot literally be asked, so the required (muqtada) is 'people'. The Hanafi position is that muqtada does not have generality (it is a meaning, not a word), while mahdhuf does. Later Hanafi scholars like Shams al-A'imma allowed generality for muqtada in specific cases where it is purely for legal soundness (e.g., 'Divorce yourself' where intention of three divorces is permissible). The commentator also engages with objections to these distinctions, showing the depth of Hanafi methodology. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that these distinctions are critical for correct legal interpretation.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): What is legally required by the text but not explicitly stated; its existence is inferred for legal soundness.
المحذوف (al-mahdhuf): What is omitted from speech for brevity, but the indication remains upon it; it can be explicitly stated without changing the meaning or syntax.
العموم (al-‘umūm): Generality; applying to all individuals of a category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to early Hanafi scholars, muqtada (the required meaning) does not admit generality because:
A) It is a word
B) It is a meaning
C) It is an omitted word
D) It is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Muqtada is a legally required meaning, not a word, and therefore does not admit generality; mahdhuf is an omitted word that does admit generality.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Muqtada Does Not Admit Specification
States the rule that what is established by muqtada cannot be specified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why muqtada does not admit specification according to Hanafi usul, apply the rule to oath examples, and contrast the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Muqtada is a necessary meaning, not a word, so it has no generality and cannot be specified.

This lesson introduces a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: what is established by the muqtada (the meaning required by the text) does not admit specification. The principle has direct practical consequences, such as in oaths. We will examine the rational proof and the disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of muqtada
Muqtada is the meaning that a text necessarily implies for the ruling to be sound. It is not an explicit word but a derived meaning.
Example: 'I will not drink' implies any drinkable substance.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof that muqtada has no generality
The proof states that generality (umum) and specification (takhsis) are accidents (ʿawāriḍ) of words, not meanings. Since muqtada is a meaning, it lacks the property of generality.
The operative phrase: 'العموم والخصوص من عوارض الألفاظ والمقتضى معنى لا لفظ'
3
Apply the rule to the oath example
If someone swears 'I will not drink' and intends a specific drink, that intention is invalid. The muqtada covers all drinks, so any drink breaks the oath.
Contrast with the Shafi'i view where intention might specify.
4
Contrast the Shafi'i position and practical difference
Shafi'is treat muqtada as a word that can be general and specifiable. The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the oath is broken by any drink; under Shafi'i, only if the intended drink (e.g., water) is consumed.
Know the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الثابت بمقتضى النص لا يحتمل التخصيص، حتى لو حلف: "لا يشرب" ونوى به شراباً دون شراب، لاتعمل نيته؛ لأن المقتضى لا عموم له خلافاً للشافعي؛ لأن التخصيص فيما يحتمل العموم، وكذلك الثابت بدلالة النص لا يحتمل التخصيص؛ لأن معنى النص إذا ثبت كونه علة لا يحتمل أن يكون غير علة. وهو محال،
Then what is established by the muqtada (required meaning) of the text does not admit specification, so if one swears 'I will not drink' and intends thereby a specific drink but not another, his intention is not effective, because muqtada has no generality—contrary to al-Shafi'i—because specification applies only to what admits generality. Similarly, what is established by the indication (dalalah) of the text does not admit specification, because the meaning of the text, if it is established as a cause ('illah), it cannot be other than a cause, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This is a rule in Hanafi usul: the meaning required by a text (muqtada) cannot be specified or restricted. The example given is an oath: 'I will not drink' — the muqtada here is 'any drinkable substance' since drinking cannot occur without something drunk. If the swearer intends only one type of drink (e.g., water but not milk), that intention is invalid under Hanafi law. The reason is that muqtada is a meaning derived necessarily from the text; it is not a word that can be general or specific. Specification only applies to words that admit generality. The Shafi'i school disagrees, holding that muqtada can be general and thus can be specified. The commentator also extends this rule to dalalah (indication): if the text indicates a cause (like 'uff' indicating harm), that cause is fixed and cannot be specified—wherever the cause exists, the ruling applies fully.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): The meaning required by the text for legal soundness.
التخصيص (al-takhṣīṣ): Specification; restricting a general ruling to some individuals or circumstances.
دلالة النص (dalālat al-naṣṣ): Indicative meaning of the text; the implied cause or ratio legis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not drink' and intends not to drink water but is okay drinking milk, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) His intention is valid; he only breaks the oath if he drinks water.
B) His intention is invalid; any drink breaks the oath.
C) The oath is invalid because no specific drink was mentioned.
D) He must specify his intention at the time of swearing.

Key Takeaway
Muqtada, a necessary meaning, has no generality and thus cannot be specified—a key Hanafi usul distinction from Shafi'i.

The 'Illah Established by Dalalah Cannot Be Specified
Extends the rule that indicative meaning cannot be specified to the cause derived from text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the 'illah derived from dalalah is fixed and cannot be specified, and justify it using the rational proof.

Core Idea
A cause cannot be a cause if it is applied selectively; once the text indicates a cause, it binds universally.

This short rule extends the principle that what is established by the indication of the text (dalalah) cannot be specified. Just as the muqtada (demanded thing) is binding, so too is the effective cause ('illah) that the text points to. Understanding this helps avoid arbitrarily limiting a divine ruling's scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the rule: what is established by dalalah of the text does not admit specification (takhsis).
The rule applies specifically to the 'illah indicated by the text, not the explicit wording.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is rational: if a text's meaning is established as a cause, it cannot be other than a cause. This is because a cause by definition necessitates its effect.
The proof is a rational necessity (dalil aqli), not transmitted text.
3
Understand the reasoning
If a cause could be specified (i.e., present in some instances but not others), it would contradict its nature as a cause. Therefore, specification is impossible.
This is the core of the argument: a cause is a single, consistent factor.
4
Apply with an example
Take the verse prohibiting saying 'uff' to parents. The Hanafi rule holds that the cause is harming parents. Since the cause is fixed, the prohibition extends to all forms of harm, not just saying 'uff'.
This demonstrates the practical effect of the rule.
5
Distinguish from textual specification
Specification (takhsis) can apply to the explicit wording of a text (e.g., specifying a general command), but it cannot apply to the cause (illah) that the text indicates by implication.
The rule is about the cause, not the explicit ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الثابت بدلالة النص لا يحتمل التخصيص؛ لأن معنى النص إذا ثبت كونه علة لا يحتمل أن يكون غير علة. وهو محال،
Likewise, what is established by the indication (dalalah) of the text does not admit specification, because the meaning of the text, if it is established as a cause ('illah), it cannot be other than a cause, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This short but important rule complements the previous: just as muqtada cannot be specified, the 'illah (cause) derived from a text via dalalah (indication) also cannot be specified. The reasoning is that once the text indicates a certain meaning as the effective cause for a ruling (e.g., the prohibition of saying 'uff' to parents indicates that the cause is causing harm), that cause is fixed and cannot be limited to only some instances. If one were to say the cause exists in some cases but not others, it would contradict its being a cause. For example, in the verse 'Do not say to them 'uff'' the cause is harming parents; this 'illah is present whenever harm occurs, so the prohibition applies universally to all forms of harm, not just saying 'uff'. Specification would require the cause to sometimes produce the ruling and sometimes not, which is impossible for a true cause.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalālat al-naṣṣ): The implied meaning of a text that indicates a legal cause ('illah).
العلة (al-‘illah): The effective cause (ratio legis) for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school hold that the dalalah (indicative meaning) of a text cannot be specified?
A) Because the text itself specifies the limits.
B) Because the cause ('illah) is a unified meaning that cannot be partially applied.
C) Because specification only applies to words, not meanings.
D) Because the Hanafis reject all forms of specification.

Key Takeaway
The 'illah established by dalalat al-nass is fixed and cannot be specified; this ensures the legal cause applies universally to all instances where it exists.

Does Isharah Admit Takhsis? The Hanafi Internal Dispute
Presenting the dispute over whether allusive meaning admits specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on whether isharah admits takhsis and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a specific verse example.

Core Idea
Isharah stands on wording, but its generality is debated: if the text wasn't meant for it, can it be specified?

In this passage, the author presents a subtle but important internal disagreement among Hanafi usulis: does the allusive meaning (isharah) of a text admit specification (takhsis)? The answer turns on whether isharah is genuinely general in scope, given that it is not the primary intent of the speech. We will walk through the arguments of Qadi Abu Zaid and Shams al-A'immah, and see how the disagreement plays out in real fiqh examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested concept
What is isharah? How does it differ from ibarah? Isharah is a meaning derived by implication, not the primary intent of the text.
2
State the two positions
Qadi Abu Zaid: isharah does NOT admit takhsis. Shams al-A'immah: isharah DOES admit takhsis.
3
Trace the reasoning
Qadi Abu Zaid: Generality requires the speech to be for that purpose; isharah is additional, so no generality. Shams al-A'immah: Isharah is established by wording, like ibarah, so it can be general and specified.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Use the martyr verse example: under Qadi Abu Zaid, the isharah that martyrs are not prayed upon cannot be specified; under Shams al-A'immah, it can be specified (Hamzah exception).
The second example about the father's right also illustrates the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثابت بإشارة النص فيحتمل أن يكون عاماً يخص؛ لأنه ثابت بصيغة الكلام، والعموم باعتبار الصيغة. وأما الثابت بإشارة إلخ اختلف في هذا، فقال القاضي أبو زيد ومن سلك مسلكه: هو أيضاً لا يحتمل التخصيص؛ لأن العموم إنما يوجد فيما يكون سياق الكلام لأجله، فأما ما يقع الإشارة إليه فهو زيادة على المطلوب بالنص، فإذا لم يوجد العموم لا يوجد التخصيص ؛ لأنه فرعه. وقال شمس الأئمة وأتباعه ومنهم إنه يحتمل ذلك؛ لأن الثابت بالإشارة مثل الثابت بالعبارة من حيث إنه ثابت بصيغة الكلام، فكما أن الثابت بالعبارة يحتمل العموم فيمكن أن يخص كذا هذا. المصنف: ومثال إشارة المخصوص البعض قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقُولُوا لِمَنْ يُقْتَلُ فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ أَمْوَاتٌ بَلْ أَحْيَاءٌ وَلَكِنْ لَا تَشْعُرُونَ﴾ [البقرة:154]، فإن هذا الكلام سيق لعلوّ درجات الشهداء، ويفهم منه بطريق الإشارة أنه لا يصلى عليهم؛ لأنهم أحياء، كما ذهب إليه الشافعي لله، لكن خص منه حمزة بما روى أحمد عن عبد الله ابن مسعود له أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم صلّى على حمزة سبعين صلاةً، وأما مثاله على مذهبنا فهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الْمَوْلُودِ لَهُ﴾ [البقرة:233]، فإن هذا الكلام يدلّ بطريق الإشارة على أن للأب حق التملك في مال ولده، فخص منه وطء الأب جارية ولده، فإنه لا يحلّ حتى تجب عليه قيمتها على ما عرف.
As for what is established by isharah (allusion) of the text, it may be general and then specified, because it is established by the wording of the speech, and generality is due to the wording. As for what is established by isharah, there is disagreement on this. Al-Qadi Abu Zaid and those who follow his path said: it also does not admit takhsis, because generality exists only in what the context of speech is intended for. As for what is alluded to, it is additional to what is sought by the text, so if there is no generality, there is no takhsis, because takhsis is its branch. Shams al-A'immah and his followers, including the author, said that it does admit takhsis, because what is established by isharah is like what is established by ibarah (explicit wording) in that it is established by the wording of the speech. So just as what is established by ibarah admits generality, it can be specified. The author: An example of isharah specifying some [individuals] is His statement ﴿Do not say to those killed in the way of Allah 'dead,' rather they are alive, but you do not perceive﴾ [al-Baqarah:154]. This speech is intended to elevate the ranks of martyrs, and from it is understood by way of isharah that funeral prayer is not performed over them, because they are alive, as al-Shafi'i held. However, Hamzah was excluded from this based on what Ahmad narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas‘ud that the Prophet ﷺ performed seventy funeral prayers over Hamzah. As for an example according to our school, it is His statement ﴿And upon the one to whom he is born﴾ [al-Baqarah:233]. This speech indicates by way of isharah that the father has the right to own his child's property. Then from this was excluded the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, for it is not permissible unless he owes her value, as is known.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage discusses isharah (allusion), i.e., a meaning inferred from the wording of the text but not its primary purpose. The author first states that it may be general and then specified. Then he presents a disagreement among Hanafis. Qadi Abu Zaid and his followers argue that isharah does not admit takhsis because the text is not primarily intended to convey the alluded meaning; hence it lacks the generality of wording necessary for takhsis. On the other hand, Shams al-A'immah and the author hold that isharah is established by the wording, so just like explicit wording (ibarah), it can be general and then specified. The text provides two examples: the Shafi'i example from the martyr verse, where the isharah is that martyrs are alive and thus not prayed upon—but this was specified for Hamzah. The Hanafi example is from the verse about maintenance, where the isharah indicates the father's right to his child's property, but it is specified regarding intercourse with the son's slave-girl. The lesson illustrates the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on isharah and demonstrates practical applications.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-isharah): An indication from the wording of the text that is not the primary intended meaning, but is understood as a secondary implication.
القاضي أبو زيد (al-Qadi Abu Zaid): A prominent Hanafi jurist from Transoxiana who held that isharah does not admit takhsis.
شمس الأئمة (Shams al-A'immah): A title for the Hanafi scholar al-Sarakhsi, who held that isharah admits takhsis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shams al-A'immah, why does isharah admit takhsis?
A) Because it is based on the wording of the text, like ibarah.
B) Because it is purely rational and not dependent on wording.
C) Because it is always general by consensus.
D) Because it is part of the primary intent of the text.

Key Takeaway
Isharah, although secondary, is established by wording, and according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, it admits specification.

Can Indication (Ishara) Be Specified? Hanafi Internal Dispute
Internal Hanafi dispute on specification of isharat al-nass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on specification of isharat al-nass and apply them to derive rulings from textual examples.

Core Idea
Hanafis disagree: ishara shares the wording’s generality, but some say it is incidental and lacks it.

In Hanafi usul, rulings can be derived not only from explicit statements but also from indications (ishara) within the text. But can these implied rulings be restricted by specification (takhsis)? This lesson explores an internal dispute between two great Hanafi usulis—Qadi Abu Zaid al-Dabusi and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi—on this very question, with concrete examples from the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Read the first two lines of the Arabic passage. The author states that what is established by ishara may be general and specifiable. Then he introduces an internal Hanafi disagreement. Note the exact point of contention: whether rulings from isharat al-nass can be specified (takhsis).
2
Analyze Qadi Abu Zaid’s position
Qadi Abu Zaid and his followers argue that ishara cannot be specified. Their proof: generality (umum) exists only when the speech’s primary context is directed at that general meaning. Since ishara is incidental and not the main purpose, it does not carry the property of umum, and thus cannot be specified.
Key phrase: 'العموم إنما يوجد فيما يكون سياق الكلام لأجله'
3
Analyze Shams al-A'imma’s position
Shams al-A'imma and his followers hold that ishara can be specified. Their proof: what is established by ishara is still established by the wording (sigha) of the speech, just like explicit statement (ibara). Since ibara admits umum and takhsis, so does ishara.
Key phrase: 'لأن الثابت بالإشارة مثل الثابت بالعبارة من حيث إنه ثابت بصيغة الكلام'
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf through examples
The author gives two examples of specification (takhsis) applied to rulings derived from ishara. First, the verse on martyrs indicates no funeral prayer (ishara), but Hamza was specified out. Second, the verse on maintenance indicates father’s ownership of child’s property (ishara), but intercourse with the son’s slave-girl is specified out. These examples show that actual specification occurs, favoring Shams al-A'imma’s view.
The examples are from al-Baqara 154 and 233.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and practical impact
The passage does not explicitly state which position is mufta bihi, but the inclusion of examples where specification actually happened suggests that Shams al-A'imma’s view is stronger or more widely applied. The practical impact: if ishara can be specified, then many implied rulings are open to restriction by evidence; if not, they remain absolute.
In Hanafi fatwa, the position that ishara can be specified is generally relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثابت بإشارة النص فيحتمل أن يكون عاماً يخص؛ لأنه ثابت بصيغة الكلام، والعموم باعتبار الصيغة. وأما الثابت بإشارة إلخ اختلف في هذا، فقال القاضي أبو زيد ومن سلك مسلكه: هو أيضاً لا يحتمل التخصيص؛ لأن العموم إنما يوجد فيما يكون سياق الكلام لأجله، فأما ما يقع الإشارة إليه فهو زيادة على المطلوب بالنص، فإذا لم يوجد العموم لا يوجد التخصيص ؛ لأنه فرعه. وقال شمس الأئمة وأتباعه ومنهم إنه يحتمل ذلك؛ لأن الثابت بالإشارة مثل الثابت بالعبارة من حيث إنه ثابت بصيغة الكلام، فكما أن الثابت بالعبارة يحتمل العموم فيمكن أن يخص كذا هذا. المصنف: ومثال إشارة المخصوص البعض قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقُولُوا لِمَنْ يُقْتَلُ فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ أَمْوَاتٌ بَلْ أَحْيَاءٌ وَلَكِنْ لَا تَشْعُرُونَ (البقرة: ١٥٤)، فإن هذا الكلام سيق لعلوّ درجات الشهداء، ويفهم منه بطريق الإشارة أنه لا يصلى عليهم؛ لأنهم أحياء، كما ذهب إليه الشافعي لله، لكن خص منه حمزة بما روى أحمد عن عبد الله ابن مسعود له أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم صلّى على حمزة سبعين صلاةً، وأما مثاله على مذهبنا فهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الْمَوْلُودِ لَهُ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۳۳)، فإن هذا الكلام يدلّ بطريق الإشارة على أن للأب حق التملك في مال ولده، فخص منه وطء الأب جارية ولده، فإنه لا يحلّ حتى تجب عليه قيمتها على ما عرف
As for what is established by the indication of the text, it may be general and subject to specification, because it is established by the wording of the speech, and generality is based on the wording. As for the text established by indication [the author says], there is disagreement on this. Qadi Abu Zaid and those who follow his path said: It also cannot be specified, because generality only exists where the context of speech is intended for it. As for that which is indicated by allusion, it is an addition to what is sought by the text. So if there is no generality, there is no specification, because specification is a branch of it. Shams al-A'imma and his followers, among them, said that it can be specified, because what is established by indication is like what is established by explicit statement insofar as it is established by the wording of the speech. Just as what is established by explicit statement admits generality, so it can be specified. The author: An example of indication specifying a part is the Almighty's saying: 'And do not say of those who are killed in the way of Allah, 'They are dead.' Rather, they are alive, but you perceive [it] not' (al-Baqara 154). This speech was intended for the high ranks of martyrs, and it is understood by way of indication that funeral prayer is not performed over them, because they are alive, as al-Shafi'i held. But Hamza was specified from it by what Ahmad narrated from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet ﷺ prayed over Hamza seventy times. As for an example according to our school, it is the Almighty's saying: 'And upon the one to whom he is born' (al-Baqara 233). This speech indicates by way of indication that the father has the right of ownership over his child's wealth. So from it was specified the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, for it is not permissible until its value is wajib upon him, as is known.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the nature of rulings derived through isharat al-nass (indication of the text). The issue is whether such rulings can be subject to takhsis (specification). Qadi Abu Zaid al-Dabusi represents one position: rulings from isharat al-nass cannot be specified because generality (umum) only exists where the text's primary purpose (siyaq al-kalam) is to convey a general rule. Isharat al-nass is secondary and incidental, so it does not carry the quality of umum. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and others argue that since the ishara is still established through the same linguistic form (sigha), it shares the same potential for umum as the explicit statement (ibara). The author provides examples: The verse on martyrs (al-Baqara 154) indicates by way of ishara that martyrs should not be prayed over. Al-Shafi'i used this to derive that no funeral prayer is offered for martyrs. However, this was specified for Hamza because the Prophet ﷺ did pray over him. According to the Hanafi school, an example is the verse on child maintenance (al-Baqara 233) which indicates that a father can own his child's property. This is specified to exclude the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, as that is not permissible without compensating the value. The passage illustrates how the Hanafi usul tradition handles secondary textual indications and their interaction with specification.

Key Terms
إشارة النص (isharat al-nass): The indication derived from the text by way of implication, not explicitly stated.
تخصيص (takhsis): The restriction of a general ruling to exclude some of its potential applications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shams al-A'imma, why can rulings derived from isharat al-nass be specified?
A) Because they are based on consensus.
B) Because they are established by the same wording that admits generality.
C) Because they are weaker than explicit statements.
D) Because they are always general.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, even implied rulings (ishara) can be general and subject to specification, as shown by the examples of Hamza and the father's ownership.

Hanafi Refutation of Mafhum al-Mukhalafa
Shafi'i classification of dalala and Hanafi refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mafhum al-muwafaqa and mafhum al-mukhalafa and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the latter while accepting the former.

Core Idea
Hanafis accept only implied meanings that agree with the text; opposite implications (mafhum al-mukhalafa) are invalid.

The Shafi'i school divides textual indication into explicit (mantiq) and implied (mafhum), further splitting implied into agreement (muwafaqa) and opposition (mukhalafa). The Hanafi school, however, only accepts the implied meaning that aligns with the text, which they call dalalat al-nass. This lesson explains that stance and the rationale behind rejecting mafhum al-mukhalafa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i classification
Understand the Shafi'i division of dalala into mantiq (explicit) and mafhum (implied), and the two subtypes of mafhum: muwafaqa and mukhalafa.
Mantiq corresponds to Hanafi ibara, ishara, and iqtida. Mafhum al-muwafaqa corresponds to Hanafi dalalat al-nass.
2
Contrast with Hanafi categories
Note that Hanafis do not use mantiq/mafhum as primary categories. Their equivalent is ibara, ishara, iqtida, and dalalat al-nass.
Dalalat al-nass is the only implied meaning accepted.
3
Examine the dispute over mafhum al-mukhalafa
The Shafi'is accept mafhum al-mukhalafa as proof; Hanafis reject it because specification does not necessarily negate the ruling for other cases.
The key epistemological issue: does mentioning a condition or description imply that the ruling is limited to that case?
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the hadith 'Fi al-ghanam al-sa'ima zakat' (grazing sheep have zakat). Shafi'is infer non-grazing sheep have no zakat; Hanafis do not accept this inference.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the practical difference in ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل واعلم أن عامة الأصوليين من أصحاب الشافعي الله قسموا الدلالة إلى منطوق ومفهوم، وقالوا: المنطوق ما دلّ عليه اللفظ في محلّ النطق، وهذا يشمل ما سميناه عبارةً أو إشارة أو اقتضاء، والمفهوم ما دلّ عليه اللفظ، لا في محلّ النطق. ثم المفهوم عندهم نوعان: مفهوم موافقة، وهو أن يفهم من اللفظ حال المسكوت عنه على وفق المنطوق، وهذا ما سميناه دلالة النص، ومفهوم مخالفة، وهو أن يفهم منه حاله خلاف ما فهم من المنطوق. ثم المفهوم المخالف على أنواع؛ لأنه إن فهم من اسم العلم فهو مفهوم اللقب، وإن فهم من الشرط أو الوصف فهو مفهوم الشرط أو الوصف، وتلك الأقسام من المفهوم فاسدة عندنا، لايصح الاحتجاج بها كما قال المصنف.
Section: Know that most usuli scholars among the followers of al-Shafi'i divided dalala into mantiq and mafhum. They said: Mantiq is what the wording indicates at the place of utterance, and this includes what we have called ibara, ishara, and iqtida. Mafhum is what the wording indicates, not at the place of utterance. Then mafhum according to them is of two types: mafhum al-muwafaqa, which is understanding from the wording the case of the unmentioned in agreement with the mentioned, and this is what we call dalalat al-nass; and mafhum al-mukhalafa, which is understanding from it a case contrary to what is understood from the utterance. Then mafhum al-mukhalafa is of several types: if it is understood from a proper name, it is mafhum al-laqab; if from a condition or description, it is mafhum al-shart or mafhum al-wasf. All these types of mafhum are invalid according to us; it is not permissible to use them as proof, as the author said.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This section explains the Shafi'i classification of textual indication into mantiq (explicit) and mafhum (implied), and the Hanafi rejection of mafhum al-mukhalafa. The Shafi'is define mantiq as what the text indicates at the place of utterance, which corresponds to the Hanafi categories of ibara (explicit statement), ishara (indication), and iqtida (implication). Mafhum is what the text implies beyond the locus of utterance. They subdivide mafhum: mafhum al-muwafaqa (also called fahwa al-khitab or dalalat al-nass) is where the unmentioned case follows the ruling of the mentioned case in agreement, e.g., the verse forbidding saying 'uff' to parents implies a prohibition on beating them. Mafhum al-mukhalafa is where the unmentioned case takes the opposite ruling, e.g., if a condition is stated, its absence negates the ruling. The Shafi'is accept both types as valid proofs. The Hanafi school, however, completely rejects mafhum al-mukhalafa (including mafhum al-laqab, mafhum al-shart, and mafhum al-wasf). They consider these invalid because they rely on absence of a condition or description, which does not necessitate the opposite ruling. The only implied meaning Hanafis accept is dalalat al-nass (what we call dalala), which corresponds to mafhum al-muwafaqa of the Shafi'is. The author affirms this position, stating that these types of mafhum are fasid (invalid) according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
منطوق (mantiq): What the wording indicates at the place of utterance; explicit meaning.
مفهوم (mafhum): What the wording indicates not at the place of utterance; understood meaning.
مفهوم موافقة (mafhum al-muwafaqa): Implied meaning in agreement with the explicit ruling for the unmentioned case.
مفهوم مخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafa): Implied meaning opposite to the explicit ruling for the unmentioned case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a valid type of meaning according to Hanafi usul?
A) Mantiq
B) Mafhum al-muwafaqa
C) Mafhum al-mukhalafa
D) Dalalat al-nass

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts only mafhum al-muwafaqa (implied agreement) as valid proof, rejecting mafhum al-mukhalafa (implied opposition) due to the unreliability of inferring opposite rulings from unspecified conditions.
