Invalid Methods: Specification by Proper Name and Conditional Description
Refutation of two Shafi'i inference methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why the Hanafi school rejects inference from proper names (mafhum al-laqab) and from conditional/descriptive phrases (mafhum al-shart/al-wasf) as valid methods of textual interpretation.

Core Idea
A proper name does not negate; a condition does not exclude. Only explicit textual methods bind.

In this passage, the author tackles two arguments that some scholars use to derive rulings from texts. The first relies on proper names, the second on conditions or descriptions. Both are rejected by the Hanafi school as invalid methods. Understanding why reveals the Hanafi commitment to explicit textual cues (ibara, ishara, dalala, iqtida) and their caution about implied meanings that lack clear textual support.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two invalid methods
State the two methods: (1) specification by proper name (al-tansis bi-ism al-'ilm), which claims that a ruling tied to a proper name applies only to that named entity; (2) conditioning by a condition or description (al-ta'liq bi-shart aw wasf), which claims that the ruling is negated in the absence of that condition or description.
These are forms of mafhum al-mukhalafa rejected by the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the Hanafi refutation for proper names
The author argues that a proper name does not imply anything about others. The example 'Zaid came' does not tell us about Amr. Extending this to sacred texts would lead to absurdity: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would imply that others are not messengers, which is kufr.
The Ansar's understanding of 'Water is from water' was based on the definite article 'al-', not the proper name itself.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation for conditional/descriptive phrases
The condition or description does not automatically negate the ruling in its absence. The hadith 'On grazing sheep, zakat' does not prove that non-grazing sheep are exempt; zakat may be established by other evidence such as qiyas on general principles of wealth.
The Shafi'i position leads to restricting rulings unnecessarily without explicit textual support.
4
Recognize the practical consequence and benefit
The thamarah al-khilaf: in marriage, if freedom is stated as a condition, Shafi'is infer that marrying a slave-girl is impermissible when the condition is absent; Hanafis do not negate the ruling but seek other evidence. The benefit of rejecting these methods is that the mujtahid can then use qiyas for unmentioned cases and attain the level of ijtihad.
The Hanafi approach preserves the use of qiyas and other valid methods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجوه الفاسدة العمل بالنصوص بوجوه أخر] فصل: ومن الناس من عمل في النصوص بوجوه أخر، هي فاسدة عندنا، منها ما قال بعضهم: إن التنصيص على الشيء باسمه العلم يوجب التخصيص ونفي الحكم عما عداه، وهذا فاسد؛ لأن النص لم يتناوله، فكيف يوجب الحكم فيه نفياً أو إثباتاً، أي قولهم المذكور ومنها ما قال الشافعي له: إن الحكم متى علّق بشرط، أو أضيف إلى مسمّى بوصف خاص أوجب نفي الحكم عند عدم الشرط أو الوصف، ولهذا لم يُجَوّز نكاحَ الأمة عند فوات الشرط، جنس بوجوه أخر غير ما ذكرنا من العبارة والإشارة والدلالة والاقتضاء بعضهم وهو أبو بكر الدقاق وأبو حامد وبعض الحنابلة والأشعرية التنصيص على الشيء باسمه العلم، أي بالاسم الذي ليس بصفة، سواء كان اسم كالماء في حديث الغسل، أو علما كزيد. يوجب التخصيص إلخ والحاصل أن الحكم على العلم يدلّ على نفيه عن غيره عند هؤلاء القوم، كقوله عليه: "إنما الماء من الماء [مسلم رقم ٧٧٥] والمراد بالماء الأول الغسل، وبالثاني المني، فلما نص عليها بالماء هو علم فَهِمَ الأنصارُ عدم وجوب الاغتسال بالإكسال لعدم الماء، فلو لم يدلّ على النفي عما عداه لما فهموا ذلك. نفياً أو إثباتا: يعني لما لم يتناول النص المسكوت عنه فكيف يثبت الحكم فيه نفياً أو إثباتاً. فإنك إذا قلت: جاء زيد فهذا الكلام لا يتناول غيره ،كعمرو، فلا يدلّ على أن عمرًا لم يجئ أو جاء، وإلا يلزم الكفر في قولك: "محمد" رسول الله ، فإنه على هذا يدلّ على أن غيره ليس ،برسول، وهو كفر، ففائدة التخصيص أن يتأمل المجتهد فيه فيثبت الحكم في غيره بالقياس وينال درجة الاجتهاد. وأما استدلال الأنصار فهو بحرف الاستغراق الذي هو اللام؛ لأن الأصل فيه الاستغراق عند عدم العهد، فيكون المعنى جميع أفراد الغسل من المنّي، يعني جميع أفراد الغسل الذي يتعلق بالشهوة منحصر في الماء، فقول "الماء" قد يثبت عياناً بأن ينزل في النوم أو اليقظة بالوطء أو بغيره، وقد يثبت دلالةً بأن يقام دليله وهو التقاء الختانين ،مقامه، كما يقام السفر مقام المشقة. متى علّق بشرط كما يكون في مفهوم الشرط. بوصف خاص كما يكون في مفهوم الوصف بأن يكون عاما ولكنه قيد بوصف مختص بالبعض كقوله عليه: " في الغنم السائمة "زكاة" [جامع العلوم والحكم]، فإن اسم الغنم عام في جنسه، ووصف السوم مختص ببعضه لا بكلّه أوجب التعليق في الأول، والإضافة في الثاني.
Section on invalid methods: Acting upon texts by other ways. Section: Among people are those who act upon texts by other ways, which are invalid according to us. Among them is what some said: Specification of a thing by its proper name necessitates specification and negation of the ruling from what is other than it. This is invalid because the text does not include it, so how can it necessitate a ruling, either negative or affirmative? That is their saying mentioned. And among them is what al-Shafi'i said: When a ruling is conditioned by a condition, or added to a name by a specific description, it necessitates negation of the ruling in the absence of the condition or description. For this reason, he did not permit marriage to a slave-girl when the condition is absent. [Furthermore], some others (Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq, Abu Hamid, and some Hanbalis and Ash'aris) (contend that) specification of a thing by its proper name, i.e., by a name that is not a description, whether it is a noun like 'water' in the hadith of ghusl, or a proper name like Zaid, necessitates specification, etc. The gist is that the ruling on the proper name indicates its negation from other than it according to these people. For example, his saying: 'Water is from water' (Muslim no. 775). By the first 'water' is meant ghusl, and by the second, semen. When he specified the ruling by 'water' which is a proper noun, the Ansar understood that there is no obligation of ghusl after ejaculation (if there is no water) because of the absence of water. If it did not indicate negation from other than it, they would not have understood that. 'Negatively or positively' means: Since the text does not include the unspoken, how can it establish a ruling for it, either negative or positive? For if you say 'Zaid came', this statement does not include others like 'Amr', so it does not indicate that Amr did not come or came. Otherwise, it would necessitate kufr in your saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', because according to this it would indicate that others are not messengers, which is kufr. The benefit of specification is that the mujtahid reflects upon it and then establishes the ruling for others by qiyas and attains the level of ijtihad. As for the argument of the Ansar, it is through the letter of generality which is 'al-', because the original in it is generality when there is no covenant. So the meaning is all instances of ghusl from semen, i.e., all instances of ghusl related to desire are confined to water. So the word 'water' may be established factually, by discharging in sleep or wakefulness, through intercourse or otherwise, and may be established by indication, by setting up its evidence, which is the meeting of the two circumcised parts, in its place, just as travel is set up in place of hardship. 'When it is conditioned by a condition' as in mafhum al-shart. 'By a specific description' as in mafhum al-wasf, where it is general but restricted by a description specific to some, like his saying: 'On grazing sheep, zakat' (Jami' al-'Ulum wa al-Hikam). The word 'sheep' is general in its genus, and the description 'grazing' is specific to some, not all. So conditioning necessitates in the first, and addition in the second.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two invalid methods of textual inference attributed to some scholars (including al-Shafi'i and others). The first method is 'specification by proper name' (al-tansis 'ala al-shay' bi-ismihi al-'ilm). Some claim that when a ruling is attached to a specific noun (e.g., 'water' in the hadith 'Water is from water'), it indicates that the ruling applies only to that named thing and negates it from all others. The Hanafi author refutes this by arguing that the text does not include the unmentioned cases; a proper name simply designates its referent without implying anything about others. To support this, he gives logical examples: saying 'Zaid came' does not tell us anything about 'Amr's coming. Moreover, if proper names implied negation for others, then saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would imply that others are not messengers, which is kufr. The second method is 'conditioning or description' (ta'liq bi-shart aw wasf), i.e., mafhum al-shart and mafhum al-wasf, which the Shafi'is accept. The author refutes this by arguing that specifying a condition or description does not necessarily negate the ruling in its absence. The ruling may be established through other means, such as qiyas. The text also clarifies the correct interpretation of the Ansar's inference in the hadith: they understood from the letter 'al-' (definite article) which indicates istighraq (generality), not from the proper name. The passage underscores the Hanafi position that only explicit textual methods (ibara, ishara, dalala, iqtida) are valid, and that mafhum al-mukhalafa in all its forms is invalid.

Key Terms
التنصيص باسم العلم (al-tansis bi-ism al-'ilm): Specifying a ruling by a proper name, claiming it negates the ruling for others.
مفهوم اللقب (mafhum al-laqab): Implied meaning from a proper name; that the ruling applies only to that name.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): Implied meaning from a condition; that the absence of the condition negates the ruling.
مفهوم الوصف (mafhum al-wasf): Implied meaning from a description; that the absence of the description negates the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the inference from a proper name (like in the hadith 'Water is from water') invalid?
A) Because the hadith is weak.
B) Because a proper name does not imply anything about other entities; it only refers to itself.
C) Because the Hanafi school does not accept any hadith.
D) Because the Ansar misunderstood the hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects mafhum al-mukhalafa, including inference from proper names and conditional/descriptive phrases, because proper names do not imply negation and conditions do not automatically exclude; rulings for unmentioned cases must be sought through other evidence or qiyas.

Invalid Interpretive Methods: Tansis bil-ʿAlam and Mafhum al-Shart
Refutes two invalid approaches to textual interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two invalid interpretive methods rejected by Hanafis and apply the Hanafi critique to the Shafiʿi use of mafhum al-shart and mafhum al-wasf in Quran 4:25.

Core Idea
Neither a proper name nor a condition/description in a text implies negation of the ruling for other cases.

This passage from a Hanafi usul work identifies two interpretive approaches that are considered invalid in the Hanafi school. The first claims that mentioning a specific name (like 'Zayd' or 'water') automatically negates the ruling for everything else. The second, attributed to Imam al-Shafiʿi, holds that a condition or description attached to a ruling implies its opposite when absent. Understanding these critiques is essential for mastering Hanafi textual interpretation and distinguishing it from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two invalid methods
Locate in the text the two interpretive approaches that the author declares fāsid (invalid). The first is al-tansīṣ bil-ʿalam (specification by proper name), and the second is taʿlīq al-ḥukm bi-sharṭ aw waṣf (conditioning or describing a ruling).
The first method is attributed to 'some people' (baʿḍuhum), the second to al-Shafiʿi explicitly.
2
Understand the Hanafi critique of tansīṣ bil-ʿalam
The author argues that if a text mentions a specific name (e.g., 'this water is pure'), it does not mean that other waters are impure. The text simply does not speak about other cases; it cannot be used to prove either affirmation or negation.
This rejects the notion that naming implies exclusivity.
3
Examine the Shafiʿi argument on mafhum al-shart and mafhum al-wasf
Al-Shafiʿi held that when a ruling is tied to a condition (e.g., 'if you cannot afford') or a description (e.g., 'believing'), the absence of that condition/description negates the ruling. He applied this to Quran 4:25, forbidding marriage to a slave woman if one can afford a free woman, or if the slave woman is not believing.
This is the basis for the practical difference between the schools on this issue.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that conditions delay the ruling but do not negate the cause; descriptions are not conditions but mere recommendations. The text does not encompass the counter-case, so it cannot be used to prove negation. Conditions like 'if you cannot afford' are practical restrictions, not logical implications.
The Hanafi position permits marriage to a slave woman even when one can afford a free woman, and permits marriage to a Kitābiyyah slave woman.
5
Compare the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis allow marriage to a slave woman under all circumstances except when she is a mushrikah (idolater), while Shafiʿis forbid it unless the conditions are met exactly (inability to afford free believing women, and the slave woman being believing).
This example shows how interpretive principles directly affect fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: ومن الناس من عمل في النصوص بوجوه أخر، هي فاسدة عندنا، منها ما قال بعضهم: إن التنصيص على الشيء باسمه العلم يوجب التخصيص ونفي الحكم عما عداه، وهذا فاسد؛ لأن النص لم يتناوله، فكيف يوجب الحكم فيه نفياً أو إثباتاً. ومنها ما قال الشافعي له: إن الحكم متى علّق بشرط، أو أضيف إلى مسمّى بوصف خاص أوجب نفي الحكم عند عدم الشرط أو الوصف، ولهذا لم يُجَوّز نكاحَ الأمة عند فوات الشرط، أو الوصف المذكورين في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلاً أَنْ يَنْكِحَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ فَمِنْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ مِنْ فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ﴾. وحاصله أنه ألحق الوصف بالشرط، واعتبر التعليق بالشرط عاملاً في منع الحكم دون السبب، ولذلك أبطل تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك.
Section: Among people, there are those who work with texts in other ways, which are invalid in our view. Among them is what some said: specifying a thing by its proper name necessitates particularization and negation of the ruling from what is other than it. This is invalid because the text does not encompass it, so how can it necessitate a ruling about it, negation or affirmation? And among them is what al-Shafi'i said: whenever a ruling is conditioned upon a condition, or added to a noun with a specific description, it necessitates negation of the ruling when the condition or description is absent. For this reason, he did not permit marriage to a slave woman when the condition or description mentioned in the verse is absent: {And whoever among you does not have the means to marry free believing women, then [let him marry] from what your right hands possess of believing slave-girls.} The summary is that he equated description with condition, and considered the conditioning upon the condition effective in preventing the ruling but not the cause. Therefore, he invalidated the suspension of divorce and manumission upon ownership.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage identifies two invalid interpretive approaches. The first is the claim that specifying something by its proper name (like 'Zayd' or 'water') automatically implies that the ruling does not apply to anything else. The author refutes this by noting that the text simply does not speak about other things; it cannot be made to imply either affirmation or negation about them. The second invalid approach is attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that when a ruling is tied to a condition or restricted by a description, the absence of that condition or description necessitates the absence of the ruling. For example, from the Quranic verse permitting marriage to believing slave women only if one cannot afford free believing women, and with the description 'believing', al-Shafi'i infers that when one can afford a free woman, marriage to a slave woman is forbidden, and that a Muslim man cannot marry a non-believing slave woman. The Hanafi critique is that this wrongly treats descriptions like conditions, and that conditions only delay the ruling without preventing the cause from existing. Al-Shafi'i's view leads to the invalidity of suspended divorces and manumissions before ownership—a position the Hanafis reject.

Key Terms
التنصيص بالعلم (al-tansis bil-'alam): Specifying a ruling using a proper name (e.g., 'Zayd' or 'water') to indicate that the ruling applies only to that named entity.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The implied meaning that a condition attached to a ruling indicates that the ruling does not apply when the condition is absent.
مفهوم الوصف (mafhum al-wasf): The implied meaning that a description attached to a ruling indicates that the ruling does not apply when the description is absent.
العارض (al-'arid): An incidental attribute that does not affect the essence of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is forbidden because the verse specifies 'believing slave women'.
B) It is permitted because the description 'believing' is not a condition but a recommendation.
C) It is permitted only if the woman converts to Islam.
D) It is forbidden according to all schools.

Key Takeaway
A proper name or a condition/description in a text does not imply the negation of the ruling for unmentioned cases; silence is not a source of law.

Condition and Description in Negating Rulings
Analyzing the Shafi'i position on condition and description negating rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether a description (wasf) negates a ruling for other cases, contrast it with the Shafi'i position, and apply the Hanafi principle that a condition prevents the cause from being effective in suspended contracts.

Core Idea
A description in a legal text is not necessarily a condition; the Hanafi school holds it may be incidental.

This lesson delves into a key usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a description (wasf) mentioned in a legal text operates like a condition (shart) to negate the ruling for all other cases. The debate centers on the interpretation of Qur'an 4:25 regarding marriage to slave women and extends to the validity of suspended divorces and manumissions. Understanding this disagreement clarifies why the two schools reach different rulings on the permissibility of marrying non-believing slave women and on the effect of conditional pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognize that the dispute is about whether a description (e.g., 'believing' in 'believing slave girls') in a legal text implies that the ruling applies only to that described case and not to others (mafhum al-mukhalafa).
The Shafi'i school generally adopts mafhum al-mukhalafa, while the Hanafi school restricts it.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi position is that descriptions are often incidental (ghayr mu'tabara) and do not necessarily negate the ruling for other cases. In the verse, the description 'believing' does not prohibit marriage to non-believing slave women.
The Hanafi view relies on the principle that al-wasf al-ghayr al-mu'aththir la yufid al-hasr.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Proof
The Shafi'i school uses the verse to argue that both the condition (inability to marry free women) and the description (faith) indicate that the ruling is limited to that specific scenario, thus prohibiting marriage to non-believing slave women and to slave women when able to marry free women.
They also draw an analogy with suspended divorces: the condition only delays the effect (hukm) but does not invalidate the cause (sabab).
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) the description is incidental, not conditional; (2) in suspended contracts, the condition prevents the cause from being validly formed, so no ruling can arise at all.
For example, 'If I marry you, you are divorced' is invalid because at the time of utterance, the cause (divorce pronouncement) cannot attach to a non-wife.
5
Determine the Practical Difference
The thamarah (practical consequence) is: for a suspended divorce before marriage, the Shafi'i school considers it valid but delayed until marriage, while the Hanafi school considers it entirely void.
The Hanafi ruling also permits marrying non-Muslim slave women, unlike the Shafi'i ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى
الوجوه الفاسدة
أو الوصف المذكورين في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلاً أَنْ يَنْكِحَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ فَمِنْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ مِنْ فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ﴾، وحاصله أنه ألحق الوصف بالشرط، واعتبر التعليق بالشرط عاملاً في منع الحكم دون السبب، ولذلك أبطل تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك، الشافعي. أو الوصف: وهو الإيمان في الفتيات أي الإماء. وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلاً إلخ : أي من لم يستطع منكم زيادة وقدرة في المال أن ينكح الحرائر المؤمنات لأجل زيادة مهرهن ونفقتهن فله أن ينكح أمة مؤمنة من مملوكات أيمانكم، أي إخوانكم بحذف المضاف، وإلا لايجوز نكاح السيد بأمة نفسه، فالله تعالى علّق حكم نكاح الإماء بشرط عدم طول الحرة، ثم قيّد الأمة بوصف الإيمان، فعلى مفهوم الشرط والوصف لايجوز النكاح بالأمة عند القدرة بنكاح الحرة، وأيضا لايجوز نكاح الكتابية للمؤمن، كما هو مذهب الشافعي له، وعندنا جاز الأمران. وحاصله: أي حاصل ما قاله الشافعي من أن التقيد بالشرط أو الوصف يدلّ على نفي الحكم عما عداه. ألحق الوصف إلخ في كونه موجباً للعدم عند العدم؛ لأن أثر الوصف في المنع كأثر الشرط، بدليل أن الحكم يتوقف عليه كما يتوقف على الشرط ألا ترى أن من قال لامرأته: أنت طالق راكبةً، فإن الطلاق في هذا القول متعلّق بركوبها، كما أن الطلاق في قوله : أنت طالق إن كنت راكبة متعلق بركوبها، فلما لم يوجد الطلاق في كلا القولين بغير الركوب الحق الوصف بالشرط. واعتبر التعليق إلخ واعلم أن أثر التعليق في المنع اتفاقي، لكن عند الشافعي يحلله هو يمنع الحكم عن الثبوت إلى زمان وجود الشرط، ولا يمنع السبب عن الانعقاد، فلايكون السبب موجوداً موجباً للحكم في الحال، لكن التعليق منع وجود الحكم إلى زمان وجود الشرط، فكان عدمه مضافا إلى عدم الشرط، وعندنا التعليق يمنع السبب عن الانعقاد، فلا يكون السبب موجوداً موجباً للحكم في الحال، فكان عدم الحكم بناء على العدم الأصلي، ففي قوله: "إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق" السبب هو أنت طالق ، والحكم هو وقوع الطلاق فعند الشافعي التعليق بالشرط (أعني الدخول في الدار) إنما عمل في منع الحكم فقط دون السبب؛ إذ السبب قد وجد حساً لا مردَّ له. ولذلك: أي لأجل أن التعليق إنما يؤثر في منع الحكم دون السبب. تعليق الطلاق إلخ فمن قال للأجنبية: "إن نكحتك فأنت طالق، أو قال لعبد غيره: "إن ملكتك فأنت حر"، فهذا الكلام عنده باطل، حتى لايقع الطلاق بعد النكاح ولا الإعتاق بعد الملك؛ لأن السبب وهو قوله: "أنت طالق" و"أنت حر" موجود عند التعليق، فلا بد لانعاقده من وجود الملك في المحلّ، فإذا خلا المحلّ عن الملك لغا، وصار كما إذا قال للأجنبية: "إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق"، وهذا باطل بالاتفاق.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The book of Allah Almighty. The corrupt aspects. Or the description mentioned in His statement: 'And whoever among you does not have the means to marry free believing women, then [let him marry] from what your right hands possess of believing slave girls.' The summary is that he (al-Shafi'i) equated the description with the condition, and considered that the suspension by a condition acts to prevent the ruling without affecting the cause, and therefore he invalidated the suspension of divorce and manumission upon possession. Al-Shafi'i. Or the description: it is faith in the slave girls, i.e., the maid-servants. 'And whoever among you does not have the means...' means: whoever among you does not have the extra capability and wealth to marry free believing women due to the increase in their dowries and expenses, then he may marry a believing slave girl from the captives of your hands, i.e., from your brothers, by ellipsis of the annexed; otherwise, it is not permissible for a master to marry his own slave girl. So Allah Almighty made the ruling of marrying slave girls conditional upon the lack of ability to marry a free woman, then restricted the slave girl with the description of faith. So, based on the concept of condition and description, it is not permissible to marry a slave girl when one has the ability to marry a free woman, and also it is not permissible for a believer to marry a non-Muslim woman, as is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i, but according to us both are permissible. And its summary: the summary of what al-Shafi'i said is that being restricted by a condition or description indicates the negation of the ruling for other than it. He equated the description etc. in its being a cause for non-existence when the condition is absent; because the effect of the description in prevention is like the effect of the condition, as evidence that the ruling depends on it just as it depends on the condition. Do you not see that if a man says to his wife: 'You are divorced while riding,' then divorce in this statement is attached to her riding, just as divorce in his statement: 'You are divorced if you are riding' is attached to her riding. Since divorce does not occur in both statements without riding, the description is equated with the condition. And consider the suspension etc. And know that the effect of suspension in prevention is agreed upon, but according to al-Shafi'i it prevents the ruling from being established until the time the condition exists, and does not prevent the cause from being effective, so the cause does not exist as a cause for the ruling at present, but the suspension prevents the existence of the ruling until the time the condition exists, so its non-existence is attributed to the non-existence of the condition. According to us, suspension prevents the cause from being effective, so the cause does not exist as a cause for the ruling at present, so the non-existence of the ruling is based on the original non-existence. So in his statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' the cause is 'you are divorced,' and the ruling is the occurrence of divorce. According to al-Shafi'i, the suspension by the condition (i.e., entering the house) only acts to prevent the ruling, not the cause, since the cause has occurred sensibly and cannot be avoided. Therefore: because the suspension only affects the prevention of the ruling, not the cause. So he said: 'Suspension of divorce etc.' So if someone says to a non-marriageable woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or says to another's slave: 'If I possess you, you are free,' then this statement is invalid according to him, so divorce does not occur after marriage nor manumission after possession, because the cause—which is his statement 'you are divorced' and 'you are free'—exists at the time of suspension, but for its effectiveness the existence of possession in the subject is necessary. So if the subject lacks possession, it becomes void, like when he says to a non-marriageable woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' and this is invalid by consensus.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute analysis. It presents the Shafi'i argument that a description (wasf) in a legal text operates like a condition (shart) in negating the ruling for other cases. They base this on the Quranic verse about marrying slave women, where the condition of inability to marry free women and the description of faith in the slave girl are taken to prohibit marriage to non-believing slave girls or when able to marry free women. The Shafi'i also argue that in suspended divorces, the condition only prevents the effect (hukm) but not the cause (sabab), so the cause is formed but the effect is delayed. The Hanafi counter-argument follows: the passage explains that according to the Hanafis, suspension prevents the cause itself from being effective. Therefore, a suspended divorce before possession is invalid because the cause (the pronouncement of divorce) cannot take effect without possession. The Hanafi view is that the description is not always a condition; it can be incidental. The commentary follows the author's order: first stating the Shafi'i position, then detailing their reasoning, then presenting the Hanafi response with the example of suspended divorce to show the difference.

Key Terms
وصف (wasf): A description or attribute mentioned in a legal text that qualifies a ruling.
شرط (shart): A condition that must exist for a ruling to apply.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling upon a future event.
سبب (sabab): The legal cause that generates a ruling.
حكم (hukm): The legal ruling that results from the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position discussed in this passage, what is the effect of a condition (shart) in a suspended divorce?
A) It prevents the effect (hukm) but not the cause (sabab).
B) It prevents the cause from being validly formed.
C) It has no effect on the cause or the effect.
D) It prevents the effect only temporarily.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between a description (wasf) and a condition (shart), holding that descriptions are often incidental and that a condition in a suspended contract prevents the cause from being effective, making the pronouncement void.

The Hanafi and Shafi'i Views on Carrying Mutlaq to Muqayyad
Presenting the Shafi'i and Hanafi positions on haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on carrying mutlaq to muqayyad and apply the Hanafi rule to determine when the mutlaq is not carried.

Core Idea
Mutlaq is not carried to muqayyad when rulings differ; each incident can have its own intended scope.

The dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether an absolute text should be interpreted in light of a qualified text is a foundational usul issue. It affects how we derive rulings from Quranic verses that mention expiations. In this lesson, we will examine both positions and their proofs, focusing on the example of kaffarat al-qatl and kaffarat al-yamin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Understand the question: whether a mutlaq text (e.g., freeing a slave without qualification) should be interpreted as though it carries the same qualification as a muqayyad text (e.g., freeing a believing slave) when they appear in different incidents.
2
Present the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the mutlaq is carried to the muqayyad, even across different incidents, because the qualification is like a condition and all expiations share the same genus (jins).
The Shafi'i argument relies on mafhum (implied meaning) and qiyas.
3
Present the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the mutlaq is not carried to the muqayyad when they are in different rulings, because it is possible to act on both and the Lawgiver may intend tawsi'a (leniency) in one and tadyiq (rigor) in another.
Example: kaffarat al-qatl requires a believing slave; kaffarat al-yamin does not.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in kaffarat al-yamin, Shafi'i requires a believing slave; Hanafi does not.
This difference arises from the usul principle itself.
5
Note the tarjih
The text indicates that the Hanafi position is relied upon (indana).

ومن هذه الجملة ما قال الشافعي له: إن المطلق محمول على المقيد وإن كانا في حادثتين، مثل كفارة القتل وسائر الكفارات؛ لأن قيد الإيمان زيادة وصف يجري مجرى الشرط، فيوجب نفي الحكم عند عدمه في المنصوص عليه وفي نظيره من الكفارات؛ لأنها جنس واحد. وعندنا لا يحمل المطلق على المقيد وإن كانا في حادثة واحدة بعد أن يكونا في حكمين؛ لإمكان العمل بهما. قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما: فيمن قرب التي ظاهر منها في خلال الصوم ليلاً عامداً أو نهاراً ناسياً: إنه يستأنف الصوم مع أن المطلق إلخ المطلق هو المعترض للذات دون الصفات لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات والمقيد هو المعترض للذات صفة منها، فإذا وردا في مسألة شرعية فعند الشافعي الله المطلق يحمل على المقيد، أي يراد به المقيد وإن كانا واقعين في حادثتين مثل كفارة القتل وسائر الكفارات، فإن كفارة القتل حادثة ورد فيها المقيد، وهو قوله تعالى: تحرير رقبة بدون قيد الإيمان، فعند الشافعي له قيد الإيمان معتبر هنا أيضاً. في المنصوص عليه وهو كفارة القتل، ويوجب نفي الحكم عند عدمه لأنها جنس واحد شرعت للستر والزجر. والحاصل أنه بني كلامه على أن المفهوم حجة قائلاً بأن التقييد بالوصف بمنزلة التعليق بالشرط، وأنه يوجب عدم الحكم عند عدمه كما مرّ سابقاً، فكأنه قيل في كفارة القتل فتحرير رقبة إن كانت مؤمنةً، فإذا لم يكن وصف الإيمان لايجوز بناء على أن عدم الحكم عند عدم الشرط، وإذا ثبت هذا في المنصوص وهو عدم شرعي يحمل عليه سائر الكفارات بطريق القياس لاشتراكها في كونها كفارة. وعندنا لا يحمل إلخ يعني إذا ورد المطلق والمقيد في حادثة واحدة أو حادثتين ويكون أحدهما ورد في حكم وأحدهما في حكم آخر لا يحمل المطلق على المقيد أصلا عندنا لإمكان العمل بكل واحد منهما؛ إذ يجوز يكون التوسعة هو المقصود للشارع في حكم حادثة، والتضييق هو المقصود في هذا الحكم في حادثة أخرى، كما في إعتاق الرقبة في الأخرى ورد المطلق كما قال تعالى : وتَحرير رقبة بغير قيد الإيمان، فلا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر؛ إذ يجوز أن يكون مقصود الشارع التغليظ في الأولى والتوسعة في الأخرى، وكذا لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر إذا كانا في Is the mutlaq carried to the muqayyad or not?
Among these is what al-Shafi'i said: The mutlaq is carried on the muqayyad even if they are in two incidents, like the expiation of killing and other expiations; because the qualification of faith is an additional attribute that runs in the course of a condition, so it necessitates the negation of the ruling in its absence in the specified text and in its equivalent from expiations, because they are one genus. And according to us, the mutlaq is not carried to the muqayyad even if they are in one incident as long as they are in two rulings; because it is possible to act on both. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: Concerning one who approached the woman from whom he had made zihar during the fast at night intentionally or during the day forgetfully: He must start the fast anew, even though the mutlaq etc. Al-mutlaq is that which applies to the essence without qualifications, neither by negation nor affirmation, and al-muqayyad is that which applies to the essence with a qualification. When they occur in a legal question, according to al-Shafi'i, the mutlaq is carried on the muqayyad, meaning the muqayyad is intended even if they occur in two incidents, like the expiation of killing and other expiations. For the expiation of killing is an incident in which the muqayyad occurs, which is His statement: 'Freeing a slave without the qualification of faith'. According to al-Shafi'i, the qualification of faith is considered here as well. In the specified text (mansus 'alaih) which is the expiation of killing, it necessitates the negation of the ruling in its absence because they are one genus prescribed for covering and deterrence. The summary is that he based his statement on the concept (mafhum) being an evidence, saying that qualification by an attribute is akin to conditioning by a condition, and that it necessitates the absence of the ruling when that condition is absent, as mentioned before. So it is as if it was said in the expiation of killing: 'free a believing slave', so if the attribute of faith is not present, it is not valid, based on the principle that the ruling is absent when the condition is absent. When this is established in the specified text, it is legally effective; then other expiations are carried to it by way of analogy due to their sharing being expiation. According to us, it is not carried, meaning: if the mutlaq and muqayyad occur in one incident or two incidents, and one of them occurs in one ruling and the other in another ruling, the mutlaq is not carried to the muqayyad at all according to us, because it is possible to act on each; it is possible that widening (tawsi'a) is intended by the Lawgiver in the ruling of one incident, and narrowing (tadyiq) is intended in this ruling in another incident, as in freeing a slave in the expiation of killing and the expiation of a vow: the ruling is one, which is freeing a slave, but the incidents are two: one is the expiation of killing, and the other is the expiation of a vow. In the first, the muqayyad occurs as Allah Almighty said: 'So the freeing of a believing slave' (4:92), and in the other, the mutlaq occurs as Allah Almighty said: 'And the freeing of a slave' without the qualification of faith. So one is not carried to the other, because it is possible that the Lawgiver's intention is rigor in the first and leniency in the other. Similarly, one is not carried to the other if they are in two rulings even if the incident is one, as the author explained with his example in the text: He must start the fast anew, meaning one who has made zihar with his wife by saying: 'Your back is like my mother's back...'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) presenting a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the usul principle of haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad (carrying the absolute to the qualified). The Shafi'i position, attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, holds that if a mutlaq and muqayyad text appear in the same genre (e.g., kaffarah), the mutlaq is interpreted in light of the muqayyad, even if they occur in different incidents (e.g., kaffarat al-qatl and kaffarat al-yamin). This is because the qualification (like faith in a slave) is treated as a condition, and the absence of the condition negates the ruling; since all expiations are a single genus, the ruling from one applies to others by analogy. The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) is that the mutlaq is not carried to the muqayyad when the two are in different rulings (even if the same incident) because it is possible to act on both, and the Lawgiver may have intended leniency in one case and rigor in another. The example given is freeing a slave in kaffarat al-qatl (muqayyad with faith) and kaffarat al-yamin (mutlaq without faith): Hanafis do not require the slave to be believing in the latter, because the difference in incident allows for different intentions. Another example from the matn: in zihar, if someone has intercourse during the fast expiating for zihar, he must start the fast anew; this is applied even though the mutlaq and muqayyad might be in the same incident but different rulings.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-mutlaq): A term that applies to the essence without any qualifying attribute, neither by affirmation nor negation.
المقيد (al-muqayyad): A term that applies to the essence with an additional qualifying attribute.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for certain offenses, such as killing, oath-breaking, or zihar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi view, why is kaffarat al-yamin (expiation for a broken oath) not required to involve a believing slave?
A) Because the mutlaq in that verse cannot be interpreted by analogy.
B) Because the incident of kaffarat al-yamin is different from kaffarat al-qatl, allowing for distinct rulings.
C) Because all expiations are one genus and the muqayyad must apply.
D) Because the Hanafis do not accept the concept of mafhum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not carry mutlaq to muqayyad when they appear in different rulings because the Lawgiver may intend different scopes for each incident.

Mutlaq and Muqayyad: The Hanafi Exception When the Cause Differs
Explaining when absolute and restricted texts are not conflated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when an absolute text and a restricted text both refer to the same cause (sabab), they are not carried to each other, and distinguish this from cases where carrying is obligatory.

Core Idea
When restriction is on the cause itself, absolute and restricted coexist without conflation.

This passage from a classical Hanafi usul text presents a nuanced rule: when two texts—one absolute and one restricted—both refer to the same cause triggering an obligation, they are not conflated; each stands as a separate cause. This principle has direct application to sadaqat al-fitr and other obligations, and it marks a key difference from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two texts
Locate the mutlaq text (e.g., 'pay for every slave') and the muqayyad text ('pay for every Muslim slave') from the sadaqat al-fitr hadiths.
The mutlaq text is cited from Badai' al-Sanai'; the muqayyad from Tirmidhi.
2
Determine the sabab (cause)
Recognize that the cause of obligation is the 'head' (person). Both texts refer to the same sabab, but one restricts the type of head (Muslim).
Sabab here is the person on whom zakat is due.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
Since the restriction is on the sabab itself, the two texts are not carried to each other; each establishes a separate cause. Thus, zakat is due on both Muslim and non-Muslim slaves.
Key: la mazahama fi al-asbab (no conflict in causes).
4
Contrast with cases of obligatory carrying
When the ruling and the incident are identical and the restriction is not on the cause, carrying is obligatory by consensus (e.g., the bedouin case).
Example: fasting two months vs. two consecutive months.
5
Analyze the ikhtilaf
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i would carry the absolute to the restricted even when the sabab is the same, leading to a practical difference in fiqh.
Practical difference: zakat on non-Muslim slaves obligated in Hanafi, not in Shafi'i.

كتاب الله تعالى
الوجوه الفاسدة
إنه يجب أداؤها عن العبد الكافر بالنص المطلق باسم العبد، وعن العبد المسلم بالنص المقيّد بالإسلام؛ لأنه لا مزاحمة في الأسباب، فوجب الجمع. أي لا منافاة
بالمطلق إلخ وهو قوله "أدوا عن كل حرّ وعبد صغير أو كبير نصف صاع من بر، أو صاع من تمر" [بدائع الصنائع]. ففي هذا الحديث ليس قيد المسلم. بالنص المقيّد إلخ وهو ما روى البخاري ومسلم وابن ماجة وغيرهم عن ابن عمر: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فرض زكاة الفطر من رمضان على الناس صاعاً من تمر أو صاعاً من شعير، على كل حر وعبد ذكر وأنثى من المسلمين. وقال أبو عيسى الترمذي: رواه مالك عن نافع و زاد فيه "من المسلمين"، ورواه غير واحد عن نافع ولم يذكروا فيه "من المسلمين". [الترمذي، رقم: ٦٧٦]. والحاصل أن المطلق والمقيد إذا دخلا على السبب (نحو): "أدوا عن كل حرّ وعبد" هذا مطلق، و"عن كل حرّ وعبد من المسلمين"، وهذا مقيّد، فإن هذين النصين دخلا على السبب وهو الرأس، فإن سبب وجوب صدقة الفطر هو الرأس، فأحدهما يدلّ على أن الرأس المطلق سبب، والثاني على أن رأس المسلم سبب لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر. فوجب الجمع بين المطلق والمقيد، ولا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر، فيجعل كل واحد منهما سبباً، وأما لو كان الحكم واحداً مع اتحاد الحادثة فالحمل واجب بالاتفاق، كما أمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لأعرابي جامع امرأته في نهار رمضان أن يصوم شهرين متتابعين. رواه أبو داود رقم: ۲۳۹۰] وغيره. وفي رواية له عن ابن جريج عن الزهري على لفظ مالك: أن يصوم شهرين بلا قيد التتابع والحكم والحادثة واحد، فوجب حمل أحدهما على الآخر؛ لأن الحكم وهو الصوم لا يقبل وصفين متضادّين، فإذا ثبت تقييده بطل إطلاقه وضبط المقام أن المطلق والمقيد إذا وردا لبيان الحكم فإما أن يختلف الحكم أو يتحد، فإن اختلف فإن لم يكن أحد الحكمين موجباً لتقييد الآخر لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر، مثل: أطعم رجلاً، واكس رجلا عارياً، وإلا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر، مثل: أعتق عني رقبة، ولاتعتق رقبة كافرةً، فإن أحدهما موجب لتقييد الآخر بالذات، ومثل: أعتق عني رقبة ولا تملكني رقبة كافرة، فإن أحدهما يوجب تقيد الأخر بالواسطة؛ لأن نفي تمليك الكافرة يستلزم نفي إعتاقها، وهذا يوجب تقيد إيجاب الإعتاق عنه بالمؤمنة.
·
وإن أتحد الحكم فإما أن يكون منفياً أو مثبتاً، فإن كان الأول فلا حمل، مثل: لا تعتق رقبة ولا تعتق رقبة كافرة، لإمكان الجمع بأن لا يعتق أصلاً، وإن كان الثاني فالحادثة: إما مختلفة أو متحدة، فإن اختلفت فلا حمل خلافاً للشافعي محله ككفارة اليمين والقتل، وإن اتحدت فإما أن يكون الإطلاق والتقييد في السبب ونحوه أو لا، فإن كان فلا حمل، كوجوب نصف الصاع في صدقة الفطر بسبب الرأس مطلقاً في بعض الأحاديث، ومقيداً بالإسلام في بعضها، وإلا يحمل المطلق على المقيد بالاتفاق، كما في قصة أعرابي جاء: "صُمْ شهرين" في رواية، و"شهرين متتابعين" في الأخرى، كذا قيل.

[PAGE_065 متصلة]
من
الوجوه الفاسدة
وهو نظير ما سبق أن التعليق بالشرط لا يوجب النفي عند عدمه فصار الحكم نفى الحكم أي عدم الشرط الواحد قبل وجوده معلقا ومرسلا؛ لأن الإرسال والتعليق يتنافيان وجودًا، فأما قبل وجوده فهو معلّق بالشرط أي معدوم يتعلّق وجوده بالشرط، ومرسل عن الشرط أي معدوم محتمل للوجود قبله والعدم الأصلي كان محتملا للوجود ولم يتبدل قبل التعليق العدم، فصار محتملاً للوجود بطريقين. أي بقى عند وجوده: أي العمل بالمطلق والمقيد الواردين في السبب وعدم حمل أحدهما على الآخر. فصار الحكم إلخ: محتملا لأن يكون معلقا بالشرط ومرسلا: أي مطلقاً عن الشرط، وذلك لأن الإرسال إلخ. وجوداً: يعني الحكم، وثبوته لايمكن أن يثبت ذلك الحكم الواحد بالإرسال والتعليق لأجل المنافاة بينهما. قبل وجوده: أي قبل وجود ذلك الحكم. قبله أي قبل الشرط، كالطلقات الثلاث المعلّقة بدخول الدار يحتمل أن يتحقق وجودها عند وجود الشرط أي دخول الدار، ويحتمل أن يوجد قبل وجود الشرط بالتنجيز محتملا للوجود بالإرسال والتعليق، وبعد التعليق أيضاً. يوجد بطريقين: أحدهما الإرسال والتعليق بالشرط، فكما يصلح الحكم الواحد قبل وجوده أن يوجد بالشرط وأن يوجد بغيره أيضاً؛ لأنه لا منافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق قبل وجود الحكم؛ لأن الأمر العدمي يصلح أن بالشرط، وأن يوجد بغيره أيضاً، ولا يتبدّل العدم بعد التعليق، فهو بعد التعليق أيضاً يصلح أن يوجد بطريقين، وإنما المنافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق بعد وجود الحكم، فإنه إذا وجد الحكم بالشرط لا يمكن أن يقال: إنه وجد بالإرسال أي بغير الشرط، وكذا العكس، كذلك يصلح الحكم الواحد قبل وجوده أن يثبت بسبب مقيد، وأن يثبت بسبب مطلق على سبيل البدل؛ إذ لا منافاة بينهما حينئذ، كما لم يكن المنافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق قبل وجود الحكم وإن امتنع ثبوته بهما جميعاً، فلذا لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر بل يجمع بينهما. ثم في هذا الكلام دفع سوال كان يرد على مسألة التعليق بالشرط تقرير السوال أن عندكم التعليق بالشرط لا يوجب نفي الحكم عند عدمه بل يجوز أن يوجد الحكم عند عدم الشرط أيضاً، كما يجوز أن يوجد عند الشرط، فصار الشيء الواحد معلقا ومرسلاً. وهذا اجتماع المتنافيين؟ تقرير الجواب: أنه لا منافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق قبل وجود الشيء، وإنما المنافاة بعد وجوده، ونحن نجعل معلقا ومرسلاً قبل وجوده، لا بعده، فتأمل.
* وذلك: أي احتمال الحكم لأن يكون معلقا ومرسلا.
It is obligatory to pay (zakat al-fitr) for the unbelieving slave by the absolute text mentioning 'slave', and for the Muslim slave by the restricted text with 'Muslim', because there is no conflict in the causes, so combining is necessary. By the absolute text, meaning His saying: 'Pay for every free and slave, young or old, half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of dates' [Badai' al-Sanai']. In this hadith there is no restriction of Muslim. By the restricted text, meaning what Bukhari, Muslim, Ibn Majah and others narrated from Ibn Umar: that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) enjoined zakat al-fitr of Ramadan upon people: a sa' of dates or a sa' of barley, upon every free and slave, male and female among the Muslims. Abu Isa al-Tirmidhi said: Malik narrated from Nafi' and added 'among the Muslims', and others narrated from Nafi' without mentioning 'among the Muslims'. [Tirmidhi, no. 676]. The conclusion is that when the absolute and restricted come upon the cause (sabab), such as: 'Pay for every free and slave' — this is absolute, and 'for every free and slave among the Muslims' — this is restricted; these two texts come upon the cause which is the head (ra's), for the cause of obligation of sadaqat al-fitr is the head. One indicates that the absolute head is a cause, and the second that the Muslim head is a cause; one is not carried to the other. So combining between absolute and restricted is necessary, and one is not carried to the other; each is made a cause. But if the ruling is the same with a single incident, then carrying is obligatory by consensus, as when the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded the bedouin who had intercourse with his wife during Ramadan to fast two consecutive months. [Abu Dawud, no. 2390] In another narration from Ibn Jurayj from al-Zuhri in the wording of Malik: that he fast two months without the restriction of consecutive, but the ruling and incident are one, so carrying one to the other is obligatory, because the ruling, which is fasting, cannot accept two contradictory descriptions. So when its restriction is established, its absoluteness is nullified. The regulation is that when absolute and restricted are given to explain the ruling, either the ruling differs or is the same. If it differs, then unless one ruling necessitates restricting the other, one is not carried to the other, like: 'Feed a man' and 'Clothe a naked man'; otherwise it is carried, like: 'Free a slave for me' and 'Do not free an unbelieving slave', for one necessitates restricting the other intrinsically, and like: 'Free a slave for me' and 'Do not make me possess an unbelieving slave', for one necessitates restricting the other mediately, because negating possession of an unbelieving slave entails negating freeing her, and this necessitates restricting the obligation of freeing to a believing (slave). If the ruling is the same, then either it is negated or affirmed. If it is the former, then no carrying, like: 'Do not free a slave' and 'Do not free an unbelieving slave', because it is possible to combine by not freeing at all. If it is the latter, then the incident is either different or the same. If different, then no carrying, contrary to al-Shafi'i, as in expiation for oath and homicide. If the same, then either the absoluteness and restriction are in the cause or not; if they are, then no carrying, like the obligation of half a sa' in sadaqat al-fitr due to the head absolutely in some hadiths and restricted to Islam in others; otherwise, the absolute is carried to the restricted by consensus, as in the story of the bedouin: 'Fast two months' in one narration, and 'two consecutive months' in another. Thus it is said.

This is analogous to the previous point: that suspension (ta'liq) by a condition does not necessitate negation when it is absent, so the ruling becomes ... before its existence suspended and released; because release and suspension are contradictory in existence. Before its existence, it is suspended by the condition, i.e., non-existent whose existence depends on the condition, and released from the condition, i.e., non-existent possible of existence before it, and the original non-existence was possible of existence and did not change before the suspension of the original non-existence; so it becomes possible of existence in two ways. ... i.e., acting upon the absolute and restricted that come upon the cause and not carrying one to the other. So the ruling becomes ... possible to be suspended by the condition and released: i.e., absolute from the condition, because release etc. existence: meaning the ruling, and its establishment cannot be established by release and suspension due to the conflict between them. Before its existence: i.e., before the existence of that ruling. Before it, i.e., before the condition, like three divorces suspended on entering the house: it is possible that their occurrence occurs at the occurrence of the condition, i.e., entering the house, and possible that it occurs before the condition by immediate (pronouncement), being possible of existence by release and suspension, and also after suspension. It exists in two ways: one is release and suspension by the condition. Just as a single ruling before its existence can exist by the condition and also by other than it; because there is no conflict between release and suspension before the existence of the ruling; because a non-existent thing can be by the condition and also by other than it, and the non-existence does not change after suspension; so after suspension it also can exist in two ways. Conflict between release and suspension is only after the existence of the ruling; for if the ruling occurs by the condition, it cannot be said that it occurred by release, i.e., without the condition, and vice versa. Similarly, a single ruling before its existence can be established by a restricted cause and by an absolute cause alternatively, since there is no conflict between them at that time, just as there was no conflict between release and suspension before the existence of the ruling, even though its establishment by both together is impossible. Hence, one is not carried to the other, but rather they are combined. Then in this speech there is a rebuttal of an objection that could be raised on the issue of suspension by a condition: the formulation of the objection is that you hold that suspension by a condition does not necessitate negation of the ruling when it is absent; rather, the ruling may exist even when the condition is absent, just as it may exist when the condition is present, so the same thing becomes suspended and released. Is this not a combination of contradictories? The formulation of the answer: that there is no conflict between release and suspension before the existence of the thing; conflict is only after its existence, and we make it suspended and released before its existence, not after it. Reflect.
* That is: i.e., the possibility of the ruling being suspended and released.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rule of handling absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad) texts when they both relate to the same cause (sabab). The Hanafi position is that when two texts — one absolute, one restricted — are both attached to the same cause (like the 'head' for sadaqat al-fitr), they are not conflated; instead, each is treated as establishing a separate cause. The example from sadaqat al-fitr hadiths shows that one hadith says 'pay for every slave' (absolute) and another says 'pay for every Muslim slave' (restricted). Since the cause (the head) is the same, both remain operative but for different categories. This is contrasted with cases where the ruling (hukm) and the incident (haditha) are identical, where carrying the absolute to the restricted is obligatory by consensus. The passage then systematically categorizes scenarios: (1) when the rulings differ, (2) when they are the same but negated, (3) when affirmed but with different incidents, and (4) when the incident is the same but the restriction is on the cause or not. The key nuance is that if the restriction is in the cause itself, no carrying occurs. This is a subtle point where the Hanafis differ from al-Shafi'i in one subcase. The passage ends with an analogy to ta'liq (suspension) and irsal (release) to illustrate that the potential for a ruling to arise from either an absolute or restricted cause is not contradictory before the ruling exists. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh (sadaqat al-fitr, expiations, oaths).

Key Terms
المطلق (al-mutlaq): A text that is unrestricted, without any condition or qualification
المقيد (al-muqayyad): A text that has a restriction or condition attached to its ruling or subject
السبب (al-sabab): The cause or reason that triggers a legal obligation, e.g., head for zakat al-fitr
الحمل (al-haml): The act of interpreting the absolute text as limited by the restricted text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle explained in this passage, when an absolute text and a restricted text both refer to the same cause (sabab), what is the correct approach?
A) The absolute is always carried to the restricted text.
B) Both texts are combined without one overriding the other.
C) The restricted text is considered abrogated by the absolute.
D) The later text abrogates the earlier one.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a restriction is attached to the cause (sabab) itself, the absolute and restricted texts operate independently without conflation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Combining Ta'liq and Irsal Before Existence
Explains that ta'liq and irsal can coexist before the ruling's existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a ruling can be both mu'allaq and mursal before its existence and apply this principle to resolve apparent contradictions in legal texts.

Core Idea
A ruling can be both suspended and absolute before it exists — contradiction only arises after existence.

This lesson explores a subtle but crucial usul principle: how a single ruling can be described as both suspended on a condition (mu'allaq) and absolute (mursal) before it actually exists. At first glance, these two descriptions seem contradictory. However, the Hanafi school provides a rational argument showing that the contradiction only arises after the ruling comes into existence. Before that, the ruling is non-existent and can bear both possibilities. This principle helps jurists reconcile seemingly conflicting legal texts and avoid prematurely negating one description in favor of the other.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
First, ensure you understand the definitions of ta'liq (suspension on a condition) and irsal (absoluteness). Ta'liq means a ruling is tied to a condition; irsal means it is not tied. Recognize that these are opposites when the ruling exists.
2
Understand the nature of non-existence
The concept of 'al-'adam al-asli' (original non-existence) is key. A non-existent thing can potentially exist in multiple ways. Before a ruling exists, it is pure potential, capable of being actualized either by the condition or without it.
This is a specifically Hanafi concept.
3
Identify when contradiction arises
The author argues that contradiction between ta'liq and irsal only occurs after the ruling exists. For example, if a divorce is suspended on entry, after entry occurs, the divorce is definitely by the condition, not by immediate pronouncement. But before entry, it could be either.
4
Apply to the divorce example
Consider three divorces suspended on entering a house. Before the wife enters, the divorces are non-existent. They can potentially occur either when she enters (by the condition) or before entry (if the husband pronounces them immediately). Thus, the same potential ruling carries both descriptions.
The example is a rational proof (qiyas) for the principle.
5
Respond to the objection
An objector may say that a single thing cannot be both mu'allaq and mursal. The Hanafi response: this is true only after existence. Before existence, there is no contradiction. Hence, we may combine both descriptions without conflict.
This step involves critical analysis of the counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو نظير ما سبق أن التعليق بالشرط لا يوجب النفي عند عدمه فصار الحكم نفى الحكم أي عدم الشرط الواحد قبل وجوده معلقا ومرسلا؛ لأن الإرسال والتعليق يتنافيان وجودًا، فأما قبل وجوده فهو معلّق بالشرط أي معدوم يتعلّق وجوده بالشرط، ومرسل عن الشرط أي معدوم محتمل للوجود قبله والعدم الأصلي كان محتملا للوجود ولم يتبدل قبل التعليق العدم الأصلي أي بقى فصار محتملاً للوجود بطريقين. عند وجود الشرط أي مطلقاً عن الشرط، وذلك لأن الإرسال والتعليق وجوداً: يعني الحكم، وثبوته لايمكن أن يثبت ذلك الحكم الواحد بالإرسال والتعليق لأجل المنافاة بينهما. قبله أي قبل الشرط، كالطلقات الثلاث المعلّقة بدخول الدار يحتمل أن يتحقق وجودها عند وجود الشرط أي دخول الدار، ويحتمل أن يوجد قبل وجود الشرط بالتنجيز محتملا للوجود بالإرسال والتعليق، وبعد التعليق أيضاً يوجد بطريقين: أحدهما الإرسال والتعليق بالشرط، فكما يصلح الحكم الواحد قبل وجوده أن يوجد بالشرط وأن يوجد بغيره أيضاً؛ لأنه لا منافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق قبل وجود الحكم؛ لأن الأمر العدمي يصلح أن بالشرط، وأن يوجد بغيره أيضاً، ولا يتبدّل العدم بعد التعليق، فهو بعد التعليق أيضاً يصلح أن يوجد بطريقين، وإنما المنافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق بعد وجود الحكم، فإنه إذا وجد الحكم بالشرط لا يمكن أن يقال: إنه وجد بالإرسال أي بغير الشرط، وكذا العكس، كذلك يصلح الحكم الواحد قبل وجوده أن يثبت بسبب مقيد، وأن يثبت بسبب مطلق على سبيل البدل؛ إذ لا منافاة بينهما حينئذ، كما لم يكن المنافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق قبل وجود الحكم وإن امتنع ثبوته بهما جميعاً، فلذا لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر بل يجمع بينهما. ثم في هذا الكلام دفع سوال كان يرد على مسألة التعليق بالشرط تقرير السوال أن عندكم التعليق بالشرط لا يوجب نفي الحكم عند عدمه بل يجوز أن يوجد الحكم عند عدم الشرط أيضاً، كما يجوز أن يوجد عند الشرط، فصار الشيء الواحد معلقا ومرسلاً. وهذا اجتماع المتنافيين؟ تقرير الجواب: أنه لا منافاة بين الإرسال والتعليق قبل وجود الشيء، وإنما المنافاة بعد وجوده، ونحن نجعل معلقا ومرسلاً قبل وجوده، لا بعده، فتأمل. * وذلك: أي احتمال الحكم لأن يكون معلقاً ومرسلا.
And it is analogous to what preceded: that ta'liq with a condition does not necessitate negation in its absence, so the ruling becomes [such that] the single ruling before its existence is both mu'allaq and mursal; because irsal and ta'liq are mutually exclusive in existence. As for before its existence, it is mu'allaq with the condition, i.e., a non-existent whose existence is related to the condition, and mursal from the condition, i.e., a non-existent possible of existence before it, and the original non-existence was possible of existence and did not change before the condition, the original non-existence remained, so it becomes possible of existence in two ways. At the existence of the condition, i.e., absolutely from the condition, because irsal and ta'liq [in] existence: meaning the ruling, and its establishment cannot be that the single ruling is established by irsal and ta'liq due to the contradiction between them. Before it: i.e., before the condition, like three divorces suspended on entering the house: it is possible that their existence occurs at the existence of the condition, i.e., entering the house, and it is possible that it occurs before the existence of the condition by immediate execution, being possible of existence by irsal and ta'liq, and after ta'liq also it can occur in two ways: one of them is irsal and ta'liq by the condition. For just as the single ruling before its existence is suitable to exist by the condition and also by other than it; because there is no contradiction between irsal and ta'liq before the existence of the ruling; because the non-existent matter is suitable to be by the condition, and also to exist by other than it, and the non-existence does not change after ta'liq, so after ta'liq also it is suitable to exist in two ways. The contradiction between irsal and ta'liq is only after the existence of the ruling: because if the ruling exists by the condition, it cannot be said that it existed by irsal, i.e., without the condition, and similarly the opposite. In the same way, the single ruling before its existence is suitable to be established by a restricted cause and by an absolute cause alternatively; since there is no contradiction between them then, just as there was no contradiction between irsal and ta'liq before the existence of the ruling, even though it is impossible for it to be established by both together. Therefore, one is not carried upon the other, but rather both are combined. Then in this speech is a refutation of a question that was raised regarding the issue of ta'liq with a condition. The formulation of the question: according to you, ta'liq with a condition does not necessitate negation of the ruling in its absence, but rather the ruling may occur when the condition is absent as well as when it is present, so the single thing becomes mu'allaq and mursal. Is this a combination of contradictories? The formulation of the answer: There is no contradiction between irsal and ta'liq before the existence of the thing; the contradiction is only after its existence. And we make it mu'allaq and mursal before its existence, not after it. So ponder. * That is: meaning the possibility of the ruling being mu'allaq and mursal.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule explaining how a ruling can be both suspended (mu'allaq) on a condition and absolute (mursal) simultaneously before its actual existence. The author clarifies that there is no contradiction between these two descriptions before the ruling comes into being. He uses the example of three divorces suspended on entering a house: before the condition is met, the divorces are non-existent but can potentially occur either through the condition or through immediate pronouncement. The argument responds to a potential objection that a single thing cannot be both mu'allaq and mursal, as they are opposites. The response is that the opposition only exists after the ruling is realized; before that, both possibilities coexist. This principle is used in Hanafi usul to avoid interpreting one of two seemingly conflicting texts as overruling the other; instead, both are given effect where possible. The discussion is firmly within Hanafi methodology, emphasizing rational argument (dalil aqli) and analogical reasoning (qiyas) from the example of divorced. The authority weight is not explicitly stated but this is a standard Hanafi usul principle found in texts of the uṣūl tradition.

Key Terms
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta'liq bi al-shart): The suspension of a ruling upon the occurrence of a condition, such that the ruling only takes effect when the condition is met.
الإرسال (al-irsal): The absoluteness of a ruling, i.e., not being tied to any condition; it can take effect immediately.
العدم الأصلي (al-'adam al-asli): Original non-existence, the state before any ruling is issued, which is capable of being actualized in multiple ways.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why can a single ruling be both mu'allaq and mursal before it exists?
A) Because they are not mutually exclusive
B) Because the contradiction only occurs after the ruling's existence
C) Because the ruling is non-existent and thus meaningless
D) Because ta'liq overrides irsal

Key Takeaway
The opposition between ta'liq and irsal is temporal: they can coexist before the ruling's existence, resolving apparent contradictions in conditional texts.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
General Not Specified by Its Cause
Explains Hanafi principle and its exceptions, with dispute against Malik/Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a general ('amm) is not specified by its specific cause (sabab) and identify the exceptions where specification occurs.

Core Idea
The general text applies to all, not just its cause—unless the general itself depends on the cause for meaning.

One of the key disputes in usul al-fiqh is whether a general Quranic or Prophetic statement, revealed concerning a specific event or person, is restricted to that context. The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of the Companions, holds that the generality remains intact. In this lesson, we examine the evidence and the exceptions where the general is indeed specified by its cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Understand the dispute: Does the specific cause of revelation (sabab) automatically restrict a general statement (amm) to that particular incident?
The issue is commonly referred to as 'takhsis al-amm bi sababihi'.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the general is not specified by its cause unless the general wording is not independent (such as the particles 'na'am' and 'bala' or a conditional result).
Independence means the word conveys meaning without reference to the cause.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis rely on the consensus (ijma) of the Companions and Followers, who applied general verses (e.g., zihar, li'an, qadhf, theft) to all cases despite their specific causes.
This is a naqli (textual) proof based on observed practice.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
Malik, al-Shafi'i, and others argue that the general is specified by its cause unless another text or qiyas extends it.
Their dalil is that the cause provides context and limits the ruling.
5
Analyze the Hanafi exceptions
The Hanafis acknowledge specification when the general is not independent: (1) particles like 'na'am' and 'bala' that rely on the cause for meaning, and (2) speech that is the result (jaza') of a condition, e.g., the Prophet's prostration after forgetfulness.
For independent speech, the general remains general.

Source Text

That the general is specified by its cause. And among them is what some said: that the general is specified by its cause. And according to us, it is only specified by its cause if the general is not independent in itself, like his saying: 'yes' or 'indeed', or it comes out as a result (jaza'), like the narrator's saying: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forgot in the mosque', or it comes out as a reply, and did not add to it. Some of them, and they are Malik and al-Shafi'i, adopted it and were followed by al-Muzani, al-Qaffal, Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq, and Abu Thawr. [They said:] the general: if it occurs regarding a specific person in a text or saying of the Companions, and it was not an independent speech. The Prophet (peace be upon him) [addressed] the right of that questioner or the person of the incident, even if by its cause whether the cause was a question from a questioner by someone asking in his presence and he answered: 'Whoever does such and such, upon him is such and such,' or 'for him is such and such,' and he did not say: 'for you such and such' or 'upon you such and such,' or the occurrence of an incident that happened to someone during his time, and the text came regarding that incident, it includes the person of the incident and others. According to these scholars, that general is specified, so the ruling is established regarding others by another text or by analogy. The majority of scholars from the early and later generations held the opposite view. They are supported by the consensus of the Companions and the Followers in applying the texts that came restricted by their causes to their generality. Do you not see that the verse of zihar was revealed regarding Khawla bint Tha'laba? the wife of Aws ibn al-Samit, and the verse of li'an regarding Hilal ibn Umayyah, and the verse of qadhf regarding the accusation of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), and the verse of theft regarding the theft of the cloak of Safwan or the theft of the Majnun? Yet despite that, they did not specify these generalities to their specific instances. So it is known that the general is not specified by the cause of revelation, similarly in 'Ghayat al-Tahqiq'. Then the sheikh wanted to explain what is correct according to us and to detail the cause, and he explained it in four divisions. 'Independent in itself' means it does not independently convey meaning without what preceded it of the cause, and this is the first division. Like his saying in reply to someone who said: 'Did I have a thousand dirhams owed to me by you?' and he said: 'yes,' or said: 'Do I not have a thousand dirhams owed to me by you?' and he said: 'indeed.' So his saying 'indeed' and 'yes' are general due to their ambiguity in that they are suitable as answers to various types of speech, so when the cause is mentioned, they become attached to it; because each of them is a particle not independent in itself, it does not benefit without attachment to what preceded it of the cause. So if it does not benefit without attachment to what preceded it of the cause, how can it benefit a general meaning covering all? Because that is the function of the independent. Rather, it is confined to the cause. As for if it were an independent speech itself, like his saying: 'You have a thousand dirhams owed to me,' it is an initiation outside of what we are discussing, so there is no dispute about it. And the difference between 'bala' and 'na'am' is known in the lengthy works. Like the narrator's saying: his story with the statement of Dhu al-Yadayn: 'Was the prayer shortened or did you forget?' the hadith is narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari no. 714 and Muslim no. 129. And this is the second division. So his saying: 'he prostrated' is general, suitable in itself for every prostration, but since it occurred in the position of a result (jaza') indicated by the letter fa, it was specified by its cause, which is forgetfulness. That is because when the speech is made a result of what preceded, the preceded becomes the cause of its obligation, so it becomes attached to it and does not convey a general meaning.

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul dispute on whether a general ('amm) statement is restricted (takhsis) by its specific cause (sabab) of revelation. The author states the Hanafi position: the general is not specified by its cause unless the general is not independent in meaning. He contrasts with Malik, al-Shafi'i, and others who hold that the general is specified by its cause. The Hanafi evidence is the consensus (ijma) of the Companions and Followers, who applied general verses (zihar, li'an, qadhf, theft) despite their specific causes, not restricting them. The author then explains the Hanafi view in four divisions, giving examples of non-independent speech (like 'na'am' and 'bala') and speech that functions as the result of a condition (like the Prophet's prostration after forgetfulness). For these, the generality is restricted to the cause. The passage is a clear exposition of Hanafi methodology, relying on textual analysis (Quranic examples) and consensus. The authority weight is not explicitly stated but the reliance on 'ijma' and common examples from the Quran suggests it is from the mainstream usul tradition.

Key Terms
العام (al-'amm): A general word that encompasses all individuals of its category, e.g., 'al-sariq' (the thief).
السبب (al-sabab): The specific cause or occasion for a ruling's revelation or utterance.
تخصيص (takhsis): The restriction of a general term to some of its individuals.

Key Takeaway
A general text is not restricted by its cause of revelation; it retains its generality unless its wording is inherently dependent on that cause.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Core Dispute: Does the Cause of Revelation Restrict the General?
Explaining the core dispute and the Hanafi proof of ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on takhsis al-amm bi sababihi, state the opposing view, and apply the Hanafi proof of ijma al-sahabah to uphold the generality of the verses of zihar, li'an, qadhf, and theft.

Core Idea
A general statement revealed for a specific cause remains general unless it lacks linguistic independence.

This lesson introduces a fundamental dispute in Hanafi usul: does the specific cause of revelation (sabab) restrict the general ruling ('amm) to that context? While some scholars, including Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i, argue for restriction, the Hanafi school maintains that the general remains general, citing the consensus of the Companions who applied verses like zihar and theft universally despite their specific causes. We will examine the proofs and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Explain what takhsis al-amm bi sababihi means: when a general text comes in response to a specific question or incident, does it apply only to that context?
2
Identify the Two Positions
Summarize the Hanafi position (general remains general unless the statement is not independent) vs. the opposing view (Malik/Shafi'i: general is restricted by its cause unless extended by another text or analogy).
The opposing school includes Malik, al-Shafi'i, al-Muzani, al-Qaffal, Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq, Abu Thawr, and Zufar (in some cases).
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proof: Ijma al-Sahabah
Review the examples given: the verses of zihar (revealed regarding Khawla), li'an (Hilal ibn Umayya), qadhf (slander of Aisha), and theft (cloak of Safwan or shield). Despite specific causes, the Companions did not restrict these general rulings; they applied them universally.
This consensus (ijma) of the Companions is the primary Hanafi proof.
4
Evaluate the Opposing View's Basis
Understand why the opposing school argues for restriction (context of revelation, custom of the Prophet to address specific situations). Then see the Hanafi response: the Companions' practice shows they did not restrict, and if the statement is linguistically independent, it cannot be restricted.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in Hanafi fiqh, the verses of zihar, etc., apply to all cases, not just the original incident.

فقوله: "بلى" و"نعم" عام لإبهامه من حيث أنه يصلح جواباً لأنواع من الكلام، فعند ذكر السبب يتعلّق به؛ لأن كل واحد منهما حرف غير مستقل بنفسه، لا يفيد بدون تعلقه بما قبله من السبب، فإذا لم يفد بدون
